Su Qingyuan opened the bag. Inside were two golden, crisp croissants, still warm. She picked one up and took a bite; the flaky pastry crumbled between her teeth, and the scent of butter filled her mouth.
"It's good," she said, mouth full.
Lu Shiyan watched her eat, the corners of his lips lifting.
"How were things in Shenzhen?"
"Great. Zhenyi's Director Chen is very professional—we can bring the 'First Light' cost down eight points without dropping quality." Su Qingyuan took another bite of croissant. "Oh, right—Director Chen said that back when you negotiated the OEM agreement with them, you asked for eight points too. You two have worked together before?"
"Mm. Lu Group has a high-end watch brand, and Zhenyi is one of its suppliers." Lu Shiyan started the car. "Director Chen is a hard man to negotiate with. It took me three months to bring the price down."
Su Qingyuan tilted her head toward him. "Well, I got it down in a single day—so does that make me better than you?"
Lu Shiyan glanced at her, a smile in his eyes. "Mm. Better than me."
That "mm" left Su Qingyuan feeling a little guilty. She knew a large part of why Director Chen was willing to deal with her came down to Lu Shiyan's standing. But he didn't say, "That's because of me." He said, "Mm, better than me." This man handed every bit of the credit to her and stood between her and every storm.
She lowered her head and went on eating the croissant, the tips of her ears turning faintly red.
The car pulled off the airport expressway and merged into Nancheng's traffic. The rain came down harder, the wipers sweeping fast across the windshield. Su Qingyuan finished her croissant, settled back into her seat, and as she watched the blurred streetscape outside, something suddenly occurred to her.
"Has Su Yurou made any moves lately?"
Lu Shiyan tapped his fingers lightly twice on the steering wheel. "Yes. She's moved to a new address—an ordinary apartment complex on the outskirts of Nancheng. She hasn't looked for a new job, and she hasn't contacted anyone from her old circles. Lawyer Fang's side has terminated their retainer too."
Su Qingyuan frowned. "Terminated? Gu Changfeng isn't helping her anymore?"
"He is. But the method has changed. After Lawyer Fang dropped her, Su Yurou didn't hire a new lawyer—she's handling everything herself. That tells me Gu Changfeng is having her 'disappear'—no public appearances, no traceable handle left behind."
"Then how is she living?"
"Someone's wiring her money. The account is well hidden, but Lu Group's risk-control department found a trace of it—the money comes from an offshore account, and the ultimate source points to a subsidiary of Dingfeng Group."
Su Qingyuan fell silent. Gu Changfeng hadn't abandoned Su Yurou; he'd moved her from out in the open into the shadows. A piece hidden in the dark was harder to deal with than an enemy in plain sight.
"Lu Shiyan, what do you think Su Yurou will do next?"
"I don't know." Lu Shiyan's voice was low. "But she won't do nothing. Su Yurou is the kind of person whose hatred outlasts her love. She can wait a long time."
Su Qingyuan nodded and asked nothing more. She watched the rain outside and suddenly said, "If she comes for me, I'll face her."
Lu Shiyan said nothing, only reached out and took her hand.
The wipers kept sweeping, one stroke, then another, clearing the rain from the windshield again and again, only to have it covered again and again by fresh rain.
Just like their life—trouble kept appearing, and again and again the two of them joined hands and dealt with it.
On the third day after returning to Nancheng, Su Qingyuan received an unexpected phone call.
It was Cartier headquarters' creative director, calling in person—a Frenchman with a low, gentle voice. He said the three sample pieces of the "Light Traces" collection had completed all their testing in Paris, and the results exceeded expectations. He asked whether Su Qingyuan would be willing to come to Paris next spring for the global launch event of the "Light Traces" collection.
Su Qingyuan gripped the phone, her heart racing, but her voice steady. "I would. Thank you for the invitation, Director."
"Just call me Jean-Pierre." The man let out a soft laugh. "Miss Su, I've designed jewelry for thirty years and met countless designers. You're the first one to make me feel that 'this industry still has hope.'"
Su Qingyuan was taken aback, then said earnestly, "Jean-Pierre, those words make me happier than winning a gold medal."
"Why?"
"Because a gold medal is for the past. Those words are for the future."
There was a moment's silence on the other end, and then Jean-Pierre's laughter grew more pronounced. "Miss Su, I'm beginning to understand why Cartier wanted to work with you. It's not because of how talented you are. It's because you have a soul."
After hanging up, Su Qingyuan sat at her worktable and didn't move for a long while.
Jean-Pierre had said, "You have a soul." Those few words weighed more than any award. Because an award can come down to skill, to luck, to timing—but a soul can't be faked. It's the mark left on your work by what you've lived through, what you believe, and the person you've become.
She looked down at her own hands. These hands had drawn thousands of sketches, ground hundreds of stones, been cut by carving knives more times than she could count. Every scar was a story. And those stories were her soul.
Su Qingyuan opened her sketchbook and began drawing new drafts. Inspiration came fast, like water bursting through a dam; the pen tip raced across the paper, lines moving from chaos to clarity, from blur to certainty.
What she was drawing was a series—not "First Light," not "Light Traces," but an entirely new one with no name yet. The theme was "Roots."
Something suddenly came clear to her. She used to fight desperately to escape the old Su family house, to flee the place that had trapped her, believing that escape was freedom. But now she knew: true freedom wasn't escape. It was carrying everything from the past—the good and the bad, the sweet and the bitter—and walking forward, neither trapped by it nor pretending it didn't exist.
The "Roots" series was about exactly that.
She drew deep into the night, forgetting to eat, forgetting to drink, forgetting to message Lu Shiyan. Until the studio door was pushed open and Lu Shiyan walked in carrying an insulated bag. Seeing her hands covered in pencil dust yet her eyes shining like stars, he let out a sigh.
"You didn't check your phone?"
Su Qingyuan glanced down at her phone. The screen showed more than a dozen unread messages, all from Lu Shiyan.
The first: [What do you want to eat tonight?]
The second: [I'll bring it for you.]
The third: [Busy?]
The fourth: [Reply when you see this.]
The fifth: [I'm just coming over.]
The sixth: [Your studio lights are still on.]
The last: [On my way.]
Su Qingyuan looked up at him, a little guilty. "I… forgot."
Lu Shiyan set the insulated bag on the table, came over, and took a look at her sketchbook. He didn't ask, "What is this." Instead he gazed at it in silence for a long while, then said, "This series will be better than 'First Light.'"
Su Qingyuan was stunned. "You can tell?"
"I can't." Lu Shiyan moved his gaze from the sketchbook to her face. "But I can read your eyes. When you were drawing 'First Light,' your eyes held 'breaking free.' Drawing this, your eyes hold 'acceptance.' The latter is harder than the former."
Su Qingyuan's nose stung. This man didn't understand jewelry, didn't understand design, but he understood her. He understood the emotion behind every one of her drawings, because what he was looking at wasn't the drawing—it was her.
"Lu Shiyan, don't come anymore from now on."
Lu Shiyan's brows knit slightly.
"Every time you come, I want to put down my work and talk to you. But I don't want to put down my work."
"Then work and talk to me at the same time."
"I'll get distracted."
"Distracted is fine. I'll be right beside you, not getting in your way."
Su Qingyuan looked at his earnest expression and couldn't help laughing. "You really know how to talk people around."
Lu Shiyan pulled out a chair and sat down, taking the food out of the insulated bag and laying out each dish one by one.
"Eat first. Draw after you've eaten."
Su Qingyuan obediently sat down and picked up her bowl, then suddenly remembered something. "Oh, right—Cartier headquarters' creative director called me today and invited me to Paris next spring for the global launch of the 'Light Traces' collection."
Lu Shiyan's hand, in the middle of putting food into her bowl, paused. "When?"
"March next year."
"I'll go with you."
"Aren't you really busy?"
"March isn't busy."
Su Qingyuan watched him say "March isn't busy" without batting an eye, and knew perfectly well—Lu Shiyan's March was never not busy. He was simply making time for her.
"Okay." She didn't call out his lie. "We'll go together."
The corners of Lu Shiyan's lips lifted, and he picked out another piece of ribs for her.
At some point the rain outside had stopped. Nancheng's night was quiet and deep, the studio lights a warm amber. The two of them sat across from each other eating, exchanging a word or two now and then, sometimes saying nothing at all.
This kind of quiet brought more peace than any noise ever could.
Meanwhile, in a rented room in a run-down apartment complex on the outskirts of Nancheng.
Su Yurou sat in front of a computer, the glow of the screen lighting up her gaunt, pale face. She wore a pilled gray loungewear set, her hair carelessly tied back, looking for all the world like an ordinary young woman scraping by—if you ignored the obsessive light in her eyes.
On the screen was the interface of an encrypted chat app. The other party's avatar was a blank, their handle a meaningless string of letters.
Su Yurou typed:
[I've thought it over—the thing Director Gu mentioned. I'll do it. But I have a condition.]
The reply came quickly:
[Go on.]
Su Yurou bit her lip, her fingers hovering over the keyboard for a few seconds, then rapidly tapped out a line:
[Once it's done, I want a sum of money. Enough to leave the country and start over. I'm not staying in China anymore—there's no place left for me here.]
The other side was silent for a moment.
[How much?]
Su Yurou quoted a figure.
The reply came fast:
[Fine. But if you fail, this has nothing to do with me. Director Gu will deny any connection to you whatsoever.]
Su Yurou stared at the line, and a cold arc slowly curved at the corner of her mouth.
[I know. I never expected him to admit anything in the first place.]
The other party didn't reply again.
Su Yurou closed the chat app and leaned back in her chair, eyes shut. The room was very quiet, broken only by the occasional gurgle of the heating pipes. Her breathing was light—light enough to seem nonexistent.
She thought back to many years ago, when she was still a little girl. Su Qingyuan had just been adopted not long before, scrawny and small, curled up in a corner too afraid to speak. Yurou had found it amusing and went to tease her—snatching her toys, tearing up the pictures she'd drawn. Su Qingyuan never fought back, only stared at her with reddened eyes, then silently picked up the torn scraps and pieced them back together.
At the time, she'd thought: this one is so easy to push around.
Later they grew up, and she discovered Su Qingyuan's drawing kept getting better, the teachers always praising her, while she herself was forever just "Su Qingyuan's little sister." That feeling of being outshone was like a thorn driven into her heart, deeper and deeper.
Later still, she discovered a secret—Su Qingyuan wasn't their parents' biological daughter. That secret made her feel she finally had the upper hand. You were brought in; I was born here. In this family you're an outsider, and I'm not. She used that advantage to grind Su Qingyuan down again and again. She stole her design drafts, stole her boyfriend, stole everything.
She'd thought she would keep on winning.
But she'd lost. Lost utterly and completely.
Su Yurou opened her eyes, looked at a blackened water stain on the ceiling, and murmured, "Sister Qingyuan, do you know why I hate you?"
"It's not because you're better than me."
"It's because you never once hated me."
"I stole your things, and you didn't hate me. I stole your boyfriend, and still you didn't hate me. The more you refused to hate me, the more I felt like a joke."
Her voice was light as a sigh.
"So this time, I'm going to make you hate me."
"Even if it's only once."
She turned off the light, and the room sank into darkness. Only the computer's power indicator stayed lit—like a red, unblinking eye glowing in the dark.
The next day, when Su Qingyuan arrived at her studio, she found an envelope set by the door.
White, with no sender information—only three characters written on it: Su Qingyuan (To).
She opened the envelope. Inside was a photograph.
The photo was of herself—but not as she was now. It was from a few years ago. Back then she was still working at the Su family's shop, dressed in faded work clothes, hair tied in a messy knot, heavy dark circles under her eyes, holding a half-finished stone, absorbed in grinding it.
The angle of the shot was furtive, as if taken in secret.
Su Qingyuan turned the photo over and saw a line of crooked handwriting on the back:
"Do you still remember the way you looked back then? I remember. I've never forgotten.—Su Yurou"
Su Qingyuan looked at that line and was silent for a long time.
She felt no anger, no fear—only a strange calm, something close to "so it's finally here."
Su Yurou wouldn't lunge out of the dark and bite her all at once. Su Yurou would slowly, bit by bit, plant unease in her heart. This photo was the first seed.
She was telling her: I've been watching you all along. It started a very long time ago. You never knew, but every step you took, I was watching.
Su Qingyuan tucked the photo back into the envelope and put it in a drawer—locked away together with Chen Xiaoman's photo and Lin Haoyu's USB drive.
She wouldn't throw it away. Because she meant to keep them and, at the most crucial moment, lay them all out at once.
Then she sat back down at her worktable, picked up her carving knife, and went on grinding the first sample piece of the "Roots" series.
The sound of the carving knife scraping across metal was fine and fragmented, yet resolute.
The footsteps outside the door, the wind, the distant blare of car horns—none of it had anything to do with her.
She immersed herself in her own world, like a ship sailing into the deep sea—calm on the surface, all power underneath.
That evening, when Lu Shiyan came to pick her up, Su Qingyuan told him about the envelope.
Lu Shiyan listened and stayed silent for a long while. His silence made Su Qingyuan a little uneasy—he usually responded the instant she finished speaking, even if only with "Mm" or "I see." But this time, he didn't.
"What's wrong?" Su Qingyuan asked.
"Su Yurou is testing your limits." Lu Shiyan's voice was low. "She wants to know what it takes to make you lose control. She won't hurt you directly, but she'll do plenty of things to unsettle you—mailing photos, sending messages, leaving traces of herself in the places you least expect. Boiling a frog in slow water; once you've grown used to it, she'll strike hard."
Su Qingyuan nodded. "I know."
"What do you plan to do?"
"Nothing." Su Qingyuan watched the neon flowing past outside the window. "She mails a photo, I'll keep it. She sends a message, I'll read it and delete it. The traces she leaves, I'll see them but pay them no mind. She wants me to lose control—and I just won't."
Lu Shiyan reached out and took her hand.
"Good. Then that's how it'll be."
The car stopped at a red light. Su Qingyuan turned to look at him and suddenly said, "Lu Shiyan, what do you think Su Yurou actually wants?"
Lu Shiyan thought for a moment, then said, "She wants you to become her. To hate her, envy her, suffer the way she does. That way she won't have to suffer alone."
Su Qingyuan was silent for a long time.
The light turned green, and the car moved on.
"Then I won't give her what she wants," she said, her voice soft but firm.
Outside, Nancheng's night grew ever deeper. The streetlights joined into a warm river of light, illuminating the road home.
[System: This chapter has buried a great many foreshadowings. The Cartier global-launch invitation, Su Yurou's new plan, the female lead's "Roots" series. Su Yurou's revenge is no longer open warfare with bared blades—it's psychological warfare. But the female lead is steadier than she imagines. Gu Changfeng's side is quiet for now, but quiet means a bigger move is coming. The next chapter may bring real conflict. The System can't wait.]

========================================

Chapter 27 — Roots and Undercurrents: On Her Home Turf, Her Word Is Law
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626229.html

Su Yurou's photograph was like a stone dropped into a lake—it sent out a single ripple, then sank back into stillness. Su Qingyuan didn't press for answers, didn't investigate, didn't even mention it to anyone again. She simply locked the envelope away in a drawer and carried on with her daily work—as if that letter had never existed at all.
But that silence was, in itself, a kind of answer.
Su Yurou wanted her to be afraid, to panic, to go running all over town asking, "How does she have an old photo of me?" Su Qingyuan did nothing. Her lack of reaction stung Su Yurou far more than any furious outburst could have. Because when you've gone to all that trouble to hand someone a knife, and they won't even spare it a glance—that's the cruelest cut of all.
A few days later, Su Qingyuan received a text. The number was new, but the tone was one she knew far too well.
"Aren't you the least bit curious who took the photo?"
Su Qingyuan looked at it for two seconds, then typed back two words: "Not curious."
The other end went silent for a full ten minutes. Then came another message: "You weren't always like this. You used to get hurt, used to cry, used to ask me why I treated you that way."
This time Su Qingyuan's reply was even shorter: "That was then."
The silence on the other end stretched longer still. Su Qingyuan didn't wait for more. She slipped the phone into her pocket and went back to polishing the first sample piece of her "Roots" series.
She didn't tell Lu Shiyan about the text. Not to hide it—it simply wasn't worth mentioning. What Su Yurou was doing now was like a child waving a plastic knife in front of an adult: alarming to look at, but incapable of so much as scratching her. The real threat wasn't these childish probings—it was Gu Changfeng, who lurked behind Su Yurou and had yet to show his face.
He was too quiet.
After the Hengsheng affair, Gu Changfeng had vanished as if into thin air. He appeared at no public events, commented on nothing, and had even moved Dingfeng Group's internal meetings online. That kind of quiet wasn't normal. It was the suffocating, heavy stillness that comes before a storm.
Lu Shiyan had clearly noticed it too. Lately he'd been coming to the studio more often—sometimes not even in the evening; he'd show up suddenly in the afternoon, settle onto the sofa with his documents, and work there for hours at a stretch. Su Qingyuan knew it wasn't because he was free—it was his way of standing guard at her side without intruding on her work.
She didn't call him out on it. She just brewed an extra pot of tea each day, set it on the side table by the sofa, and went on with her own work. The two of them, each absorbed in their own tasks, would occasionally glance up, catch each other's eye, share a smile, then bow their heads again.
That kind of unspoken understanding was more moving than any sweet words could ever be.
The first piece of the "Roots" series was finished late one Thursday night.
It was a brooch, its main body a cross-section of a tree—growth rings, fissures, scars, all faithfully recreated through micro-pavé work. Each ring was a different gemstone: at the very center sat a deep brown diamond, representing the beginning; moving outward came dark green emeralds for growth; then golden sapphires for the light that settled out of all the struggle; and the outermost ring was a layer of near-transparent white diamonds, thin as frost, like a film of protection.
The rings weren't perfect circles. There were several obvious "wounds"—and in those places she'd used darker stones, not to disguise them, but to let them become part of the rings.
Su Qingyuan cradled the brooch in her palm and studied it under the lamplight for a long time.
This tree was herself. That deep brown diamond at the very center was the attic of the old Su family house—cramped, dim, but the place where she had begun. Each ring spreading outward was a struggle, a stage of growth, a process of new flesh growing out of old wounds. That thin outermost layer of white diamonds was her life now—calm, cool, yet shot through with light.
She took a photo of it, sent it to no one, just saved it into an album on her phone titled "Roots."
Then she wrote a single line for the series: "Roots aren't the place that traps you. They're the place you grew out of."
That line, she decided, would go on the very first page when the series launched.
Having finished the first piece of the "Roots" series, Su Qingyuan did something rare and gave herself a day off. Not to rest—but to see an exhibition.
The Nancheng Art Museum was hosting a contemporary art exhibition called "Land and Bloodline." The participating artists came from all over the world, and the theme of every work was "roots"—homeland, family, memory, identity. When Su Qingyuan saw the exhibition listing online, something in her stirred. Her own "Roots" series was still in the works, and seeing this show right now might just spark some new inspiration.
Lu Shiyan couldn't accompany her—he had a board meeting today that he absolutely had to attend. He arranged for his driver to take her, and Su Qingyuan didn't refuse, because she genuinely wanted to focus on the exhibition and didn't want to waste time in traffic.
The museum wasn't crowded—it was a weekday, after all. Su Qingyuan walked alone through the spacious galleries, looking at the paintings on the walls, the installation pieces, the video works, her steps slow, examining each one carefully.
When she reached a corner deep in the gallery, she stopped.
On the wall hung an enormous black-and-white photograph—the roots of a tree. Not the lush canopy of leaves and branches above ground, but the part below the surface, in the darkness: roots crossing and tangling together. They burrowed deep into the soil, some thick and sturdy, some slender, some already rotting away, some just putting out new shoots.
The photograph was titled: "The Part You Can't See."
On the placard beside it, the artist had written a single line: "People are always quick to praise the leaves and branches, yet forget that it's the roots that hold the tree upright."
Su Qingyuan stood before that photograph for a long time.
She suddenly remembered the old pagoda tree in the back courtyard of the Su family house. As a child, when she'd been beaten and scolded, ignored and left out, locked in the attic and forbidden to eat, she would sneak off to that tree, sit on the roots exposed above the ground, bury her face in her knees, and cry. Those roots were rough and hard, digging painfully into her legs—but to her they were the one thing in that whole house that would never hurt her.
She took out her phone, meaning to photograph the piece. Just as she raised the lens, a voice came from beside her.
"You like this one too?"
Su Qingyuan turned to see a woman in her mid-forties: short hair, no makeup, dressed in loose linen clothes, a sketchbook in her hand. Her eyes were bright, as if they held a great many stories.
"Very much," Su Qingyuan said, lowering her phone. "'The Part You Can't See'—it's a wonderful title."
The woman smiled. "I came up with it. Because I'm the one who took this photograph."
Su Qingyuan paused, then took a fresh look at the woman before her. "You're... Qu Ya?"
She remembered the lead artist of "Land and Bloodline" from the exhibition brochure—Qu Ya, an internationally renowned photographic artist whose work centered on the theme of "root systems," and who had held dozens of solo exhibitions around the world.
Qu Ya nodded with a smile. "You know me?"
"I've seen your work. I never imagined I'd run into you here."
"You came to see the exhibition?"
"Mm. I'm a jewelry designer. I've been working on a series about 'roots' lately, and I wanted to come look for inspiration."
Qu Ya's eyes lit up. "A jewelry designer? What's your name?"
"Su Qingyuan."
Qu Ya thought for a moment, then suddenly let out an "Ah!" "You're the designer behind the 'First Light' collection? My daughter bought your 'First Snow' earrings—she's absolutely smitten with them, wears them every single day."
Su Qingyuan was a little surprised; she hadn't expected an artist of Qu Ya's caliber to know her name.
"Thank you—I'm glad your daughter likes them."
"Not 'likes'—obsessed," Qu Ya said with a laugh, shaking the sketchbook in her hand. "She's a hard child to please. For her to wear something every day, it has to be no ordinary thing."
The two of them fell into conversation. From "The Part You Can't See" to the philosophical meaning of "roots," from photography to jewelry design, from creative work to life itself. Qu Ya was a talkative person, but never grating—every word she spoke carried weight.
"You said you're working on a 'Roots' series," Qu Ya said, opening her sketchbook to show Su Qingyuan the drawings inside. "This is a new set of pieces I've been shooting lately. The theme is 'the roots of the city'—the people who've lived in a city for many years yet have never felt they belong here. Their roots aren't in the soil; they're in memory."
Su Qingyuan looked at those sketches, and something struck her right in the heart. The roots of the city, in memory. Wasn't she exactly that kind of person? She'd lived in Nancheng for more than twenty years, yet had never once felt this was her true home. Her roots weren't Nancheng, weren't the old Su family house—they were those memories: the good and the bad, the sweet and the bitter, all added together, forming who she was.
"Teacher Qu, would you be interested in... collaborating, just once?" Even Su Qingyuan herself was a little startled to hear the words come out. She wasn't someone who extended invitations lightly.
Qu Ya looked at her, curiosity in her gaze. "Collaborate how?"
"You photograph 'roots,' I make 'roots' in jewelry. The same theme, two different ways of expressing it. Maybe we could put on an exhibition together."
Qu Ya was silent for a few seconds, then smiled. "I've done photography exhibitions for twenty years, and no one has ever proposed this kind of collaboration to me." She closed her sketchbook and held out her hand. "Su Qingyuan, I'm looking forward to it."
Su Qingyuan took her hand, her heart beating a little fast.
This was no ordinary collaboration offer. Qu Ya was an internationally renowned photographic artist. To work with her meant that the "Roots" series, before it had even debuted, would already carry a top-tier artistic endorsement.
The two exchanged contact details and agreed to meet next week at Qu Ya's studio to discuss the details. As Su Qingyuan walked out of the museum, the sun was just right, falling warm on her shoulders. She looked up at the sky—it was high and blue, with a few white clouds drifting lazily by.
She suddenly felt that the heavens seemed to be helping her. Not the kind of help where good fortune falls from the sky, but the kind where—once you're ready, the opportunity comes. Before, she hadn't been ready, so opportunities had slipped right past her without her even knowing. Now she was ready, and she caught every chance squarely in her hands.
She took out her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message:
[Ran into the photographer Qu Ya at the museum today. Her daughter's a fan of yours? No, wait, her daughter's a fan of mine? Anyway, she really loves the "First Snow" earrings. We talked about the possibility of collaborating—doing a joint "Roots" exhibition together.]
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: [Qu Ya? Tickets to her solo exhibitions sell out three months in advance. How did you run into her?]
Su Qingyuan: [Fate?]
Lu Shiyan: [Not fate. It's that you now stand at a height where she'd notice you.]
Su Qingyuan stared at that line, the corners of her mouth curving up.
Once, she had looked up to people like Qu Ya, thinking them impossibly out of reach. Now Qu Ya had approached her first, exchanged contact details first, asked to meet first. It wasn't that Qu Ya had changed—it was that she had. She stood in a different place now, saw a different view, and the people who saw that view were different too.
Su Qingyuan: [Are you done with your meeting?]
Lu Shiyan: [Just finished. Where are you? I'll come get you.]
Su Qingyuan sent her location. Fifteen minutes later, Lu Shiyan's car pulled up at the museum entrance.
As Su Qingyuan got in, she noticed a white paper bag on the back seat, printed with a logo she'd never seen before.
"What's this?"
"Passed by a new dessert shop and picked it up on the way," Lu Shiyan said, starting the car, his tone as casual as if he were remarking that the weather was nice today.
Su Qingyuan opened the bag. Inside was an exquisite strawberry cake, the cream adorned with gold leaf and edible flower petals, too pretty to look like food. She picked up her phone and snapped a photo, then scooped a spoonful into her mouth. The cream was light, the cake fluffy, the sweet-tart of the strawberry melting on the tip of her tongue.
"It's good."
"Mm."
"This dessert shop you 'passed by'—what's it called?"
Lu Shiyan gave the name. Su Qingyuan made a mental note of it. Not because she wanted to buy more—but because she wanted to remember that behind every one of his "on the way" gestures, nothing was ever on the way.
As the car turned into the creative park district, Su Qingyuan saw someone standing at the entrance to her studio.
Her smile slowly faded.
It was Su Yurou.
Not the Su Yurou of before, with her flawless makeup and meticulous outfits. She wore a black down jacket, jeans, canvas shoes, her hair loose and unkempt, no makeup on her face. She looked like an ordinary young woman who'd been waiting a long time in the cold wind. But her eyes gave her away—what lay in those eyes was exactly the same as the day they'd torn off all pretense in the hotel lobby months ago, only more intense now.
Lu Shiyan had clearly seen her too. He didn't stop the car; he kept driving forward.
"Not stopping?" Su Qingyuan asked.
"Not stopping. She's come to find you—she won't come just once. See her today, and she'll be back tomorrow. Let her wait."
Su Qingyuan glanced at him and said nothing. She knew he was right. Su Yurou hadn't come today to apologize, hadn't come to make peace—she'd come to test the waters, to see whether Su Qingyuan still had any feelings left toward her. If there were feelings, it meant she still cared. To not care at all—that was the ultimate indifference.
The car circled the park district once, then stopped in a small alley behind the studio. Lu Shiyan cut the engine and turned to look at her.
"Do you want to see her?"
Su Qingyuan thought it over, then shook her head. "No."
"Then don't."
"But she'll keep waiting at the door."
"Then let her wait." Lu Shiyan's voice was very calm. "Her time is hers, your time is yours. You're under no obligation to spend your time answering hers."
Su Qingyuan leaned back against her seat, looking out at the gray, overcast sky beyond the windshield. The two of them just sat there in the car, neither saying a word. At the studio door, Su Yurou was still waiting. Every so often she'd lower her head to check her phone, then look up and glance around, pacing back and forth on the spot, looking restless and on edge.
Forty minutes later, she finally left. As she went, she looked back once at the studio door, her expression carrying both resentment and a stubborn "I'll be back."
Su Qingyuan waited until she was well out of sight before pushing open the door and getting out.
Inside the studio, everything was as usual. The butterfly orchid on the windowsill had bloomed with new flowers, the tools on the workbench were laid out neatly, the sketchbook open to the page she'd drawn up to yesterday.
She walked to the window, looked toward where Su Yurou had disappeared, was silent for a few seconds, then drew the curtains closed.
Not avoidance—a choice. Her gaze was to be saved for things worth looking at.
That evening, Lu Shiyan drove her home. In the car, Su Qingyuan suddenly asked, "Do you think Su Yurou came today on her own idea, or because Gu Changfeng sent her?"
Lu Shiyan tapped the steering wheel twice with his fingers. "Her own idea. Gu Changfeng wouldn't have her do something this stupid—coming straight to you with no preparation at all. Gu Changfeng's style is: either he doesn't move, or he strikes for the kill in one blow. What Su Yurou did today was far too amateur."
Su Qingyuan nodded. She'd thought the same. But Su Yurou's "amateurism" worried her all the more—because amateurs are unpredictable. A professional has methods, patterns, traces to follow; an amateur is capable of anything, because she herself doesn't even know what she's doing.
"I'll keep an eye out," Su Qingyuan said.
Lu Shiyan reached over and gave her hand a squeeze, saying nothing more.
The car stopped below her apartment building. Before getting out, Su Qingyuan suddenly leaned over and kissed him on the cheek.
"The cake today was delicious. Thank you."
Lu Shiyan touched the spot where she'd kissed him, the corner of his mouth lifting slightly. "I'll buy more tomorrow."
"You don't have to buy it every day—I'll get fat."
"You'd still look good even if you did."
Cheeks flushed, Su Qingyuan pushed the door open and got out, walking briskly into the building's entrance without looking back. But she knew he was surely watching her. It was always like this—she would leave, and he would keep watching, until the light came on in her window, before he'd start the car and drive off.
She went upstairs, turned on the light, walked to the window, and looked down. Sure enough, his car was still parked there, headlights off, but the window half-down, so she could see his profile. She picked up her phone and sent him a message: [I'm home. Go on back now.]
Lu Shiyan's reply: [Mm. Close your window tight—it's getting colder tonight.]
Su Qingyuan looked at that line, the corners of her mouth curving up, and closed the window. Then she drew the curtains, leaned against the wall by the window, and listened to his car start, drive off, and disappear into the night.
She lowered her head and looked at her own hand. When she'd met Qu Ya at the museum today, this hand had held her phone, her heart racing. When Su Yurou stood at the studio door, this hand hadn't trembled. When Lu Shiyan took her hand, this hand had been wrapped in his palm, so warm.
This hand had drawn thousands of sketches, polished hundreds of stones, signed Cartier's contract, and held Qu Ya's hand. It wasn't big enough yet, but it was already strong enough. In time, it would only grow stronger.
[System: This chapter, the female lead met Qu Ya, and the "Roots" series gained an artistic endorsement. Su Yurou came to stake out the studio, and the female lead chose not to see her. Lu Shiyan's "Her time is hers, your time is yours" is another line worthy of the textbooks. The sweetness is subtle—the strawberry cake, the glances across the car window, the "close your window tight" after she got home. Next chapter, Su Yurou may come again, or Gu Changfeng will make a move. The details of the collaboration with Qu Ya will also unfold. The System keeps watch.]

========================================

Chapter 28 — Eyes in the Shadows, Light in the Open
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626230.html

Su Yurou came three times.
The first time, she waited outside the studio door for forty minutes, and Su Qingyuan watched her leave from inside her car. The second time was at dusk; Su Qingyuan was on the phone with Qu Ya, hashing out the details of their collaboration, when through a gap in the curtains she saw that figure in the black down jacket standing in the gathering twilight for over half an hour, until the cold wind finally drove her off, shoulders hunched. The third time was early morning. Su Qingyuan had just arrived at the studio when she spotted Su Yurou from a distance, sitting on the steps by the door, a cup of long-cold coffee cradled in her hands, as if she had been sitting there a very long time.
This time, Su Qingyuan didn't walk around her.
She went over and stood in front of Su Yurou, looking down at her from above. The early light was thin, stretching their two shadows long and longer, overlapping and then parting again.
"You've come three times now." Su Qingyuan's voice was calm. "Whatever it is, say it now."
Su Yurou lifted her head. Her eyes were bloodshot, the skin beneath them a heavy bruised blue. She looked like she hadn't slept well in a long time, the whole of her like a flower battered by frost—wilted, drooping, yet with roots still clinging fiercely to the soil.
"Sister Qingyuan, could I come in and sit for a bit? It's cold out here."
Su Qingyuan looked at her for a few seconds, then took out her key and opened the door.
"Ten minutes."
Su Yurou stepped into the studio, her gaze sweeping every corner—the tools laid out neatly on the workbench, the finished pieces in the display cases, the butterfly orchid blooming just right on the windowsill, the pale beige throw blanket on the sofa in the lounge area. There was something complicated in her eyes: envy, jealousy, and a regret too tangled to name—something like, "If I hadn't taken the road I took back then, would a piece of this place have been mine too?"
She sat down in a chair, hands resting on her knees, like a little girl visiting someone else's home for the first time, awkward and ill at ease.
Su Qingyuan didn't pour her any water, and she didn't sit down either. She leaned against the window, arms crossed, watching Su Yurou.
"Go on."
Su Yurou drew a deep breath, as if steeling herself for something enormous.
"Sister Qingyuan, I want to apologize to you. Not the kind of 'I was wrong, so forgive me' apology—a real one. I know the things I did before can't be wiped away with a single 'I'm sorry.' Stealing your designs, taking your boyfriend, falsely accusing you of plagiarism—every one of them, I knew was wrong. But I did them anyway."
As she spoke, her eyes reddened, but no tears fell.
"Because I was jealous of you. From the time we were small, you were better than me at everything. Better at drawing, better at design, even Mom and Dad—even though they're your adoptive parents, they treated you better than they treated me. You never fought, never grabbed for anything, yet every good thing in the world ran straight to you on its own. I couldn't accept it."
Su Qingyuan didn't interrupt her. She listened in silence.
"I took your things not because I wanted them. It was because I wanted you to know—you can't have everything. I wanted you to taste 'loss' too." Su Yurou's voice dropped lower and lower. "But then I realized, no matter what you lost, you never cried, never made a scene. You just quietly accepted it, and then kept walking forward. The more you were like that, the more I hated you."
Su Qingyuan looked at her, silent for a long time.
"Are you finished?"
Su Yurou froze. Clearly she hadn't expected this reaction from Su Qingyuan—no anger, no being moved, not even a ripple.
"You… you're not angry?"
"No." Su Qingyuan's voice was faint. "Su Yurou, your apology is something you owe yourself, not compensation you owe me. You've said it, and it makes you feel better inside—that's your business. But to me, whether you say these things or not, it doesn't matter anymore."
She straightened up from the window, walked to the door, and pulled it open.
"Ten minutes are up. You should go."
Su Yurou stood. Her lips trembled; she wanted to say something, but when she saw Su Qingyuan's eyes, calm to the point of being almost without emotion, she swallowed all of it back. She walked to the door, paused, her back to Su Qingyuan, her voice very soft.
"Sister Qingyuan, I'm going to leave Nancheng."
Su Qingyuan said nothing.
"Gu Changfeng promised me a sum of money to go abroad. I'm planning to go to Australia, to start over." Su Yurou turned around and looked at Su Qingyuan, her eyes red, but her expression far more sincere than before. "I'm not here to say goodbye to you. Because I know you don't care for that. I just wanted you to know—I concede. Not because you won, but because I'm tired."
Su Qingyuan looked at her, silent for a few seconds.
"When you get to Australia, live well."
Just that one line. No "I forgive you," no "I hope you do well," no "we'll still be sisters from now on." Just one line—when you get to Australia, live well. Not forgiveness, but letting it go. Not a blessing, but standing aside. Not reconciliation, but simply no longer being entangled.
Su Yurou's tears finally fell. She wiped them away quickly, turned, and left. The click of her heels against the ground grew fainter and fainter, until at last it vanished into the morning light of the creative park.
Su Qingyuan closed the door, leaned against it, and shut her eyes.
She didn't feel any sense of release, nor did she feel sad. She just felt that—this matter could finally be turned to a new page. It wasn't that she had forgiven Su Yurou; it was that she no longer needed anything from Su Yurou. Not her apology, not her concession, not her departure. She had long since moved Su Yurou out of her life, moved her out cleanly and completely. Whether Su Yurou came or not, apologized or not, left or not—none of it could move her in the slightest.
This kind of "not caring" was more thorough than any revenge.
Su Qingyuan walked to the workbench, picked up her carving knife, and went on polishing the second sample of the "Roots" series. Outside the window the sunlight grew brighter and brighter, falling on her hands, on the carving knife, on the gemstone taking shape beneath them.
Everything went on as usual.
Qu Ya's studio sat in an art village on the western outskirts of Nancheng, converted from an old warehouse. When Su Qingyuan arrived, Qu Ya was in the darkroom developing photographs; hearing the doorbell, she came out to open the door with her hands still wet.
"You're here? Come in, sit wherever you like. I'll be done with this print in a minute."
Su Qingyuan stepped inside and wandered slowly through the studio. The walls were hung with Qu Ya's representative works from across the years—from her early "Homeland" series, to her mid-period "Migration" series, to her most recent "The Unseen Parts." Each photograph was like a window, and through them you could see two decades of Qu Ya's deepening exploration and understanding of the theme of "roots."
Su Qingyuan stopped in front of one photograph. It was a black-and-white image of an old man's hands. Those hands were rough, covered in calluses, with dirt that could never quite be scrubbed from beneath the nails. And yet it was these very hands that cradled a tender sprout just broken through the soil, the gesture as light as if they held the most precious thing in all the world.
The photograph's title was: "Inheritance."
Su Qingyuan looked at those hands and suddenly thought of her own pair. Her hands had once been rough too, calloused, scarred from the carving knife. Now her hands still bore calluses and scars, but those calluses and scars weren't marks of humiliation—they were marks of creation. She suddenly very much wanted to turn this feeling into a piece of work—not the hands themselves, but what the hands carried.
"Lost in it?" Qu Ya's voice came from behind her. She had already emerged from the darkroom, two cups of tea in her hands.
Su Qingyuan took the teacup. "This photograph made me think of myself."
Qu Ya followed her gaze to "Inheritance" on the wall and smiled. "Most people look at this photo and think of their elders. You're the first to say it made you think of yourself."
"Because my hands are like that too." Su Qingyuan held out her hand, palm up. There were a few faint scars on her fingers, and a thin layer of callus at the web of her thumb. "These scars used to be given to me by others. Now these calluses are ones I ground out myself."
Qu Ya lowered her head to look at Su Qingyuan's hand, fell silent for a few seconds, then raised her eyes to look at her, something hard to name now in her gaze. "Su Qingyuan, I'm beginning to understand why your work can carry that kind of warmth. Because your hands are hands with a story."
The two of them sat down on the sofa, tea in hand. Qu Ya's studio wasn't large, but every inch of it breathed its owner's character—understated, yet every detail with a provenance.
Su Qingyuan opened the tablet she'd brought and showed Qu Ya a photograph of the first completed brooch in the "Roots" series. Qu Ya took the tablet, zoomed in, and studied it for a long time.
"The growth-ring part—you used five different gemstones?" There was an undisguised astonishment in her voice.
"Six." Su Qingyuan pointed at the very center. "The innermost is a deep-brown diamond, very small, 0.3 carats. But I searched a long time to find a diamond in this color, because it had to be like a 'starting point'—not the dazzling kind, but the kind that says, 'everything begins from here.'"
Qu Ya handed the tablet back to her, leaned back against the sofa, gazed up at the ceiling, and let out a long breath.
"Su Qingyuan, I've made a decision."
"What decision?"
"Your 'Roots' series, together with my new work—we do a joint exhibition. Not 'I show mine, you show yours,' but a blended show—my photographs and your jewelry placed in the same space, echoing each other. The viewer's gaze moves from the photographs to the jewelry, then back to the photographs, and they see two expressions of the same theme."
Su Qingyuan's heartbeat quickened.
"The venue, the curation, the promotion—I'll handle it all." Qu Ya's tone was as certain as if she were stating something already settled. "You do your creating, I do mine. In half a year, we each bring out our very best work, and we hold a two-person joint exhibition."
"Teacher Qu Ya, I already have a name in mind for this exhibition." Su Qingyuan looked into her eyes. "Call it 'Roots and Light.'"
Qu Ya repeated the name once, and her eyes lit up. "Roots and Light. Your 'Roots' series, my 'Light'? No—your 'First Light' has light in it, and there's light in my photographs too."
"'Roots' is where we come from, 'Light' is where we go." Su Qingyuan said softly. "From roots to light—that's the story I've been telling all along."
Qu Ya looked at her and suddenly laughed.
"Su Qingyuan, how old are you this year?"
"Twenty-four."
"When I was twenty-four, I was still in the darkroom developing photos until dawn, with no idea whether my work would ever be seen by anyone. At twenty-four, you already know what story you want to tell." Qu Ya raised her teacup and tapped it against Su Qingyuan's. "I envy you, and I congratulate you."
Su Qingyuan was scalded a little by those words—"I envy you." Qu Ya—an internationally renowned photographic artist—saying she envied a twenty-four-year-old jewelry designer. Not as a courtesy, not as flattery, but from the heart.
"Teacher Qu Ya, thank you."
"Stop calling me 'Teacher.' Call me Sister Ya."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "Sister Ya."
When she walked out of Qu Ya's studio, the sky over Nancheng was awash with sunset—violet-red, orange-gold, pink-purple, layer upon layer, like an immense oil painting. Su Qingyuan stood at the door, tilted her head up to look at that sky, and drew a deep breath.
She thought of the line Lu Shiyan had once said—"You're standing now at a height where she can see you."
Yes. It wasn't that Qu Ya had suddenly discovered her; it was that she was already standing there. And there wasn't the mountaintop—it was halfway up. The summit was still far, but she was already on her way.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Lu Shiyan: 【Done talking? How did it go?】
Su Qingyuan: 【Done. The joint exhibition—in half a year, called "Roots and Light." Qu Ya said she envies me.】
Lu Shiyan: 【Envies you for what?】
Su Qingyuan: 【Envies that at twenty-four I already know what story I want to tell.】
Lu Shiyan paused, then sent another line: 【What she doesn't know is that before you turned twenty-four, you'd already been telling your story for twenty years. There was just no one listening back then.】
Su Qingyuan looked at that line of words, and her nose suddenly stung.
Twenty years of stories. Those stories had no audience, no applause, no one cheering her on. But she had always been telling them—in the attic of the old Su family house, in the corners of the Su family shop, through countless days and nights that went unseen. Those stories had later grown into "First Light," grown into "Light Traces," grown into "Roots." They hadn't disappeared; they had been waiting all along—waiting for her to stand somewhere high enough that the whole world would hear.
She sent Lu Shiyan two words: 【Understood.】 Then she put away her phone, walked into the sunset, and headed toward the parking lot. She knew Lu Shiyan's car would be waiting there. No matter how late, no matter the wind or rain, he would be there.
On the third day after Su Qingyuan came back from Qu Ya's studio, she received a call from Anna at Cartier.
"Miss Su, the production schedule for the 'Light Traces' collection is running 30% ahead of expectations. Headquarters would like to move the global launch up to mid-January. Is that a problem on your end?"
Su Qingyuan glanced at the calendar on the wall. Mid-January—less than two months away.
"No problem. Has the launch location been decided?"
"It has. Paris, the Cartier flagship store."
Su Qingyuan gripped her phone, her fingertips tightening slightly. The Cartier flagship store, 13 Rue de la Paix—that was the birthplace of Cartier, and a "holy land" in the hearts of jewelers the world over. Countless top-tier jewels had been launched there, but never once had the work of an independent Chinese designer had its global debut in that place.
"Anna, what do I need to prepare?"
"You only need to do one thing—make sure all five pieces of the 'Light Traces' collection are finished and shipped to Paris a week before departure. The rest, leave to us."
"The rest, leave to us" again. Su Qingyuan suddenly remembered that Lu Shiyan often said this line too—"The rest, leave to me." Different people, different matters, the same trust.
"All right. I will."
Su Qingyuan hung up and sat at the workbench for a long while. Then she picked up a pen and wrote a line in her sketchbook: January, Paris. The Cartier flagship store. The launch.
After writing it, she looked at it for a few seconds, then added another line in smaller characters underneath: Take Lu Shiyan to see the Eiffel Tower.
Once she'd written it, she felt it was a little childish and wanted to scratch it out, but her pen tip hovered above the paper and in the end she didn't do it. Never mind. He'd never see it anyway. And even if he did… he'd probably be very happy.
That evening, when Lu Shiyan came to pick her up, Su Qingyuan told him the Cartier launch had been moved up. After he'd heard it all, he didn't say "congratulations," didn't say "I'll go with you." Instead he asked one thing: "Mid-January, Paris is very cold. Do you have a thick coat?"
Su Qingyuan was taken aback, then laughed. "That's what you're worried about?"
"That's the most important thing." Lu Shiyan started the car, his tone as serious as if he were running a project assessment. "Paris in January averages three degrees, and the wind chill could be below zero. You feel the cold easily—you can't stand around in an evening gown for too long. I'll have someone prepare a coat for you, cashmere, light but warm."
Su Qingyuan looked at the dead-serious set of his face, and her heart went hopelessly soft. Another boyfriend hearing that his girlfriend was about to do a global launch in Paris would probably react first with "That's amazing" or "I'll come with you." His first reaction was whether she'd be cold. This man put her warmth and comfort ahead of a "global launch."
"Lu Shiyan, do you know that sometimes you really sound like my mom?"
Lu Shiyan turned his head to glance at her. "I'm not that old."
"I mean nagging."
"I don't nag."
"You're being naggy right now."
Lu Shiyan was silent for a second, then reached over and took her hand, saying nothing more. But the curve at the corner of his mouth—he couldn't press it down no matter what.
The following week, Su Qingyuan went into "seclusion" mode.
The second and third pieces of the "Roots" series had their concepts and first drafts completed within that week. The second was a bracelet, inspired by Qu Ya's "Inheritance"—the chain part used antiqued silver, looking like an old heirloom, but set every few links with a tiny brilliant gemstone, like light that had emerged from the dimness after being worn smooth by generation upon generation of hands. The third was a pair of stud earrings, with the theme of "wounds," using irregular pearls, each one flawed—pits, wrinkles, imperfect shapes—but she didn't hide those flaws. Instead she wrapped fine gold around them, like setting a gold rim around a wound.
A flaw isn't a defect; it's a story. This was a truth she only really understood after doing the "Roots" series. She used to chase perfection in her designs, chase flawlessness, chase having every gemstone present itself in its most perfect state. But now she knew that what truly moves people isn't perfection—it's authenticity. And those flaws were the authenticity.
At the same time, she was pushing forward the last two pieces of the "Light Traces" collection. Cartier was pressing hard, but she didn't lower her standards just to save time. Every piece, she had to personally polish the key parts herself—not because she didn't trust Zhenyi's craftsmen, but because she couldn't set her mind at ease. These works carried her name; she had to make sure every detail was above her standard.
Working over twelve hours a day, sometimes so busy she forgot to eat. Lu Shiyan didn't urge her, "Don't overwork yourself," because he knew it wouldn't work. He just showed up at the studio on time every day, bringing food, watching her finish it, then clearing away the lunch box. Not talking, not disturbing her, just being there.
This kind of "company" wasn't "I sacrificed my time for you," but "your time is my time." Sometimes Su Qingyuan would be drawing and drawing, and look up to see him sitting on the sofa handling documents, and she'd have a hazy sense that—they seemed to have been together a very long time already. Long enough that they didn't need to talk, didn't need to interact; as long as the other was there, there was peace of mind.
On Friday night, Su Qingyuan rarely knocked off early. Lu Shiyan took her to a newly opened restaurant for Sichuan food, her favorite. When the maoxuewang came out, its red chili oil bubbling and rolling, Su Qingyuan's eyes were brighter than the chili oil on the table.
"Eat slowly, no one's going to fight you for it." Lu Shiyan poured her a glass of iced water.
Su Qingyuan picked up a piece of tripe, sucked in air at the heat of it but couldn't bear to spit it out, and said in a muffled mumble, "It's good."
Lu Shiyan looked at her lips, reddened by the spice, his gaze as soft as moonlight.
"Su Qingyuan."
"Hm?"
"Once the Paris business is over, I'll take you to see the aurora."
Su Qingyuan's chopsticks paused mid-air, and she looked up at him. "Didn't you say we'd go once everything was over?"
"Everything will never be over." Lu Shiyan's voice was very soft. "Gu Changfeng won't disappear, Su Yurou won't disappear, trouble won't disappear. But we can choose, in the gaps between the trouble, to go and see the aurora."
Su Qingyuan set down her chopsticks and looked into his eyes. The restaurant's warm amber light fell on his face, his expression calm and certain, as if he were speaking of something bound to happen.
"All right. We'll go see the aurora." She picked up her iced water and tapped it against his glass.
The two of them clinked glasses, the iced water rippling faintly in them, reflecting the warm amber light overhead.
Outside the window, Nancheng's night was deepening.
The weather forecast said there would be snow tonight.
Nancheng's first snow was finally about to come.
When they walked out of the restaurant after dinner, Su Qingyuan tilted her head up to look at the sky and waited a long while, but no snow fell. A little disappointed, she pulled her scarf up to cover half her face.
"It didn't snow." Her voice was muffled in the scarf, sounding like a child who hadn't gotten the candy she was promised.
Lu Shiyan looked down at her, reached out to straighten her scarf, his fingertips brushing her cheek. "It might tomorrow. Or the day after. But it will snow eventually."
"How do you know?"
"The weather forecast said so."
"The weather forecast isn't accurate."
"Then I say it's accurate." Lu Shiyan took her hand, fingers interlaced with hers. "My forecast is more accurate than the weather forecast."
Su Qingyuan let him pull her along, and looking at their two clasped hands, she suddenly felt that whether it snowed or not no longer mattered. Because her hand was already held in the warmest place.
The car stopped at the foot of her apartment building. Su Qingyuan didn't get out right away; instead she sat in the passenger seat, watching through the windshield what was drifting down beneath the streetlamps—not snow, but sleet, fine and dense, melting the instant it struck the glass.
"Does this count as the first snow?" she asked.
Lu Shiyan looked out the window, thought for a moment, and said, "It counts. In meteorology, sleet counts as snow too."
Su Qingyuan laughed, her brows and eyes curving, like a child who'd finally gotten her candy.
"Then we saw it together."
"Mm. Together."
The two of them sat in the car, watching the fine, dense sleet beneath the streetlamps, neither of them speaking. The world outside the window was very quiet—quiet enough to hear each other's breathing.
Su Qingyuan suddenly leaned over and dropped a very, very light kiss on Lu Shiyan's lips.
"A first-snow wish."
"What wish?"
"I'm not telling you. If I say it, it won't come true."
Lu Shiyan looked at her bright, shining eyes and didn't press. He reached out and gently ruffled her hair, his voice low. "Then keep it. Tell me when it comes true."
Su Qingyuan curved her lips, pushed the door open, got out, and walked briskly into the building entrance. At the foot of the stairs, she stopped and looked down through the stairwell window. His car was still parked there, headlights off, but the window half-down, and he was gazing up at this building, though she couldn't tell which window he was looking at. She picked up her phone and sent him a message: 【I'm up. You can head back.】
Lu Shiyan's reply: 【Mm. Shut your windows tight.】
Su Qingyuan: 【Done.】
Lu Shiyan: 【Is the heating on?】
Su Qingyuan: 【On. Why are you just like my mom.】
Lu Shiyan: 【You don't like it?】
Su Qingyuan looked at those three words, the corner of her mouth curving to its widest arc, and typed: 【I like it.】
The other side was silent for a few seconds.
Lu Shiyan: 【Good, then. Goodnight.】
Su Qingyuan: 【Goodnight.】
She didn't draw the curtains; instead she stood at the window, watching his headlights come on, drive out of the complex, and disappear around the street corner.
The sleet outside the window was still falling, fine and dense, striking the glass and leaving trails of water. She stretched out a finger and drew a crooked little heart on the windowpane, then watched it slowly blur and vanish, covered over by new streaks of water.
She drew her hand back, walked to the workbench, picked up her sketchbook, and opened to a new page.
Her pen tip touched the paper, and she wrote a line:
"First snow, Nancheng, sleet. He's downstairs."
After writing it, she looked at it for a long time, then folded down a corner of the page, closed the sketchbook, and turned off the light.
In the dark, she lay in bed, listening to the fine, dense sound of the sleet outside the window, and shut her eyes. She dreamed many dreams—dreams with auroras in them, with the Eiffel Tower, with that bare osmanthus tree outside the studio window, with his face.
Every one of those dreams was bright.
【System: This chapter, Su Yurou comes to apologize and then leaves; the heroine's "not caring" is the highest-level slap in the face. The collaboration with Qu Ya is sealed, and the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition is officially greenlit. The Cartier launch is moved up to January, and Paris is within sight. The first snow may not have been pure snow, but the two of them watched the sleet together, so it counts. Su Yurou says she's going to Australia—but will Gu Changfeng really let her go? The System thinks not. Next chapter, Gu Changfeng may make his move. The System is keeping watch.】

========================================

Chapter 29 — Snow Falls in Silence, the Undercurrent Runs On
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626231.html

After the first snow, the temperature in Nancheng plunged ten degrees overnight.
Su Qingyuan changed into a thick cashmere overcoat—the one Lu Shiyan had had prepared for her, smoke-gray, cleanly tailored, warm without being bulky. When she walked into the studio wearing it, the heating had only just come on, and the room still held the chill it had gathered through the night. She didn't rush to switch on the work lamps; first she watered the moth orchid on the windowsill, then stood at the window looking out at the dull, gray sky.
Su Yurou had said she was leaving.
Five days had passed since that conversation. Su Qingyuan hadn't received a single word from Su Yurou since—no texts, no calls, no figure suddenly appearing at the studio door. It was quiet, the way a single drop of water falls into a deep pool: once the ripples spread and faded, the surface lay so still it was as if nothing had ever happened.
But Su Qingyuan knew that the calm before a storm was often the most unsettling of all.
She wasn't worried that Su Yurou would have a change of heart, nor that Su Yurou would try something more—Su Yurou's fangs and claws had been all but pulled out; even if she wanted to wound someone, she couldn't reach anywhere vital. What worried her was Gu Changfeng. That man was too quiet. Ever since the Hengsheng affair, he hadn't made a single move—like a snake lying dormant in its winter den, neither eating nor drinking, simply waiting for spring to warm the world into bloom.
But it was winter now.
What was he waiting for?
Su Qingyuan drew her gaze back, sat down at her worktable, and opened the production-progress sheet for the "Light Traces" collection. Zhenyi's capacity was running at full tilt; the first batch of finished pieces had been crated yesterday and was expected to reach Paris in three days. Cartier's flagship store was putting the final touches on the exhibition hall for the global debut of "Light Traces," and Anna sent photos from the site every day—from an empty hall to display cases gradually filling up, from white walls to the pale gray she'd specified, from ordinary lighting to a color temperature calibrated specifically for "Light Traces."
Looking at those photos, Su Qingyuan sometimes felt it wasn't quite real.
13 Rue de la Paix. The birthplace of Cartier. Her work would make its debut there, before the whole world.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Zhou Wanyin: [Qingyuan, Qu Ya's team has sent over the preliminary plan for the joint exhibition. When are you free—shall we go through it together?]
Su Qingyuan replied: [This afternoon? My studio.]
Zhou Wanyin: [Sounds good. I'll bring lunch.]
Su Qingyuan set down her phone and went on working. Lately she'd developed a new habit—filling every moment that might otherwise drift into worry with work. It wasn't avoidance; it was channeling her energy into the things she could control. When Gu Changfeng would strike, and how, she couldn't control. But she could control her own work—every line, every cut, every detail.
That afternoon, Zhou Wanyin arrived at the studio right on time.
She wore a red down jacket and carried two big insulated bags, looking less like a famous designer than like a mother dropping off a meal for her daughter. "We're having Japanese bento today—I saw a new place getting good reviews in our group chat yesterday, so I ordered two." She set the insulated bags down on the table in the lounge area, took off her down jacket, and looked the studio over. "Did you change something in here again? Did you swap out the flower on the windowsill?"
"I did. The moth orchid's bloom period was over, so I replaced it with a cyclamen." Su Qingyuan rose from her worktable and rubbed at her aching neck.
Zhou Wanyin caught the faint shadows under her eyes and frowned. "Did you stay up late again?"
"No. I just got up early."
"What time?"
"Six."
"That's not so bad." Zhou Wanyin took the bento out and laid them out bowl by bowl. "But your workload's been too heavy lately—Cartier isn't even done yet and the joint exhibition's already starting. You're not made of iron. Your body is the capital of the revolution; if you collapse, who's going to do the designing?"
Su Qingyuan sat down, picked up the miso soup, and took a sip—warm, the heat traveling down her throat all the way into her stomach. "Sister Wanyin, you're getting naggier and naggier."
"You drove me to it. If you were my daughter, I'd have pinned you down at home to rest ages ago."
Su Qingyuan curved her lips and said nothing. The way Zhou Wanyin said it carried a very natural sort of concern—not politeness, not social nicety, but the kind of unguarded bluntness you only show once you've come to think of someone as your own. After eating for a while, she suddenly asked, "Sister Wanyin, have you heard anything about Gu Changfeng making moves recently?"
Zhou Wanyin's chopsticks paused mid-air. She set them down and looked at her. "Why are you suddenly asking about that?"
"He's too quiet. After the Hengsheng business, he did nothing. That's not like him."
Zhou Wanyin fell silent for a few seconds, then took out her phone, scrolled through a few pages, and handed it over. "I didn't mean to tell you—I was afraid it would distract you. But since you've asked, see for yourself."
Su Qingyuan took it. On the screen was an unpublished press release. The headline read:
[Exclusive Exposé: Biennale Gold Medalist Su Qingyuan and Her "Unconventional" Relationship with Lu Group President Lu Shiyan]
Her fingers tightened a fraction.
The piece was written very "cleverly"—it didn't say outright that she'd plagiarized, didn't say outright that she'd leaned on a man. Instead, in a tone of "objective neutrality," it "laid out" her timeline from nobody to Biennale gold medalist, then gave a light little tap at each key juncture—"Coincidentally, around this very time, Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan's private relationship was heating up."
Not a single word in the whole thing was a lie, but strung together, every word hinted at the same meaning: her success had nothing to do with talent.
When she'd finished reading, Su Qingyuan handed the phone back to Zhou Wanyin. "When is this piece going out?"
"Originally scheduled for yesterday. But for some reason it was pulled at the last minute." Zhou Wanyin put her phone away. "I had someone look into it—it's a media company under Dingfeng Group running the operation. The piece was already laid out, the ad slots all bought, and then it was called off at the eleventh hour."
Su Qingyuan leaned back in her chair and gazed up at the ceiling. Called off. Why call it off? What was Gu Changfeng waiting for?
"Qingyuan, what are you going to do?" Zhou Wanyin asked.
"Nothing." Su Qingyuan sat up straight and took another sip of miso soup. "The piece wasn't published, so I'll act as if I don't know. If it runs, I'll respond as I should. But going and asking about it now would only make me look guilty."
Zhou Wanyin looked at her, an emotion in her eyes hard to name—something like relief, something like heartache. "You're far steadier than before. In the old days, when something like this hit you, you'd cry first, then carry it all alone and never tell a soul."
"In the old days I was alone." Su Qingyuan set down her bowl. "I'm not anymore."
Zhou Wanyin smiled and didn't ask "who is it now." The answer was too obvious.
After seeing Zhou Wanyin off, Su Qingyuan didn't go straight back to her worktable. She stood at the window, looking out at the dull gray sky, her mind turning the whole time. Gu Changfeng had bought the ad space, laid out the piece, lined it up to run yesterday—and then called it off at the last moment. What was he waiting for?
A better opportunity? Was he waiting until her "Roots and Light" joint exhibition was officially announced before publishing, so he could strike at her and Qu Ya at once? Until after the Cartier debut, so Cartier would get dragged into the whirlpool of public opinion?
Or—was he waiting for Su Yurou to leave?
Su Qingyuan picked up her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message: [Gu Changfeng had a hit piece ready earlier and pulled it at the last minute. Do you know anything about this?]
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly, but it wasn't text—it was a call.
"I know." His voice was steady, but Su Qingyuan caught the cold edge beneath it. "That piece is still in my hands."
"In your hands?"
"The ad supplier the media company used is one of Lu Group's partners. After the piece was laid out, the supplier's people saw it, felt something was off, and reported it up. I had someone go talk to the media company—no threats, no pressure, just a conversation. They were sensible about it and pulled it on their own."
Su Qingyuan held the phone, silent for a few seconds. "Does Gu Changfeng know it was you?"
"He knows. But he has no proof." Lu Shiyan's voice was light. "So for now he won't make a move. Because he doesn't know which of his moves I'll be there to intercept."
It suddenly struck Su Qingyuan that Gu Changfeng's situation now was like a man walking through the dark of night, dreading at every step that he'd tread on a trap. He couldn't be sure how far Lu Shiyan's reach extended, couldn't be sure which path was safe, couldn't be sure—whether he could still win this fight at all.
That kind of "uncertainty" wore a person down more than any direct blow.
"Lu Shiyan, when did you start laying this groundwork?"
"From the day you signed the contract with Zhou Wanyin."
That day again. Su Qingyuan couldn't help laughing. "Did you make that day your 'Su Qingyuan Protection Plan launch date'?"
A low laugh came from the other end of the line. "More or less."
Su Qingyuan stood at the window, phone in hand. The sky outside was still very gray, but inside her heart it was bright as day.
That evening, when Lu Shiyan came to pick her up, he brought a bag of sugar-roasted chestnuts.
"Saw them on the way and picked them up." He handed them over as casually as if he were remarking that traffic was good today.
Su Qingyuan took the bag. The chestnuts were still warm, their shells glossy with oil, the split openings revealing golden-yellow kernels. She peeled one and popped it into her mouth—soft and sweet, so hot she had to puff out little breaths.
"Delicious."
"Mm."
"These chestnuts you picked up on the way—where'd you buy them?"
Lu Shiyan named a street. Su Qingyuan filed it away in her mind. That street wasn't on any of the usual routes between his company and the creative park; he'd gone out of his way to buy them.
She didn't call him out. She peeled a chestnut and held it up to his lips. "Open up."
Lu Shiyan glanced at her, then opened his mouth and ate it. The chestnut's sweetness melted on his tongue, and the corners of his lips lifted faintly.
The car stopped at a red light. Su Qingyuan watched the world beyond the window and suddenly said, "Lu Shiyan, what do you think Gu Changfeng will do next?"
Lu Shiyan thought it over and said, "He'll wait. Wait for a moment when I'm not around."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "When you're not around?"
"Like when I'm away on business, or have an important commercial event I have to attend and can't stay by your side." Lu Shiyan tapped his fingers lightly once on the steering wheel. "He'll choose a time like that to strike, because that's when I won't have the bandwidth to cover both fronts."
Su Qingyuan was quiet for a moment. "Do you have any business trips coming up?"
"No. I've had my assistant push all of them back."
"Push them back to when?"
"To whenever you feel safe."
Su Qingyuan's nose prickled. He hadn't pushed his trips back to some specific date, but to a condition—"whenever you feel safe." That condition was hers to define; the initiative lay in her hands.
She lowered her head and went on peeling chestnuts, setting each peeled kernel one by one into the paper bag. When she'd gathered five or six, she handed the bag to him.
"For you. I peeled them."
Lu Shiyan glanced down at the perfectly whole kernels in the paper bag, reached out to take it, and said in a low voice, "Su Qingyuan, when you do things like this, I'm going to want to go on business trips less and less."
"Then don't go."
"What about work?"
"Is work more important than me?"
Lu Shiyan looked at her for two seconds, then laughed. Not his usual restrained, reined-in faint smile, but the kind that said "you win"—helpless and willing all at once.
"You're the most important."
Su Qingyuan was scalded by those three words and turned her face away, pretending to look out the window. Beyond the glass, Nancheng's night was descending, the streetlamps lighting up one by one, joining into a warm river of light. Her reflection showed in the car window, the curve of her lips refusing to be pressed flat no matter what.
The car stopped at the foot of her apartment building. Su Qingyuan didn't get out right away; she leaned back against the seat and looked at the streetlamps beyond the windshield.
"Lu Shiyan, do you think Su Yurou will really leave?"
"Whether she leaves depends on whether Gu Changfeng lets her." Lu Shiyan's voice was steady. "Gu Changfeng has poured a lot of resources into her—he won't let her go easily. And even if he does, he'll have her finish one last thing first."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "One last thing?"
"For instance, stepping forward at some critical moment to say things that hurt you." Lu Shiyan's tone was very calm, like someone analyzing a business case. "Su Yurou is your adoptive sister; in outsiders' eyes, her words carry credibility. If she suddenly appears at the crucial moment of the Cartier debut or the joint exhibition's opening and says some half-true, half-false things to the media, your brand image will take damage."
Su Qingyuan's heart sank. It wasn't that she hadn't thought of this possibility, but coming from Lu Shiyan's mouth, it carried a different weight entirely. He wasn't just guessing—he was telling her: this possibility, he'd already considered, and he'd already prepared a response.
"Do you have a contingency plan?" she asked.
"I do." Lu Shiyan reached out and took her hand. "If Su Yurou steps forward, we'll release everything at once—the court verdict from when she falsely accused you, the chat records Lin Haoyu provided, the photos Chen Xiaoman took. The moment she dares to open her mouth, we'll silence her for good."
Su Qingyuan looked at their two clasped hands and was quiet for a long while. Releasing all that evidence meant tearing open every scar again, letting the whole world look once more. But sometimes, tearing open a scar isn't a show of weakness—it's so people can see that the wound has healed, that it won't hurt anymore.
"All right. Let's do it that way."
She pushed the door open and got out, walked a couple of steps, then turned back. Lu Shiyan was watching her through the car window. "Lu Shiyan, the chestnuts tonight were delicious. But tomorrow you don't need to go out of your way to buy them—it's too far."
Lu Shiyan was taken aback, then laughed. "How did you know I went out of my way?"
"Because that street isn't on any route between your company and my studio. I checked the map."
Lu Shiyan looked at her, his gaze deep as the night. "Su Qingyuan, you're getting harder and harder to fool."
"When have you ever fooled me?"
"Every time I say 'on the way.'"
Su Qingyuan laughed, her eyes crinkling. "Then from now on, don't say 'on the way.' Say 'on purpose.'"
"On purpose."
The two of them looked at each other, and both smiled.
Su Qingyuan turned and went upstairs, her steps so light it was as if she were treading on clouds. At her door she fished out her keys, opened it, and switched on the light. The cyclamen on the windowsill was blooming beautifully under the lamplight, its pink-and-white petals like so many tiny butterflies.
She walked to the window and looked down. His car was still parked there, the window half down, his face tilted up toward her window.
She picked up her phone and sent him a message: [I'm in. Head back now.]
Lu Shiyan's reply: [Mm. Shut the window properly, and don't crank the heating too high—it'll dry you out.]
Su Qingyuan looked at that line, curved her lips, closed the window, and drew the curtains.
She lay down on the bed and shut her eyes.
A great many things were turning in her mind—the "Light Traces" debut, the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition, Gu Changfeng's dormancy, whether Su Yurou would stay or go, the look on his face when he said "you're the most important," that one perfectly peeled chestnut. These things turned for a long time, then slowly quieted down, like a world after a heavy snow, all its clamor buried under white.
She turned over, pulled the quilt up to her chin, and sank into a deep sleep.
At that same moment, in a high-end residential district on the outskirts of Nancheng.
Gu Changfeng stood at the floor-to-ceiling window of his study, a cup of tea gone stone-cold in his hand. Beyond the glass lay the complex's night scene; the artificial lake shimmered with cold white light under the moon, and a few ground lamps lit the pebbled paths into a soft blur.
His phone buzzed. He glanced at the caller ID and answered.
"Director Gu, about Su Yurou… she asked again today about that sum of money."
Gu Changfeng said nothing.
"She says that if she still doesn't get the money by next week, she'll figure something out herself. She says she doesn't mind 'going down in flames and taking everyone with her.'"
Gu Changfeng lifted the teacup and took a sip of the cold tea, bitterness spreading across his tongue. "Tell her the money's ready. She'll get it next week. But first she has to do one last thing."
"What thing?"
Gu Changfeng set the cup down, walked over to his desk, took a USB drive from the drawer, and turned it over in his hand. "Have her write an open letter. Doesn't need to be long—just say that Su Qingyuan's work wasn't completed by her alone. That back in the Su household, Su Yurou took part in the early conception of the 'First Light' collection too."
There was a brief silence on the other end. "Director Gu, that's fake. Su Yurou never took part in any of it."
