"True or false doesn't matter. What matters is that people believe it." Gu Changfeng's voice was cold—cold as the moonlight outside the window. "The most valuable thing Su Qingyuan has right now isn't her designs—it's her 'original creator' label. All it takes is one crack in that label and her brand value shrinks. When the brand shrinks, Cartier will reassess the partnership; when Cartier wavers, Lu Shiyan's jewelry division takes a hit; when Lu Shiyan takes a hit, my opening arrives."
"But last time Su Yurou said she didn't want to do this anymore. She wants to take the money and go."
"Then let her choose." Gu Changfeng put the USB drive back in the drawer. "Either she takes the money and writes this letter, or she gets nothing and leaves on her own. If she chooses the latter, then every single thing she's done before, I'll see to it she bears the consequences of it herself. She's not a fool. She knows how to choose."
The other end let out a sigh. "Understood. I'll go tell her."
The call ended. Gu Changfeng walked back to the window and looked out at the night. The moonlight fell across his face; his expression was very calm, but in his eyes burned an obsessive light—a light that wasn't hatred, but unwillingness. Unwilling to have lost the bid, unwilling to have been suppressed by Lu Shiyan for twenty years, unwilling to watch a girl in her early twenties stand unrivaled in the spotlight while he could only stand in the shadows, using methods that couldn't bear the light of day, trying to claw back a single round.
He thought back many years, to when he'd competed with Lu Shiyan's father for the same project. He was still young then, certain he would win. He lost. From that day on, he never once won against anyone from the Lu family.
"This time," he murmured to himself, gazing at the moon outside, "I won't lose again."
He turned off the light and walked out of the study. The whole room sank into darkness, with only the moonlight outside falling coldly across that empty desk.
And across the USB drive in the drawer—the hidden weapon it concealed.
[System: This chapter—Gu Changfeng's hit piece was intercepted by Lu Shiyan, Su Yurou was ordered to write an open letter, and the female lead's "Roots and Light" joint exhibition and Cartier debut are both moving forward. Gu Changfeng is about to strike, but not with open blades and bared spears—he's using Su Yurou, that already-rusted knife, to cut a slit into the "original creator" label the female lead treasures most. The next chapter may bring a true head-on clash. The sweetness is the sneaky kind—sugar-roasted chestnuts, going out of the way on purpose, "you're the most important." The System will be staking out the next chapter.]

Chapter 30 — The Light of Daybreak
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626232.html

The night Su Yurou received the template for the open letter, it rained over Nancheng until dawn.
She sat at the desk in her rented room, the glow of the screen lighting up her gaunt face. The document Gu Changfeng's people had sent her wasn't long—just over three hundred characters—written in a tone of "sincere confession," recounting how she had "taken part" in the early conception of Su Qingyuan's "First Light" collection. "Those late-night discussions, those sparks of inspiration—Qingyuan didn't create those works alone. I contributed my ideas to them too."
Every single word of it was a lie.
Su Yurou stared at the screen, fingers hovering over the keyboard, slow to come down. She thought of Su Qingyuan's back as she sat sketching on the attic floor. Back then their rooms had been separated by a single wall, and she had often heard the sound drifting through from the other side—pencil scraping across paper, a soft rustle, rustle, rustle, like silkworms feeding on mulberry leaves, going on and on into the dead of night. She had never once pushed that door open. Never once asked, "What are you drawing?" And she had certainly never "taken part" in any discussion.
She had only sat on this side of the wall, listening to that rustling sound, going half-mad with jealousy.
Her phone buzzed. A prodding message from Gu Changfeng's people: "Miss Su, is the letter ready? Director Gu would like to have it by tomorrow."
Su Yurou typed a few words, deleted them, typed again, deleted again. In the end she replied with a single line: "I'll have it to you before noon tomorrow."
The reply came: "Good. The money will be in your account within twenty-four hours of the letter going out."
Su Yurou closed the chat window and opened a blank document, her fingers resting on the keyboard for a long while. Then she began to type—but not the open letter. What she typed was a real letter. A letter to Su Qingyuan.
"Qingyuan,
By the time you read this, I should already be gone from Nancheng. Maybe in Australia, maybe somewhere else—anywhere but here.
Gu Changfeng asked me to write an open letter saying I had a hand in the 'First Light' collection. He wanted me to trade that for the money. He said it didn't matter whether it was true or false—as long as someone believed it.
I don't know what his face will look like when he reads this. He'll probably fly into a rage. He might send people after me, or he might not, because in his eyes I'm already a dead piece on the board, not worth the effort.
My flight is this afternoon. This evidence is the one thing I can finally do right before I go.
Qingyuan, in this life I owe you far too much. More than I can ever repay. I won't say 'I'll repay you in the next life,' because in the next life I don't want to meet you again. Not because I hate you—because I can't bear to face you.
Su Yurou"
She printed the letter out, and together with the USB drive—which held every chat record between her and Gu Changfeng, the transfer receipts, and the open-letter template—she slid it all into a kraft-paper envelope and sealed it. On the envelope she wrote three characters: Su Qingyuan.
Then she picked up her phone and booked an afternoon flight to Melbourne.
Gu Changfeng's money—she didn't want it anymore. Australia, she was still going. But not on his money—on the last of her own savings. It wasn't much, enough to last a few months. Once she got there she'd figure something out—a job, a small business, anything. Whatever happened, she couldn't stay here any longer.
She drew back the curtain. At some point the rain outside had stopped. The clouds had split open along a seam, and sunlight spilled down through the gap, falling onto the rooftop of the building across the way, golden and glittering.
Su Yurou looked at that light, and suddenly remembered when she was very small, not long after Su Qingyuan had first come to live with them. One evening they had sat together on the steps in the courtyard, and Su Qingyuan had pointed at the sunset on the horizon and said, "Look—that light is so beautiful." She had answered scornfully, "What's so great about it? It's there every day." Su Qingyuan hadn't argued. She had simply gazed quietly at that swath of light, the colors of the sunset reflected in her eyes.
Now, all at once, she understood—what Su Qingyuan had been looking at then wasn't the sunset. It was the beam of light she had always carried inside her. That light had carried her through every stretch of darkness, all the way to today.
And she, Su Yurou, had never had a light like that. She had only jealousy, resentment, and the stubborn refusal to lose. Those things could keep a person going for a while—but not for a lifetime.
Su Yurou tucked the envelope into her bag, picked up her keys, and went out the door.
It was two in the afternoon when Su Qingyuan received the envelope. She had just come back from Qu Ya's studio, the venue layout plans for the joint exhibition still in her hand. The envelope had been left under the doormat outside her studio door, one corner peeking out. No sender information, no shipping label—clearly placed there by hand.
She bent down and picked it up, recognizing the handwriting of the three characters "Su Qingyuan" on the envelope—crooked and uneven, like a grade-schooler's writing. Something stirred in her chest. She took out her keys, unlocked the door, stepped inside, and tore the envelope open.
The paper was thin, A4, as if pulled straight from a printer. She read the handwritten letter first. Su Yurou's writing was as ugly as ever, but every character was pressed down hard, some of the strokes torn through by the nib, leaving tiny smears of ink.
When she finished, she stayed silent for a long time.
Then she took out the USB drive and plugged it into her computer. The contents were more complete than she'd imagined—the chat records between Su Yurou and Gu Changfeng, from the Hengsheng affair all the way to the open-letter template, every message time-stamped. There were also several audio recordings—phone calls between Su Yurou and Gu Changfeng, their contents enough to make the blood run cold.
Su Qingyuan pulled the drive out and locked it in a drawer. Then she picked up her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "Su Yurou is gone. She didn't write the open letter—she left behind all of Gu Changfeng's evidence. The letter says her flight to Australia is this afternoon."
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "I just got word—Gu Changfeng's people are looking for her too. Looks like she didn't just refuse to write the letter. She sold Gu Changfeng out."
Su Qingyuan looked at the message and felt a sudden wave of feeling. In this life, Su Yurou had done many wrong things. But this once, she had done one thing right. Not because she had suddenly turned good—because she had finally grown tired, too tired to keep lying to herself. Sometimes a person's change isn't an epiphany. It's exhaustion. Once you've burned away all your malice, your resentment, your obsession, maybe in the ashes that remain there's still a little something clean, something like a conscience.
Su Qingyuan replied to Lu Shiyan: "Is that evidence enough to shut Gu Changfeng up?"
Lu Shiyan: "Not enough. But enough to make him realize he can't control everything."
Su Qingyuan curved her lips, set the phone down on the workbench, picked up her carving knife, and went on polishing the fourth sample in the "Roots" series. The sunlight outside crept out from behind the clouds, falling on her hand, on the carving knife, on the gem taking shape beneath it. Everything was the same as always. And yet everything was different now.
Three days later, Gu Changfeng's people found Su Yurou's rented room. The place was empty, the key still in the door, the room swept clean, as if no one had ever lived there. The only thing left was a note on the desk that read: "Don't bother looking. You won't find me."
When Gu Changfeng saw that note, his expression turned as sour as if he'd swallowed a pound of bitter herbs. The chess piece he'd poured so many resources into cultivating had bolted without a word—and before bolting had copied every piece of evidence over to his opponent. It wasn't the money he grieved over. It was his own judgment. He had thought Su Yurou would stay obedient, would go on hating Su Qingyuan forever, would go on being the good, useful blade in his hand. But he'd forgotten—a blade used long enough rolls at the edge, and a blade with a rolled edge would sooner snap itself than be held again.
He sat in the leather chair in his study, staring out at the gray, overcast sky, and was struck by the sudden feeling that this fight—he might not win it. Not because Lu Shiyan was too strong. Because he himself had chosen the wrong people. Every one he'd picked—Su Yurou, Director Jiang, that media company—had a weakness. He'd used those weaknesses to control them, and forgotten that people with weaknesses can turn and bite back at any moment.
And the person Lu Shiyan had chosen—Su Qingyuan—had no weakness. Or rather, her weakness wasn't the kind that could be exploited. The things she cared about—her work, her bottom line, her principles—none of them could be bought with money or shaken with threats.
Gu Changfeng closed his eyes and leaned back against the chair, exhaling a long breath. The breath fogged into a white cloud in the cold air, then dissolved.
He opened his eyes, picked up his phone, and dialed a number. "That piece we pulled earlier—reformat it. Run it tomorrow."
The voice on the other end hesitated. "Director Gu, if we run it now, with Su Yurou's side—"
"Su Yurou is already gone. Her account, her chat records, every trace—all wiped clean. With no evidence, Su Qingyuan can't touch me."
"But what if—"
"There is no what if." Gu Changfeng's voice was cold as iron. "Run it."
The next morning, the hit piece went out on schedule.
The headline read: "Exclusive Exposé: Biennale Gold Medalist Designer Su Qingyuan and Her 'Unconventional' Relationship with Lu Group President Lu Shiyan." The wording was more "polite" than the first version, but the insinuation ran far stronger. Every "coincidence" was bolded, every overlap in the timeline highlighted in red, and by the end, any ordinary reader would be left with a question mark in their mind—was she genuinely talented, or did she rise through connections?
In the first hour after the piece went out, the comment section was still fairly rational—some questioned its truthfulness, some demanded the evidence be shown. By the second hour, the paid trolls had moved in, and the comments began tilting one-sidedly toward the negative. "I always felt there was something off about her—how could a no-name designer with no background suddenly blow up like that?" "The Cartier collaboration was forced through by pressure from Lu Group too, right? Cartier really has no backbone." "Biennale gold medal, ha—money makes the world go round." By the third hour, Su Qingyuan's Weibo was flooded with abuse. Her DMs overflowed, her comment section fell, and the studio phone rang off the hook.
Su Qingyuan didn't panic. She sat at her workbench, the USB drive Su Yurou had left spread out before her. She hadn't wanted to use this evidence, because using it meant dragging Su Yurou back into the whirlpool. But Su Yurou was already gone, no longer in the whirlpool—and Gu Changfeng still was.
She picked up her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "I'm going to make public the evidence Su Yurou left. What do you think?"
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "Make it public. But not under your own name. Have Zhou Wanyin release it in the studio's name—it carries more credibility than if you do it yourself."
Su Qingyuan replied: "All right."
She organized the key contents from the drive—the chat records of Gu Changfeng telling Su Yurou to write the open letter, the open-letter template, and the letter Su Yurou had written just before leaving. She didn't make all the chat records public, only the most essential pages, enough to prove Gu Changfeng had orchestrated everything from behind the scenes, but without exposing more of Su Yurou's privacy. Then she called Zhou Wanyin and laid the matter out in brief.
When Zhou Wanyin had heard her out, she was quiet for a few seconds. "Qingyuan, are you sure you want to make this letter public? When Su Yurou wrote it, she probably never imagined you'd release it."
"She knew when she wrote it." Su Qingyuan's voice was very calm. "When she left the letter for me, she knew how I'd use it. This isn't a gift she gave me—it's the innocence she gave back to herself. Her innocence may not be worth much, but she wanted to do one right thing at the end."
Zhou Wanyin fell silent again for a few seconds, then said, "All right. I'll release it."
An hour later, the official account of Zhou Wanyin's studio published a long post. The headline read: "A Complete Response Regarding the Rumors Spread About Ms. Su Qingyuan." The article carried no excess sentiment, no emotional accusations—it simply laid out the evidence one piece at a time: screenshots of the chat records between Gu Changfeng and Su Yurou, the open-letter template, and Su Yurou's handwritten letter from just before she left.
The final paragraph read: "All of the above evidence has been submitted to and notarized for the record. Against any individual or organization that continues to fabricate or spread rumors, we reserve the right to pursue legal liability. Ms. Su Qingyuan's talent needs no one's endorsement. Every one of her works was drawn by her own hand, stroke by stroke, and ground out by her own hand, cut by cut. Anyone who doubts her is welcome to go and see the actual pieces from the 'First Light' collection, the 'Light Traces' collection, the 'Roots' series—and judge for themselves whether that craftsmanship is something 'connections' can produce."
In the first hour after the long post went out, public opinion began to loosen. Someone dug up the detailed craftsmanship images from the "First Light" collection and analyzed the layered halo of the Ripple Cut frame by frame, reaching a conclusion: this level of craftsmanship was not something connections could manufacture. By the second hour, Cartier's official account reposted Zhou Wanyin's long post with the caption: "Cartier's collaboration with Ms. Su Qingyuan is based on her design work. There is no other reason." By the third hour, the hashtag #BelieveInSuQingyuan# shot to the top of the trending list.
The comment section turned completely around.
"The chat records are right there—and people are still saying she relied on connections?"
"Who is Gu Changfeng? Why does he want to ruin Su Qingyuan?"
"Chairman of Dingfeng Group. Lu Group's old rival. This is a corporate war, and Su Qingyuan got dragged into it through no fault of her own."
"That letter Su Yurou wrote before she left made me cry. She did so many wrong things, and in the end she finally did one thing right."
"No matter what Su Yurou did in the past, this time she stepped up and told the truth. That deserves some respect."
Su Qingyuan looked at the comments. She didn't cry, didn't smile—she only felt a strange sort of calm settle over her heart. As if a storm that had raged for a very long time had finally stopped. Not because she had won, but because the rain had run its course, and the sky was due to clear.
The studio door was pushed open. Lu Shiyan walked in—no insulated bag in his hands, no flowers, just empty-handed. He crossed to her, looked down at her, was silent for two seconds, then reached out and pulled her into his arms, holding her tight.
Su Qingyuan buried her face against his chest, breathing in the clean, cool scent of him, and felt a sudden heat rise behind her eyes. She didn't cry—she only closed her eyes and listened to his heartbeat. One beat, two, three. Each one steady and strong, like a kind of promise.
"Gu Changfeng has lost this round—completely." Lu Shiyan's voice came from above her head, low and even. "Not in the bid. In the court of public opinion. The moment the open-letter business broke, his Dingfeng Group's stock dropped six points today. The board is already discussing whether to let him stay on as chairman."
Su Qingyuan lifted her head from his arms and looked into his eyes. "Will he step down?"
"Not necessarily. But the next time he wants to come after you, it won't be so easy." Lu Shiyan reached out and gently wiped away the dampness that had welled up at the corner of her eye at some point. "Because everyone knows now what kind of man he is."
Su Qingyuan curved her lips, drew back out of his arms, walked to the window, and pushed it open. Cold air rushed in, sweeping away the heavy mood inside the studio. The sky outside had cleared, sunlight peeking out from behind the clouds, falling on the bare osmanthus tree, on the cyclamen on the windowsill, on her face.
She drew a deep breath and let it out.
"Lu Shiyan, I want hot pot."
"Okay."
"The spicy kind."
"Okay."
"The really, really spicy kind."
"Okay." Lu Shiyan watched her profile lit by the sun, the corner of his mouth lifting slightly. "Are you crying?"
"No."
"Then what are you wiping at?"
"The sun's too bright."
Lu Shiyan didn't call her bluff. He walked over and stood beside her, looking out the window too. The two of them stood side by side at the window, the sunlight falling over them both, casting their shadows on the floor—close together, close enough to almost overlap.
Winter in Nancheng was still going on. But Su Qingyuan knew the coldest part had already passed. Not because Gu Changfeng had lost, but because she had won—won the battle inside her own heart. Before, she'd been afraid of being doubted, afraid of being dismissed, afraid that all her effort could be wiped away by a single line: "She relied on connections." Now she wasn't afraid anymore. Because her work was her evidence, her name was her brand. Whatever anyone said couldn't change that fact.
The hot pot place was the same Hunan restaurant as last time. When the pot of bubbling red-oil broth was set down, Su Qingyuan's eyes were even brighter than the last time.
"You look very happy," Lu Shiyan said, pouring her a glass of ice water.
"Mm." Su Qingyuan picked up a piece of tripe, dipped it up and down seven or eight times, and put it in her mouth, hissing at the heat but unwilling to spit it out. "Happy."
Lu Shiyan watched her lips, flushed red from the spice, his gaze as soft as moonlight. "There won't be anything like this again."
"Anything like what?"
"Someone doubting you."
Su Qingyuan set down her chopsticks and looked at him. "Lu Shiyan, when you say that, you sound exactly like you're making a promise."
"It is a promise."
The earnestness on his face left her a little flustered. She lowered her head and went on eating. As she ate, something suddenly came to mind. "Oh, right—did Su Yurou make it to Australia?"
"She did. Landed in Melbourne last night." Lu Shiyan's tone was very flat. "She posted to her Moments, said 'the sunshine in Melbourne is lovely.' Visible to one person only."
Su Qingyuan paused. "To whom?"
"You."
Su Qingyuan fell silent. When Su Yurou posted that, she probably hadn't known whether Su Qingyuan would even be able to see it. But she'd posted it anyway, as if to confirm—did you get my letter? Did you see it? You know now, don't you? You don't have to forgive me. I just wanted you to know I finally did one thing right.
Su Qingyuan didn't reply to that Moments post, and didn't mention it to anyone. She only opened Su Yurou's WeChat profile picture and glanced at it once—the avatar had been changed to a photo of a Melbourne beach, blue sky and emerald sea, the sunshine lovely. She switched off her phone and went back to eating her hot pot.
The next day, Anna from Cartier called. "Miss Su, regarding the global launch of the 'Light Traces' collection, headquarters has a proposal."
"What proposal?"
"They'd like you to attend the launch wearing a piece of jewelry you designed yourself—not a Cartier piece, but your own work. They said the 'Light Traces' collection is your light, and Cartier is only the vessel that carries the light."
Su Qingyuan gripped her phone, a scalding emotion welling up inside her. For a century-old brand like Cartier to be willing to let an independent designer wear her own work at its flagship store's global launch—that was no longer just a "collaboration." It was respect. It was placing her on equal footing—not "the brand and the partner designer," but "you and us."
"Anna, thank you."
"Thank me for what? You earned it yourself." Anna laughed. "Oh, and that Mr. Lu of yours—he'll be coming to Paris with you, won't he?"
Su Qingyuan was taken aback. "How did you know?"
"Last time he sent someone to Cartier headquarters to discuss the supply chain, he mentioned it in passing. He said if the 'Light Traces' launch was arranged in Paris, he'd come with you. He hoped we could help arrange the itinerary."
Su Qingyuan held the phone, the tips of her ears flushing faintly red. This man—without her even knowing—had already arranged every detail, from the supply chain to the itinerary, from the coat to the transportation in Paris. Not one thing he did was grand, but strung together they formed an entire dense map, written all over with the words "I'm here."
After hanging up, Su Qingyuan sent Lu Shiyan a message: "Anna said when you went to Cartier headquarters to discuss the supply chain, you mentioned the Paris business in passing."
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "Mm. In passing."
Su Qingyuan looked at the words "in passing" and smiled. She slipped the phone into her pocket, picked up her carving knife, and went back to work. The sunlight outside was lovely, falling on the workbench, on her hand, on the gem taking shape beneath her tools. All at once she remembered a rainy night long ago, when she had sat on the floor of a hotel room, staring at her empty bank account, certain her whole world had ended.
What she hadn't known then was that the rain that night was the end of the storm—and also the beginning of dawn.
[System: This chapter—Su Yurou is gone, leaving the evidence behind. Gu Changfeng's hit piece ran, and was killed by the evidence in turn. The heroine's "Roots and Light" joint exhibition and the "Light Traces" launch are both moving forward. Gu Changfeng's stock dropped, the board is questioning him—the blow on the corporate-war front has landed. Sweetness on the sly—hot pot, a promise, the Paris itinerary, and once again "in passing." Su Yurou's arc is concluded for now. Next chapter: Paris, the launch, the aurora. The System is on standby.]

Chapter 31 — Before the Journey, the Dust Settles
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626233.html

On the third day after the smear-campaign affair, Dingfeng Group released a short announcement: Gu Changfeng, citing "personal health reasons," was stepping down as chairman of Dingfeng Group, with the vice-chairman serving in the interim. The wording was dignified—no tearing, no scandal, like a carefully rehearsed curtain call.
But everyone knew what those four words, "personal health reasons," were hiding. When Lu Shiyan told Su Qingyuan the news over the phone, his voice was very calm—no victor's glee, no contempt for the loser. He simply stated a fact, and then added a single sentence: "He won't lay a hand on you again."
Su Qingyuan held her phone, standing at the studio window, looking out at the gray, overcast sky. Gu Changfeng had resigned. Not because of her—because of himself. He'd backed the wrong person, used the wrong methods, played the wrong cards at the wrong time. Every wrong thing stacked on top of another until it became the final straw that broke him.
She didn't pity him. Nor did she pity Su Yurou. She only felt that the world was, after all, quite fair—you reap what you sow. It's not that retribution never comes; it just isn't time yet. And when the time comes, no one can dodge it.
After hanging up, Su Qingyuan walked back to her workbench and picked up her sketchbook. Her hand was steady, the tip of her pen meeting the paper, the lines flowing and sure. She was drawing the fifth piece of the "Roots" series—a necklace. The centerpiece was an irregular pearl, shaped like a teardrop, but its luster wasn't a cold white; it was a warm white, as if a beam of light were hidden inside the tear.
She gave the piece a name: "Healing."
Wounds heal. They don't vanish; new flesh grows in and covers the old injury. The color of the new flesh isn't the same as the original skin—it's a little paler, a little more tender—but it's a part of you, proof that you survived.
After the final stroke, Su Qingyuan leaned back in her chair and let out a long breath. The sky outside was still gray, but the sun was about to come out.
That evening, when Lu Shiyan came to pick her up, he brought a suitcase.
Su Qingyuan looked at the silver-gray carry-on in his hand and froze for a moment. "You're going on a business trip?"
"Not a business trip." Lu Shiyan set the suitcase down at the studio door and walked in. "I'm getting the Paris things ready ahead of time. I'm afraid we'll run out of time later."
Su Qingyuan looked at the case, then at him, and couldn't help laughing. "There are still over ten days until we leave, and you're already packing?"
"I'm not packing luggage." Lu Shiyan came to stand before her, looking down at her. "I'm packing my mood."
The line made Su Qingyuan's heart skip a little faster. "What mood?"
"The can't-wait mood." Lu Shiyan reached out and tucked a stray strand of hair behind her ear. "Ten-some days is too long."
Su Qingyuan's face went red. She turned her face toward the window, pretending to study the color of the sky outside. "When did you get so sappy?"
"Since the day you said, 'You're better-looking than the night view.'"
Su Qingyuan's ears went red too. She remembered that night on the mountaintop—she really had said those words. It was the sappiest thing she'd said in her whole life, and she'd assumed he'd long forgotten it. She hadn't expected him to remember, and to remember it so clearly.
"Lu Shiyan, with a memory this good, it's a shame you don't go on Super Brain."
"My memory only works for you." Lu Shiyan's tone was serious, not at all like he was joking.
At last Su Qingyuan couldn't hold it in and burst out laughing. She reached over and gave him a light punch. "Hurry up and move your suitcase—it's blocking the doorway."
Lu Shiyan curved his lips, turned, and carried the case inside, setting it in a corner of the lounge area. The silver-gray suitcase rested there quietly, like a silent promise—I'll go with you.
Su Qingyuan glanced at the case, warmth spreading in her chest. She turned back to her workbench and went on polishing the samples for the "Roots" series. Lu Shiyan sat down on the sofa, opened his laptop, and dealt with documents. The two of them each busied themselves, occasionally looking up to meet each other's eyes, smiling, then bowing their heads again to continue.
This kind of understanding needed no words.
Over the next ten-some days, Su Qingyuan entered "Paris countdown" mode.
All five pieces of the "Light Traces" series were finished and already crated and shipped to Paris. Every day she received on-site photos from Anna—display cases in place, lighting adjustments complete, every detail being prepared for her work. The "Roots" series was also ahead of schedule: three of the five pieces were done, the fourth was in the finishing stage, and the fifth was still being polished. On Qu Ya's end, all the photos for the joint exhibition had been shot, and she'd moved into post-production.
Everything was proceeding according to plan. But there was always a string in Su Qingyuan's heart, pulled faintly taut. Not nerves—reluctance. The Paris trip would be ten-some days, the first time she'd be away this long since getting together with Lu Shiyan. Even though he'd be going with her, being away from the studio for so long left her feeling a little hollow inside.
Three days before departure, Zhou Wanyin came to the studio to drop off promotional materials for the joint exhibition. She sat on the lounge sofa, leafing through Qu Ya's draft of the exhibition catalog, studying it carefully page by page.
"Qingyuan, come take a look." Zhou Wanyin pointed to a page in the middle of the catalog.
Su Qingyuan walked over, looked down, and froze. On that page, the left side held Qu Ya's photograph—the roots of a great tree, thick roots driving deep into the earth, like an old man's hands, like the veins of time. On the right was the first piece of her "Roots" series—the tree-ring brooch. The photo and the jewelry placed side by side, one a creation of nature, one a work of human hands, yet the theme, the emotion, even the composition were astonishingly alike.
"Qu Ya said that when she chose this photo, she hadn't seen your brooch yet. It was only after she placed them together that she realized they looked like they were in conversation." There was a suppressed excitement in Zhou Wanyin's voice. "Qingyuan, this joint exhibition is going to take off."
Su Qingyuan looked at the two side-by-side images in the catalog, and a scalding emotion welled up inside her. What Qu Ya had photographed were the roots of nature; what she had made were her own roots. One turned outward, one turned inward, but both arrived at the same place—the place of origin.
"Sister Wanyin, could I have a copy of this catalog? I want to bring it to Paris."
"Of course. It was always meant for you." Zhou Wanyin closed the catalog and handed it to her. "When you get to Paris, rest well—don't just bury yourself in work. You've lost weight lately."
Su Qingyuan took the catalog and hugged it to her chest. "I know."
Zhou Wanyin looked at her and suddenly smiled. "When you go to Paris with your Mr. Lu, are you going to shoot your wedding photos while you're at it?"
Su Qingyuan's face went red in an instant. "Sister Wanyin!"
"I'm joking. But really, the two of you ought to settle things. That Lu Shiyan—he never says anything, but I can tell, he's been waiting a long time for this day."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head, fingers gently brushing the cover of the catalog. Of course she knew he'd been waiting. From the glass door to the rainy night, from the hotel lobby to the mountaintop, from Paris to Nancheng, he'd been waiting all along. Waiting for her to be sure, waiting for her to be ready, waiting for her to turn back and see—he'd been there the whole time.
The day before departure, Su Qingyuan rarely gave herself a day off.
She didn't go to the studio; she slept until she woke naturally. By the time she woke it was already past nine, sunlight slipping in through the gap in the curtains and falling golden on the quilt. She lay in bed, staring at the ceiling, lost in thought for a while. There were a lot of things turning in her mind—tomorrow's flight, the weather in Paris, the schedule on launch day, the exhibition plan Qu Ya had sent over. After a while, she suddenly remembered something.
She picked up her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "What time is the flight tomorrow?"
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "Ten in the morning. I'll come get you at eight."
Su Qingyuan: "Okay. Have you finished packing?"
Lu Shiyan: "All packed. Two cases."
Su Qingyuan: "Two? What did you bring that needs two cases?"
Lu Shiyan: "One mine, one yours."
Su Qingyuan froze. She hadn't even gotten around to packing yet, and he'd already prepared her case too? She was about to ask when his next message came: "Don't bring too many clothes, you can buy them in Paris. But I packed your carving knives and sketchbook for you—they're in the carry-on bag, won't be checked."
Su Qingyuan gripped her phone, and her nose suddenly stung. Her carving knives and sketchbook were the things she cared about most. She'd planned to pack them herself, afraid they'd get lost in checked baggage, wanting to carry them with her. Before she could even say a word, he'd already thought of it for her—and done it.
Su Qingyuan: "Lu Shiyan, did you set the combination lock on my suitcase too?"
Lu Shiyan: "Your birthday."
Su Qingyuan stared at the words "your birthday," and her eyes grew hot. Her birthday—even she herself rarely marked it. Back in the Su household, no one remembered her birthday. After she'd struck out on her own, she'd never gotten into the habit of celebrating it. She'd assumed no one would ever care. But he remembered. Not only did he remember, he'd set it as the combination to her suitcase.
Su Qingyuan pressed her phone to her chest, gazed up at the ceiling, and smiled.
At noon, Su Qingyuan went to the studio. Not to work—to say goodbye.
The studio was very quiet, the heating already turned off, and the room felt a little cold. The cyclamen on the windowsill was still in bloom, its pink-white petals trembling faintly in the chilly air. She watered the flowers, tidied the tools on the workbench one by one, and draped them with a dust cloth. Then she stood at the door and took one last look at the whole space.
Eighty square meters—not large. But this place held the lifeblood of countless days and nights of hers. "First Light" had been born here, "Light Traces" had taken shape here, "Roots" had taken root here. She picked up her phone, snapped a photo, and saved it into the album called "Roots."
As she locked the door, she suddenly remembered the day she'd first come to see this studio. That day the osmanthus had been in bloom, and Lu Shiyan had stood beneath the osmanthus tree waiting for her, a key in his hand. He'd said, "The key's here, you can come look anytime." When she took the key, her fingers had brushed his fingertips, and her heart had skipped a beat. Back then she'd thought he was just being kind, helping her find a studio. Only later did she learn that for the sake of this one studio, he'd seized control of the entire creative park.
She locked the door and slipped the key into her pocket.
After Paris, she would come back. She would return here with new work, new stories, a new self.
That evening, Lu Shiyan came to take her to dinner. He didn't take her to a restaurant—he drove straight to his own home.
Su Qingyuan had been there once—a long time ago, before they'd defined their relationship, she'd come to drop off a document. Back then she'd stood at the door, too shy to go in. Today, Lu Shiyan drove the car straight into the garage, then took her hand and rode the elevator up to the top floor.
The instant the door opened, Su Qingyuan froze.
This wasn't the kind of "bachelor's mansion" she'd pictured—cold, lifeless, like a show home. This place had warmth. The living-room lights were a warm yellow, a pale beige throw draped over the sofa—exactly like the one in her studio's lounge area. On the coffee table sat a bunch of white chamomile, and beside it an open book, looking as if someone had just been reading it. On the kitchen counter stood two cups, one large and one small, as if they were used every day.
"This is your home?" Su Qingyuan asked.
"Mm." Lu Shiyan set his car keys in the tray by the entryway. "I live alone, it's a bit big. But I'm used to it."
Su Qingyuan changed into slippers—they were her size, pale gray, soft and fuzzy. She walked into the living room and sat down on the sofa. The sofa was very soft, far more comfortable than the one in her studio. She sank back into it, hugging that pale beige throw, and suddenly felt that this place didn't feel like a "bachelor's mansion" at all—it felt like a home that had been waiting a long time for her.
Lu Shiyan brought two glasses of water out from the kitchen, set them on the coffee table, and sat down beside her.
"The reason I asked you over today is that I wanted you to see it, so that when you come in the future, you won't feel like a stranger here."
Su Qingyuan turned her head to look at him. "Will I be coming often in the future?"
"Come whenever you want. If you don't want to come, I'll go to you." Lu Shiyan's voice was very soft. "But here—you're welcome anytime."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head, her fingers slowly tracing circles in the fuzz of the throw. She understood. He was telling her—my home is your home. Not a courtesy, not romance, but a very earnest invitation about "the future."
"Lu Shiyan, when you go to Paris tomorrow, are you going with me, or going 'while you're at it'?"
The corner of Lu Shiyan's mouth lifted slightly. "Going specifically."
Su Qingyuan laughed. She set down the throw, stood up, and walked to the floor-to-ceiling window. Outside lay Nancheng's night view, the lights of ten thousand homes merging into a warm sea of light. She saw a very low building in the distance, looking especially quiet amid the lights.
"What's that?" She pointed in that direction.
Lu Shiyan came over and stood beside her. "The creative park. Your studio is in that direction."
Su Qingyuan looked at that blurred patch of lights, and a strange feeling welled up inside her. Standing at the floor-to-ceiling window of his home, she could see her own studio. This building stood at one end of the city, the studio at the other, half a city between them. But standing here, they seemed very close—because she was standing at his side.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"When you lived here alone, did you stand here at night looking out the window?"
"I did."
"Looking at what?"
"Looking at the whole city. Wondering when there'd be one more person here."
Su Qingyuan tilted her head to look at him. The light from outside fell on his face, his profile especially gentle in the night. "Now there is."
Lu Shiyan turned to look at her, his gaze as deep as the night. "Mm. There is."
The two of them looked at each other for a few seconds, and then Su Qingyuan rose onto her tiptoes and pressed a very, very light kiss to his lips.
"See you tomorrow."
"See you tomorrow."
Su Qingyuan turned, picked up her bag, and went to the door to change her shoes. Lu Shiyan saw her to the elevator. When the elevator doors opened, she stepped in, turned around, and looked at him.
"Lu Shiyan, your home is beautiful."
"It'll be even more beautiful in the future."
"Why?"
"Because it'll have your things in it."
The elevator doors closed. Su Qingyuan leaned against the elevator wall, looked at her own flushed face in the mirror, and smiled. This man—when he said sweet nothings, he never seemed to think they were sweet nothings. What he was talking about was the future. A future the two of them would share.
The next morning, Lu Shiyan showed up on time downstairs at Su Qingyuan's apartment.
When Su Qingyuan came down, she saw his car parked at the entrance, the trunk open, and him loading the suitcases inside. Two cases, one black and one white, leaning against each other like a pair.
She walked over and looked at the white case. "Is this mine?"
"Mm. The combination's your birthday. I've already packed it for you—take a look and see if anything's missing." Lu Shiyan shut the trunk and pulled open the car door.
Su Qingyuan got into the passenger seat and fastened her seatbelt. "If you packed it for me, nothing will be missing."
Lu Shiyan started the car, the corner of his mouth lifting slightly.
The car drove toward the airport. Nancheng's winter was still very cold, but the sunlight was lovely, falling on the windshield, dazzlingly bright. Su Qingyuan leaned back in her seat, watching the streetscape rush backward outside the window. The creative park, the Cartier building, the Hunan restaurant, the hotpot place, the art museum, Qu Ya's studio, the floor-to-ceiling window of his home—these were all part of her map of Nancheng. Every one of them held his shadow.
"Lu Shiyan, when we get to Paris, should we go check out the Cartier flagship first?"
"Sure."
"Then the Eiffel Tower?"
"Sure."
"Then go eat croissants?"
"Sure."
"Why do you say sure to everything?"
Lu Shiyan turned his head to glance at her, a smile in the depths of his eyes. "Because whatever you say is fine by me."
Su Qingyuan turned her face toward the car window, pretending to look at the scenery outside. Her face was reflected in the glass, the curve of her lips refusing to come down no matter what.
The airport, the departure lounge.
Su Qingyuan sat by the floor-to-ceiling window, watching the planes on the tarmac. Lu Shiyan had gone to check the luggage, and she sat there alone, holding two cups of coffee—an Americano for herself, a latte for him.
She looked down at the names on the coffee cups and suddenly remembered a time long ago, when she couldn't even bring herself to buy a single cup of coffee. Back then she'd worked at the Su family's shop, walking a long way every day, passing a café and slowing her steps when she caught the scent of coffee. She'd never once gone in, because the price of one cup of coffee was enough to feed her two meals.
Now she could buy many cups of coffee. But the one she most wanted to buy was the one in the hand of the person beside her.
Lu Shiyan came back and sat down beside her. Su Qingyuan handed him the latte. "Yours."
Lu Shiyan took it and drank a sip. "Thanks."
The two of them sat shoulder to shoulder, watching the planes beyond the window. Sunlight poured in through the enormous glass curtain wall, falling over them, warm and bright.
"Su Qingyuan."
"Mm."
"When we get to Paris, besides the launch and the Tower, there's one more place I want to take you."
"Where?"
"Montmartre."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "Why Montmartre?"
Lu Shiyan looked out the window, his voice very soft. "Because there's a wall there, called the Wall of Love. The same sentence is written on it in over three hundred languages—'I love you.' I want to take you to see it."
Su Qingyuan's heart skipped a beat.
"Which line do you want to see?"
Lu Shiyan turned to look at her, his gaze as deep as the night. "The Chinese one."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot. She lowered her head and took a sip of the Americano, using the bitterness of the coffee to press down whatever was welling up in her throat. "Lu Shiyan, you really do know how to pick a place."
"It's not that I know how to pick it—it's that that place was always meant to be visited with you."
Su Qingyuan said nothing. She reached out and clasped his hand. Ten fingers laced together, palm against palm. Outside the window, the planes' engines roared and rumbled. The sunlight grew brighter and brighter, lighting the entire departure lounge like a vast, transparent greenhouse.
The announcement came: "Passengers traveling to Paris, please prepare to board."
Su Qingyuan stood up and picked up her bag. Lu Shiyan stood up and gathered both of their coats.
"Let's go."
"Let's go."
They walked shoulder to shoulder toward the boarding gate, Nancheng's sunlight behind them, the unknown of Paris ahead.
The two of them's shadows fell on the floor, drawn close together, so close they almost overlapped.
【System: This chapter—the preparations before the Paris trip. Gu Changfeng resigns, Su Yurou heads to Australia, the female lead bids farewell to her studio, a visit to Lu Shiyan's home, the "Wall of Love" promise at the airport. The Gu Changfeng arc wraps up for now, the Su Yurou arc wraps up, the romance line heats up. Next chapter, Paris. The launch. The Wall of Love. The aurora? No, the aurora has to wait. But Paris has something brighter than the aurora—her light at 13 Rue de la Paix. The System is staking out the next chapter.】

========================================

Chapter 32 — Paris, the Wall of Love, and Light
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626234.html

Paris, the Wall of Love, and Light
As the plane sliced through the clouds, Su Qingyuan fell asleep against Lu Shiyan's shoulder.
She had a very long dream. In it, she was still a little girl, sitting on the attic floor of the old Su family house, a stub of pencil in her hand, drawing on a crumpled sheet of paper. Outside the window was Nancheng's overcast grey sky; downstairs was the voice of her foster mother, Liu Xiulan, nagging at her to cook. She didn't answer. She kept drawing. She was drawing light—the thin, golden light that filtered out from behind the clouds.
That image lasted a long time in the dream. Then it changed abruptly. She was standing before an enormous glass door; inside was a magnificent banquet hall, outside an empty corridor. Through the glass, she saw someone standing inside. He wore a black suit, and he was looking at her. She wanted to see his face clearly, but a film of mist coated the glass, and no matter how she tried, she couldn't make him out.
Then she woke.
The plane was descending. Beyond the cabin window lay the Paris night, ten thousand lights glittering like a golden sea. Lu Shiyan was still holding the exact pose he'd been in when she fell asleep—motionless, afraid of waking her.
"You're awake?" He looked down at her.
"Mm." Su Qingyuan rubbed her eyes and straightened up off his shoulder. "How long did I sleep?"
"Three hours."
"You didn't move the whole time?"
"Didn't want to wake you."
Su Qingyuan looked at his shoulder, creased from where she'd leaned, and something inside her softened. "You could have woken me."
"Couldn't bear to."
Just three words. Su Qingyuan's nose stung. She turned her face toward the window, pretending to watch the Paris night below. The lights drew closer and closer, like countless golden stars scattered across the ground. She suddenly remembered a photograph of Paris she'd seen in a magazine when she was very small—the Eiffel Tower, the Seine, the Louvre. Back then she'd thought Paris was another world, somewhere she could never go in this lifetime. Now she was here. And he was beside her.
The plane landed, taxied, and came to a stop. Su Qingyuan unbuckled her seatbelt, stood, and took her carry-on down from the overhead bin. Inside it were her sketchbook and her carving knives—Lu Shiyan had packed them, wrapped in soft cloth and tucked into the innermost layer of the bag so they wouldn't get knocked about.
"Let's go." Lu Shiyan held out his hand.
Su Qingyuan placed her hand in his palm and let him close his fingers around it. The two of them stepped out of the cabin door, and the night wind rushed to meet them. Paris in winter was colder than Nancheng; the air was dry, carrying a crisp, indescribable scent.
"Cold?" Lu Shiyan unwound his scarf and wrapped it around her neck.
"I'm fine." Su Qingyuan buried her face in the scarf and breathed in the familiar scent of him.
In the airport hall, the staff Cartier had arranged were already waiting. A Frenchman in his thirties, dressed in a navy-blue uniform, held up a sign with her name on it. Seeing them emerge, he came forward with a smile.
"Miss Su? Mr. Lu? I'm Jean-Philippe, from Cartier's concierge service. The car is ready. Please, this way."
A black executive car was parked at the airport exit. Su Qingyuan climbed in and leaned back against the seat, watching the streets of Paris stream past the window. The late-night city was quiet and gentle, the streetlamps casting a warm amber glow over the ancient buildings, each like a painting.
The car stopped in front of a hotel. Su Qingyuan got out and looked up—not one of those gilded, glittering luxury hotels, but a small boutique tucked away in a side street. The frontage was modest, but every detail breathed a restrained French elegance.
"This is the hotel Cartier arranged for you, Miss Su." Jean-Philippe opened the car door. "Your rooms are ready. Tomorrow morning at ten, Madame Anna will come to collect you and take you to the flagship store."
"Thank you." Su Qingyuan took the keycard and wheeled her suitcase into the lobby.
The room was on the fourth floor—not large, but very comfortable. Beyond the floor-to-ceiling window stretched the Paris skyline; in the distance she could see the tip of the Eiffel Tower, glinting gold against the night. Su Qingyuan stood at the window for a while, then turned and found Lu Shiyan standing behind her.
"Where's your room?" she asked.
"Next door."
Su Qingyuan glanced at him and smiled. "You booked it?"
"Mm."
"On purpose?"
"Mm."
Su Qingyuan reached out and gave him a light punch. "Why didn't you just book one room?"
Lu Shiyan caught the fist she'd swung at him and looked down at her. "Because I know you're not ready yet."
Su Qingyuan's heart skipped a beat. This man—he always left the choice to her. He would never say, "Let's stay together." He would never say, "We're together anyway." He would wait—wait until she was ready, wait until she said "yes" on her own.
"Lu Shiyan, you really are far too patient."
"With you, always."
Su Qingyuan turned her face away and looked out at the Eiffel Tower. "Tomorrow evening, shall we go up to Montmartre?"
"All right."
"See the Wall of Love, then go eat croissants?"
"All right."
Su Qingyuan turned back to look at him. "Why do you say yes to everything?"
"Because whatever you say is fine by me."
Su Qingyuan laughed, rose onto her toes, and kissed him on the lips. "Good night."
"Good night."
Lu Shiyan turned and walked out of the room, drawing the door softly shut behind him. Su Qingyuan leaned against the door panel, listening to his footsteps recede down the corridor, then the door of the next room opened and closed again. She curled her lips into a smile, walked to the bed, and let herself fall, flinging herself into the soft covers.
Paris. She and he, in Paris. Tomorrow, her work would debut at the Cartier flagship store. The day after, they would go up to Montmartre and see the wall covered in "I love you." She rolled over, pulled the covers up to her chin, and closed her eyes.
Outside the window, the lights of the Eiffel Tower blinked on and off, as if waving hello to her.
The next day, Su Qingyuan woke at six. Not jolted awake—she simply woke on her own. She lay in bed watching the morning light play across the ceiling, drifting for a moment, then got up, washed, and dressed. She wore a white turtleneck sweater, black wide-leg trousers, and over them the smoke-grey cashmere coat Lu Shiyan had given her. She didn't pin her hair up; she let it fall loose over her shoulders, and put on only a single pair of stud earrings—samples from the "First Snow" collection, her own work.
The woman in the mirror was clean, sharp, understated, yet every detail was her own choice. She smiled at her reflection, picked up her bag, and stepped out.
In the corridor, Lu Shiyan was already waiting. Today he wore a dark grey coat over a black turtleneck; he looked like he'd stepped out of a magazine. Seeing Su Qingyuan, he gave her a once-over, his gaze lingering for an instant on her earlobes. "Wearing your own work?"
"Mm. 'First Snow.'"
"Beautiful."
"Do you mean the earrings, or me?"
"Both."
Su Qingyuan laughed and slipped her arm through his. "Come on, Anna's probably getting impatient."
Down in the hotel lobby, Anna was already waiting. Today she wore a red wool dress, her presence at full force. Seeing Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan walk out side by side, she arched a brow. "Miss Su, you don't look like a designer today."
"Like what, then?"
"Like someone about to conquer Paris."
Su Qingyuan was a little embarrassed by the words, but she didn't deny them. Because it was the truth—today, she really was going to conquer Paris. Not through connections, not through luck, but through her work.
The Cartier flagship store, 13 Rue de la Paix.
Su Qingyuan stood at the entrance, looking up at that black wrought-iron door, her heart pounding like a drum. For more than a century, this door had welcomed countless royals, celebrities, and artists—but it had never once welcomed the work of a Chinese independent designer. Today was the first time.
"Nervous?" Lu Shiyan stood behind her, his voice low.
"A little."
"No need." He reached out and gently took her hand. "Your work belongs here."
Su Qingyuan drew a deep breath and pushed the door open.
The exhibition hall was on the second floor. As Su Qingyuan walked up, the first thing she saw was light. Not a harsh, dazzling light, but a soft, warm one—like the glow of the sky at daybreak. Anna said this was a color temperature Cartier had calibrated specifically for the "Light Traces" collection, so that every piece would be shown in its most perfect state.
Five pieces were displayed in five separate glass cases, each lit until it seemed to glow from within. Su Qingyuan walked slowly past, looking at them one by one. The first was the ring—a diamond of the palest pink, the hairline groove on the inner band casting a faint shadow under the light. The second was the necklace, its white diamonds set half-suspended in midair, as if they might drop at any moment. The third was the pair of drop earrings, two teardrop-shaped morganites, wholeness and brokenness set side by side. The fourth was a brooch, its theme "light trajectory"—platinum shaped into the path of a beam of light, setting out from a single tiny diamond and winding forward, like a road without end. The fifth was a bracelet, its chain made with a special weaving technique, like fibers of light twisted together strand by strand.
Su Qingyuan stood before the fifth piece, looking at the bracelet she had polished over countless days and nights, her eyes growing a little hot. But she didn't cry. Today wasn't a day for crying. Today was a day for smiling.
"Miss Su." Anna came over, two glasses of champagne in her hands. "The people from headquarters have arrived. They'd like to meet you."
Su Qingyuan took the champagne and turned. At the top of the stairs stood three people—two men and a woman, all around fifty, dressed with care, elegant in bearing. The man in the middle—Su Qingyuan recognized him—was Jean-Pierre, Cartier's creative director, the one who had said over the phone, "There's still hope for this industry."
Jean-Pierre walked over, smiling, and extended his hand. "Miss Su, at last we meet."
Su Qingyuan took his hand. "Jean-Pierre, thank you."
"Thank me for what? It was your work that made me say those words." Jean-Pierre let go and looked toward the pieces in their cases. "The 'Light Traces' collection is the first guest collection in my thirty years at Cartier that made me feel this brand still has a future."
The words scorched her. "Jean-Pierre, you're exaggerating."
"I'm not." Jean-Pierre looked at her seriously. "Do you know the greatest difference between 'Light Traces' and Cartier's past guest collections?"
"What?"
"In the past, the guest collections were designers imitating Cartier. 'Light Traces' is Cartier drawing closer to you." Jean-Pierre paused. "From today on, it isn't Cartier that has chosen you. It's you who have chosen Cartier."
The hand holding her champagne glass trembled faintly. Not from nerves, but from being understood. When she made "Light Traces," she had never thought, I want to make a Cartier-style piece. What she'd thought was, I want to make something only Su Qingyuan could make. And she had. Cartier had seen it. Jean-Pierre had seen it.
"Thank you, Jean-Pierre. I'll remember these words for the rest of my life."
"The rest of your life is too long." Jean-Pierre laughed. "Remembering them until our next collaboration is enough."
Su Qingyuan laughed too.
The launch event lasted two hours. Not many people came—they were Cartier's hand-picked guests: a few members of European royalty, top collectors, the most influential critics in the jewelry industry. Each piece had a cluster of people around it, discussing in low voices, peering through loupes, occasionally letting out a gasp of admiration.
Su Qingyuan stood in a corner, watching those people gather before her work, and felt something very strange—unreal. Those people were names she had once been able to see only in magazines. Now they stood before her work, examining through loupes the details she had polished with her own hands.
"What are you thinking about?" Lu Shiyan had come to her side without her noticing.
"I'm thinking—if this is a dream, I don't want to wake up."
Lu Shiyan reached out and gently pinched the back of her hand. "Did that hurt?"
"No."
"Then it's not a dream."
Su Qingyuan laughed and leaned against his shoulder, looking at the case in the distance with the crowd around it. Inside was that half-suspended necklace; under the light, the white diamonds looked like a frozen star. "Lu Shiyan, do you think I'll ever make something better than this?"
"Yes."
"How do you know?"
"Because you've never once stopped moving forward."
Su Qingyuan straightened off his shoulder and looked into his eyes. The light in them was brighter than the lights in the hall. She rose onto her toes and whispered into his ear: "Thank you, for bringing me here."
Lu Shiyan reached up and gently ruffled her hair. "I didn't bring you here. You walked here yourself. I just watched from the side."
After the launch, Anna suggested dinner at a restaurant by the Seine. Su Qingyuan agreed. Today she didn't want to be alone; she wanted to be among people, to talk, to laugh, to celebrate. Because she deserved it.
The restaurant was on the Left Bank of the Seine, small, its warm amber light spilling through the glass windows and falling on the river, shattering into a sheet of golden light. Su Qingyuan sat by the window, looking out at the Seine, a glass of red wine in her hand. Lu Shiyan sat beside her, his arm draped over the back of her chair, not drinking, just quietly keeping her company.
Anna sat across from them, chatting with Jean-Pierre in French about something. Su Qingyuan couldn't understand, but from their expressions she could tell they were talking about her. She didn't mind, because they were saying good things.
"Miss Su." Jean-Pierre suddenly turned to her, speaking in English. "I have a proposal."
"Please."
"Next autumn, Cartier wants to hold a global touring exhibition on the theme of 'Light.' Starting from Paris, on to New York, Tokyo, Shanghai, and finally back to Paris. I'd like to make the 'Light Traces' collection one of the core works of the tour. Would you be willing?"
Su Qingyuan set down her glass, her heart quickening. "I'm willing."
"Then it's settled." Jean-Pierre raised his glass. "Miss Su, congratulations. From today on, your light will illuminate more than just yourself."
Su Qingyuan raised her glass and touched it to his. The red wine swayed faintly in the glass, reflecting the warm amber light overhead.
After dinner, Anna and Jean-Pierre left first. Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan strolled slowly along the Seine, the river shimmering silver-grey under the streetlamps, the distant Eiffel Tower lit gold like an enormous lighthouse.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Tomorrow we'll go up to Montmartre."
"All right."
"And then?"
"And then I'll take you to see something."
"What thing?"
"A secret."
Su Qingyuan tilted her head to look at him. He kept his eyes ahead, his expression perfectly serious, but the curve at the corner of his mouth gave him away. She didn't press. Because when he said "secret," there was a light in his eyes—a light that made her feel that whatever it was, it would be good.
The next morning, Montmartre.
Su Qingyuan stood on the steps before the Sacré-Cœur, looking out over all of Paris. The weather was fine; sunlight broke through from behind the clouds and fell on the grey rooftops, on the hazy horizon in the distance, on her face. She drew a deep breath and let it out. The air was cold but fresh, as if it had rinsed her lungs clean.
"Let's go." Lu Shiyan took her hand. "The Wall of Love isn't far."
The Wall of Love was in a small square in the Montmartre garden, not large, with a blue background, covered in "I love you" written in white lettering—more than three hundred languages, more than three hundred "I love you"s. Su Qingyuan stood before the wall and read slowly, from the French "Je t'aime" to the Japanese "愛してる," from Arabic to Hebrew, from Sanskrit to Latin. Every language had its own handwriting—some neat, some scrawled, some like drawings—but all said the same thing.
She found the line in Chinese. "我愛你," three characters, in black traditional script, sitting quietly on the blue wall, like a secret long kept hidden.
Su Qingyuan looked at those three characters and suddenly felt the urge to cry. Not out of emotion, but because it had suddenly struck her—these three words, Lu Shiyan had never once said to her. Not because he didn't want to say them, but because he was waiting. Waiting for the right moment, the right occasion, waiting for her to say "I'm ready to hear it." Today, he had brought her here.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"You brought me here—is there something you want to say?"
Lu Shiyan was silent for a few seconds. Then he turned to face her and gently took both her hands in his. His palms were warm, completely enclosing her two hands. The sunlight fell on his face; his expression was calm, but Su Qingyuan could see what was hidden in the depths of his eyes—three words that had begun a very, very long time ago and had never once been spoken aloud.
"Su Qingyuan."
"Mm."
"Je t'aime." In French.
Su Qingyuan's heart skipped a beat.
"愛してる." In Japanese.
Her eyes grew hot.
"Ich liebe Dich." In German.
Her tears fell.
"Ti amo." In Italian.
She laughed through her tears.
"我愛你." In Chinese.
Su Qingyuan rose onto her toes, wound both arms around his neck, and buried her face in the hollow of his throat. She cried—not the crying of sorrow, but the kind that comes after waiting a long time, finally arriving, never having to wait again.
"Lu Shiyan, you really are infuriating."
"Mm."
"Did you say 'I love you' in so many languages because you were afraid I wouldn't understand?"
"No. Because I was afraid I hadn't said it enough."
Su Qingyuan lifted her head from his shoulder and looked at him through tear-blurred eyes. "It's enough. One is enough. Save the rest to say slowly, later."
Lu Shiyan reached out and gently wiped the tears from her face. "All right. I'll say them slowly, later."
On the Wall of Love, that line of black "我愛你" rested quietly in the sunlight. Not far off, an elderly couple was also looking at the words on the wall. The old woman pointed at the line in Chinese and asked her husband, "What's this?" He shook his head. "I don't know, but it's lovely." The old woman laughed, took his arm, and leaned against his shoulder.
Watching them, Su Qingyuan suddenly felt that this was the future she wanted. Not something grand and sweeping, but something quiet—looking at a wall together, eating together, walking together along the Seine, growing old together.
"Lu Shiyan, will we be like them someday?"
"Like what?"
Su Qingyuan pointed at the old couple. The old woman was using her scarf to wipe a smudge of ice cream from the corner of her husband's mouth, the gesture as natural as if she'd done it countless times.
Lu Shiyan glanced over, then turned back to look at her. "We will."
"How do you know?"
"Because I won't let you get ice cream on the corner of your mouth."
Su Qingyuan laughed and gave him a punch. "I wasn't talking about ice cream!"
"I know." Lu Shiyan caught the fist she'd swung at him. "What I mean is, we'll be together a long time. So long we won't be able to remember when it began."
Su Qingyuan looked at him and smiled. The sunlight fell on her face, into her eyes, on the smile at the corner of her mouth. She suddenly remembered a long time ago—a rainy night, when she'd sat on the floor of a hotel, believing her world had ended. Back then she hadn't known that that rain was the end of the storm, and also the beginning of dawn.
Now she knew.
Coming down from Montmartre, Su Qingyuan's legs ached a little. Lu Shiyan offered to carry her on his back; she wouldn't let him, so he took her hand and walked slowly. The setting sun stretched their two shadows long, long, overlapping into one—like a painting that would never fade.
"Lu Shiyan, you said you'd take me to see something. What is it?"
"We're going to see it now."
Lu Shiyan led her through a few small lanes to the entrance of a tiny shopfront. The frontage was old, made of wood, its paint peeling, but it had been wiped spotlessly clean. In the display window sat croissants of every kind—plain, almond, chocolate-filled, golden, glistening under the light.
"What's this?"
"The oldest croissant shop in Paris. It's been open eighty years." Lu Shiyan pushed open the door, and a bell rang with a tinkle. "You said you wanted croissants, so I looked it up—this one's the best."
Su Qingyuan stepped inside. The shop was tiny, with only three tables. Black-and-white photographs hung on the walls, showing the shop as it had looked decades ago. The owner was a white-haired old man, who said a word of welcome in French when he saw them come in.
Lu Shiyan ordered in French. Su Qingyuan couldn't understand, but from the old man's smile she could tell he was saying good things. The croissants came out still steaming, their golden, flaky crusts layered like petals. Su Qingyuan picked one up and took a bite; the crust shattered between her teeth, the scent of butter filling her mouth—hot, flaky, sweet.
"Good?"
"So good!"
Lu Shiyan watched her eyes shine as she ate, the corners of his mouth lifting. He picked up a croissant, took a bite, then frowned. "Too sweet."
"It's not sweet." Su Qingyuan took another big bite.
Lu Shiyan looked at her and smiled. He pushed the rest of his croissant in front of her. "If you like it, eat more."
Su Qingyuan didn't stand on ceremony; she finished both croissants. When she was done, she took a sip of coffee, leaned back in her chair, and let out a long breath. "Paris is wonderful."
"Is it Paris that's wonderful, or the croissants?"
"Both. But the best thing—"
She paused and looked at Lu Shiyan. "The best thing is that you brought me here."
Lu Shiyan reached out and gently brushed a flake of pastry from the corner of her mouth. "I'll bring you again sometime."
"When is 'sometime'?"
"Whenever you want to come."
Su Qingyuan smiled, her eyes curving. "Then it might be soon. Because I might want to come again tomorrow."
"Then tomorrow it is."
Su Qingyuan looked at his earnest expression, and her heart melted into a hopeless mess.
By the time they'd finished the croissants, the sky had darkened. They walked slowly back to the parking lot through Montmartre's little lanes, the streetlamps flickering on one by one, casting the cobbled path beneath their feet into a soft, hazy glow.
"Lu Shiyan, what shall we do tomorrow?"
"Tomorrow we'll go see an exhibition."
"What exhibition?"
"The Louvre. You once said that as a child you saw a photo of the Mona Lisa in a magazine, and wanted to see her with your own eyes."
Su Qingyuan stopped walking and looked at his back. He didn't turn around; he kept walking forward. But she knew he was waiting for her. She hurried to catch up, took his arm, and leaned against his shoulder. "Lu Shiyan, just how many surprises have you prepared for me?"
"Not many left."
"Really?"
"No."
Su Qingyuan laughed, her eyes curving into crescents. The Paris night wind was chilly, but she wasn't cold at all. Because leaning against his side was the warmest place there was.
Back at the hotel, Su Qingyuan stood at her door without going in right away. Lu Shiyan stood behind her, waiting.
"Lu Shiyan, today at the Wall of Love, you said 'I love you' in so many languages. There was one I didn't hear."
"Which one?"
"The Chinese one."
"I said it."
"Say it again."
Lu Shiyan looked at her, his gaze deep as the night. The corridor light fell on his face; his expression was very tender. "Su Qingyuan, I love you."
Not in French, not in Japanese, not in any language that needed translating. In Chinese, her mother tongue, the language she'd been able to hear and speak since the day she was born. These three words she had heard countless times—on television, in books, in other people's stories. But never once had they been spoken to her.
Today, he had made up for that.
Su Qingyuan's tears fell again. She rose onto her toes and kissed him.
The corridor was very quiet, only the sound of their two breaths. The light was bright and warm, falling over them like a beam that would never go out.
At length, Su Qingyuan let go, stepped back, and wiped her tears. "All right. Good night."
"Good night."
She turned, opened the door, went in, and shut it. She leaned against the door panel for a long while, listening to his footsteps recede down the corridor, then the door of the next room opened and closed again.
She curled her lips into a smile, walked to the window, and drew the curtains open. The Eiffel Tower was still there, its golden lights blinking on and off, as if telling her—good night.
Su Qingyuan opened the window a crack, and the cold air poured in, carrying the crisp scent unique to a Paris night. She breathed in deeply and let it out. Then she picked up her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "Good night. See you tomorrow."
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "See you tomorrow."
Su Qingyuan set her phone on the nightstand and turned off the light. In the darkness, she closed her eyes. Many things spun through her mind—the "I love you" on the Wall of Love, the flaky crust of the croissant, the lights of the Seine, the gold of the Eiffel Tower, the look on his face when he said, "Su Qingyuan, I love you." These things turned for a long while, then slowly settled into quiet.
She rolled over, pulled the covers up to her chin, and sank into a deep sleep.
【System: This chapter—the Paris launch a success, an invitation to Cartier's global tour, the "Light Traces" collection recognized. Montmartre, the Wall of Love, Lu Shiyan saying "I love you" in multiple languages. The small, certain happiness of the croissant shop. Sweetness off the charts. Next chapter, the Louvre—and perhaps other surprises. The Gu Changfeng thread and the Su Yurou thread have wrapped up; the romance line reaches a new height. The System is settling in to wait for the next chapter.】
