Chapter 33 — Shadows of the Louvre, Light on the Seine
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626235.html

On her third day in Paris, Su Qingyuan was woken by the sun.
Golden light spilled in through the gap in the curtains, falling across the white sheets like a thin river of light. She lay in bed staring at the ceiling, listening to the occasional whistle of pigeons outside the window and the blurred hum of distant traffic, her thoughts turning slow and lazy—today they were going to the Louvre, to see the Mona Lisa, the Winged Victory, the armless Venus.
She rolled over and picked up her phone. There was a message from Lu Shiyan on the screen, sent seven minutes ago: "Awake? Breakfast's downstairs."
The corners of Su Qingyuan's mouth curved up, and she replied with a single word: "Coming."
She washed and dressed at record speed. Today she wore a cream-white knit dress, with that smoke-grey cashmere coat over it, and the scarf Lu Shiyan had bought the night before at a little shop in Montmartre—deep blue, cashmere, soft as a cloud. She checked herself in the mirror, looped the scarf around again so it looked carelessly thrown on—though in truth she'd adjusted it several times.
The hotel restaurant was on the ground floor, small but warm. Floor-to-ceiling windows faced a little courtyard with a tree of some unknown kind, its bare branches still hung with last night's raindrops, glittering in the morning light. Lu Shiyan sat by the window. On the table before him were two cups of coffee, a basket of croissants, a small dish of butter, a small dish of jam. He'd changed too—a deep blue sweater, black trousers, sleeves rolled up to his forearms. The whole effect was far softer than usual, like an ordinary man simply waiting to have breakfast with his girlfriend.
Su Qingyuan walked over and sat down across from him. "Have you been here long?"
"Not long." Lu Shiyan pushed a coffee toward her. "Just came down."
Su Qingyuan glanced at his coffee cup—already half empty. "Liar."
The corner of Lu Shiyan's mouth lifted slightly; he didn't deny it. He picked up a croissant, sliced it open carefully with the butter knife, spread butter and jam over it, then set it on the plate in front of her. "Try this jam. The hotel makes it themselves—raspberry."
Su Qingyuan took a bite. The sweet-tart jam melted on the tip of her tongue, mingling with the rich scent of the butter, so good it made her narrow her eyes. "How did you know raspberry jam was good?"
"Looked it up yesterday." Lu Shiyan's tone was offhand, as if it were the most trivial thing.
Su Qingyuan watched the side of his face as he bent over the jam, and her heart went utterly soft. This man—he even looked up which breakfast jam to order in advance.
The Louvre wasn't far from the hotel, less than a twenty-minute walk. They strolled along the Seine, where sightseeing boats glided slowly past on the water, the people aboard holding up their phones to photograph the buildings on the banks, the tower in the distance, and—who knew if anyone had caught the two of them in a shot. Su Qingyuan held onto Lu Shiyan's arm, her steps unhurried, because she didn't want to walk too fast. Every corner of Paris deserved to be seen slowly, felt slowly.
"Lu Shiyan, have you been to the Louvre before?"
"Once. On a business trip, accompanying a client."
"And did you go in to look?"
"No. Spent two hours waiting for the client at the entrance."
Su Qingyuan looked up at him, picturing him standing alone at the Louvre's entrance in a suit, expressionless, waiting for a client to come out. She couldn't help laughing. "Then today you can have a proper look."
"Mm. Together with you."
The Louvre's glass pyramid sparkled in the sunlight like an enormous crystal sculpture. A long queue stretched at the entrance, but Lu Shiyan had booked tickets online ahead of time, and they went in through the fast-track lane with almost no waiting. Su Qingyuan stood beneath the pyramid, tilting her head back to gaze up at the glass structure overhead, the sunlight falling through it onto her face, refracting into rainbow-colored flecks.
"Beautiful?" Lu Shiyan stood behind her.
"Beautiful. I'd only ever seen it in photos. I never imagined that actually standing here, I'd feel so…" She thought for a moment, found the word, "moved."
Lu Shiyan said nothing, only reached out and took her hand.
They went first to the ancient Greek sculpture gallery. The armless Venus stood at the center of the hall, the white plaster glowing softly under the lights. Su Qingyuan stood before her and looked for a long time. She had seen countless pictures of this statue, but only when she truly stood before it did she understand why it was called "the model of beauty"—not because the proportions were perfect, but because it held a quiet power that transcended time. Over two thousand years, it had lost its arms, lost its color, lost its original context, yet it remained beautiful. This kind of beauty needed no wholeness, no explanation.
Su Qingyuan suddenly thought of her own "Roots" series—the piece made with flawed pearls. Imperfection wasn't a defect; it was a story. The ancient Greek craftsmen of two thousand years ago could never have imagined that the "imperfection" they left behind would find an echo in another person's work.
"What are you thinking?" Lu Shiyan asked.
"I'm thinking that beautiful things don't need to be perfect."
Lu Shiyan looked at the statue of Venus and was silent for a few seconds. "Just like you."
Su Qingyuan turned her head to look at him. "What about me isn't perfect?"
"You are perfect." Lu Shiyan's voice was very soft. "But your beauty isn't because of perfection—it's because it's real."
Su Qingyuan turned her face away, pretending to keep looking at the statue. Her ears had gone red.
The Winged Victory's gallery was up a flight of stairs. As Su Qingyuan climbed the steps, the first thing she saw was the pair of wings—spread wide, immense, facing into the wind. The statue had no head, no arms, only a body and wings, yet that posture of pressing "forward" carried more power than any intact statue. She stood before it and thought of her own "Breaking the Cocoon"—the necklace made of opal and diamonds. "Breaking the Cocoon" had no head either, only wings. Because in the moment of breaking free, the head had not yet emerged; the wings came out first.
She took out her phone and snapped a photo of the Winged Victory, sending it to Zhou Wanyin. The caption read: "Paris, the Winged Victory. It made me think of 'Breaking the Cocoon.'"
Zhou Wanyin's reply came quickly: "'Breaking the Cocoon' IS the Winged Victory's wings. You've been doing the same thing all along."
Su Qingyuan looked at the message and the corners of her mouth curved up.
The Mona Lisa's gallery was packed, layer upon layer of tourists all holding up their phones. Su Qingyuan stood at the edge of the crowd, looking from afar at that small painting—much smaller than she'd imagined, shielded behind thick glass, the lights striking it and throwing back so many bright flecks that she couldn't make out the details.
"I can't squeeze in." Su Qingyuan was a little disappointed.
"Seeing it is enough." Lu Shiyan stood behind her, blocking the people pressing in from behind. "Some things don't need to be seen up close."
Su Qingyuan tilted her head toward him. "Such as?"
"Such as—you don't need to be close to me for me to still see you."
Su Qingyuan's heart beat faster. She turned back around and kept looking at the blurred Mona Lisa in the distance, saying nothing. But her hand stayed held in his the whole time.
By the time they came out of the Louvre, it was already two in the afternoon. Su Qingyuan's legs ached a little, and she was hungry too. Lu Shiyan took her to a restaurant tucked away in a small alley near the Louvre, the storefront tiny, but pushing the door open revealed a whole other world inside—a glass atrium planted with greenery, sunlight pouring down from overhead, warm and golden.
"You looked this up too?" Su Qingyuan asked.
"Mm."
"How many things did you look up?"
"Not many."
"Tell me the truth."
Lu Shiyan looked at her, the corner of his mouth lifting. "From the day we settled on the Paris trip, I looked up every place you might want to go."
Su Qingyuan opened her mouth to say something, but her throat felt as if it were blocked by something. From the day they settled on the trip. How long ago was that? Half a month? Twenty days? Back then she'd still been racing to finish the "Light Traces" collection, still fretting over Gu Changfeng's smear pieces. And he had already been researching Paris guides for her—the croissant shop, the Wall of Love, the Louvre, this restaurant hidden in an alley.
Everything he did was small. But strung together, it was one whole map, densely marked, with her name written all over it.
"Lu Shiyan, from now on don't look things up anymore."
Lu Shiyan's brow furrowed slightly.
"From now on, wherever we want to go, we'll look it up together."
Lu Shiyan looked into her eyes for a few seconds, then smiled. "All right. We'll look it up together."
The restaurant's food was wonderful. The starter was foie gras with toasted bread, the main course a confit duck leg, the skin crisp, the meat so tender it fell apart at a flick of the fork. Su Qingyuan ate slowly, because she didn't want the meal to end too soon.
"Lu Shiyan, where do you think is the best place in Paris?"
Lu Shiyan thought for a moment and said, "Montmartre."
"Why?"
"Because that's where the Wall of Love is."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head, prodding at the leftover duck on her plate with her fork. "You never used to say things like that."
"Things like what?"
"Sweet nothings."
Lu Shiyan was silent for a second. "I never used to know how. But with you, I don't need to know how—when I want to say it, I just say it."
Su Qingyuan's face flushed. She picked up her wine glass and took a big sip of red wine, using the burn of it to suppress the heat rising in her cheeks. Across from her, Lu Shiyan watched her, the smile in his eyes impossible to hide.
After lunch, they strolled along the Seine. The afternoon sun spilled over the water, breaking into a sheet of golden light. Su Qingyuan leaned against the railing of the riverbank, gazing at the distant Eiffel Tower, and suddenly remembered something.
"Lu Shiyan, you said you'd take me to see the Northern Lights—when are we going?"
"When do you want to go?"
"I don't know. When things aren't busy?"
"When are you ever not busy?"
Su Qingyuan thought about it and laughed. "Seems like I'm always busy."
"Then we'll make time." Lu Shiyan stood beside her, looking at the tower in the distance. "Time is like water in a sponge—squeeze, and there's always some to be had."
"When did you get so full of feel-good wisdom?"
"Learned it from you."
Su Qingyuan laughed and gave him a light punch. "I am not full of feel-good wisdom."
"Every piece you make is feel-good wisdom. 'Breaking the Cocoon' is feel-good wisdom, 'Daybreak' is feel-good wisdom, 'Roots' is feel-good wisdom too. But the feel-good wisdom you make tastes good."
Su Qingyuan didn't know whether to laugh or cry at that. She wanted to argue, but when she thought about it, she realized he wasn't really wrong. Her work really did all speak to a single theme—walking out of the darkness, finding the light, becoming the light. If that counted as feel-good wisdom, then it was a brew she'd simmered with her own hands, using her own bones and flesh.
"Then the Northern Lights, it's settled. After the 'Roots and Light' joint exhibition ends, we'll go."
"All right."
"To Northern Europe?"
"All right."
"See the aurora, ride a sled, stay in a glass igloo?"
"All right."
"Why do you keep saying all right to everything?"
"Because whatever you say is all right by me."
Su Qingyuan looked at his perfectly serious expression and laughed. She laughed until her eyes curved into crescent moons, laughed until the corners of her mouth tilted high, laughed until a passing old Frenchman glanced at her and started smiling too.
In the evening, they went to a bridge over the Seine—the Pont des Arts. The bridge was covered with love locks, dense and countless, some already rusted, some still brand new. Su Qingyuan walked across it, looking at the locks—some inscribed with names, some with dates, some with just a single heart.
"Should we lock one too?" Lu Shiyan asked.
Su Qingyuan thought about it and shook her head. "No. If we lock it, we'd have to come back to unlock it—too much trouble."
Lu Shiyan looked at her and smiled. "Then we won't lock one."
The two of them walked across the bridge, the setting sun sinking slowly behind them, dyeing the whole sky orange-red. Su Qingyuan glanced back, and that bridge hung with locks looked in the sunset like a golden necklace stretched across the Seine.
She suddenly felt that not locking one was just as good. Because a lock stood for "fear of losing." And she wasn't afraid of losing, because he wouldn't leave.
At night, they went to the Eiffel Tower. Not up it—they stood at its base, heads tilted back, looking up at it. The tower glowed with golden light in the night, and on the hour it would even sparkle, like countless stars lighting up all at once. Su Qingyuan looked up, her neck aching but unwilling to lower her head.
"Lu Shiyan, why do you think the Eiffel Tower is so tall?"
"So people can see."
"See what?"
"See all of Paris. See where they themselves are standing."
Su Qingyuan lowered her tilted head and looked at him. "Then where are you standing?"
Lu Shiyan looked down at her, his gaze deep as the night. "Standing beside you."
Su Qingyuan smiled. She rose onto her tiptoes and pressed a light peck to his lips. "Let's head back. We've got the exhibition to see tomorrow."
"All right."
They walked back to the hotel along the Seine. The night breeze was cool, but Su Qingyuan wasn't cold at all. Because she wore the cashmere coat he'd given her, wrapped in the handmade scarf he'd bought in Montmartre, her hand held warm in his palm. She looked down at their two clasped hands and suddenly felt that winter in Paris was warmer than spring in Nancheng.
Back at the hotel, Su Qingyuan stood at the door of her room and didn't go in right away.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Tomorrow's the last day in Paris."
"Mm."
"I'm a little reluctant to leave."
Lu Shiyan looked at her, reached out to unwind her scarf, folded it, and placed it in her hands. "Reluctant to leave Paris?"
"Reluctant to leave these few days of you showing me around Paris."
Lu Shiyan was silent for a second, then leaned in and dropped an exceedingly light, exceedingly gentle kiss on her forehead. "There'll be other chances. This isn't the last time."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes, feeling the warmth of that kiss. Then she opened her eyes and smiled. "Goodnight."
"Goodnight."
She turned and walked into her room, closing the door. She leaned against the door panel for a long while, listening to the sound of his footsteps gradually fading down the corridor, then the door of the room next to hers opened, and closed again.
She pressed the scarf to her face and caught the clean, cool scent of him. Then she picked up her phone and sent him a message: "I forgot to give you back the scarf."
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "It's a gift for you."
Su Qingyuan: "Didn't you say you just bought it on a whim in Montmartre? How did it become a gift now?"
Lu Shiyan: "I bought it on the side in Montmartre, but it was always meant for you. Not on the side—on purpose."
Su Qingyuan stared at those words, "on purpose," and the corners of her mouth curved to their widest arc. She folded the scarf, set it beside her pillow, and turned off the light.
In the darkness, she closed her eyes. The last night in Paris was quiet and tender. Outside the window came the occasional sound of a car driving past, and from somewhere far off the faint strains of music, as if someone were playing the violin, broken and intermittent, but lovely.
She rolled over, pulled the blanket up to her chin, and sank into a deep sleep.
Tomorrow, back to Nancheng.
After she returned, the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition would be waiting for her, the global tour of the "Light Traces" collection would be waiting for her, new designs, new challenges, new stories—all waiting for her. But she wasn't anxious, because she knew that whatever lay ahead, he would be there.
[System: This chapter covered all of Paris's classic romantic elements—the Louvre, the Seine, the Pont des Arts, the Eiffel Tower. Lu Shiyan's "researching the guides in advance," the "scarf bought on purpose," and "standing beside you" raised the sweetness on the sly. The heroine's career line—the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition, the global tour—advanced in the background, but never stole the romance line's thunder. Next chapter: back to Nancheng. Exhibition preparations, new challenges, and perhaps a new rival or opportunity. The System stands by.]

Chapter 34 — The Journey Home, the Journey Begins
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626236.html

On their last day in Paris, Su Qingyuan rose early. She walked alone into the hotel's little courtyard, sat down beneath that nameless tree, and cradled a cup of hot coffee in her hands. Morning light spilled through the gaps in the rooftops and fell across the bluestone paving, like gold shattered all over the ground. From somewhere far off came the toll of a church bell, deep and slow, one stroke after another, like some ancient promise.
These past few days in Paris, she had been to many places and done many things. The launch at the Cartier flagship, the Wall of Love in Montmartre, the three treasures of the Louvre, the bridges over the Seine, gazing up beneath the Eiffel Tower. But what stayed with her most weren't those landmark views—it was the small, trivial moments that weren't worth mentioning: the slight embarrassment on Lu Shiyan's face as he ordered in French at the croissant shop; the way he gripped her hand when he said "I love you" at the Wall of Love; the effortless ease with which he shielded her from the crush of the crowd at the Louvre; the light in his eyes when he said "standing beside you" by the Seine.
Those moments were worth keeping far more than any scenery.
"Up this early?" Lu Shiyan's voice came from behind her. Su Qingyuan turned and saw him standing in the courtyard doorway, wearing a dark grey cardigan, a cup of coffee in his hand, his hair not yet combed, a few strands falling over his forehead. He looked far softer than usual. She curved her lips. "It's our last day. I couldn't bear to sleep."
Lu Shiyan came over and sat down beside her. The two of them sat shoulder to shoulder beneath that nameless tree, drinking coffee, listening to the faint, distant bells. Neither spoke, but the silence was comfortable—like two rivers that had run their long courses and finally converged on a wide, open plain.
"Lu Shiyan, do you think we'll ever come back to Paris?"
"We will."
"When?"
"Whenever you want to."
Su Qingyuan tilted her head to look at him. The sunlight fell across the side of his face, throwing his profile into tender, clear relief. "Then it's settled. Once every year from now on."
"All right."
"Every year we'll go to Montmartre, eat croissants, see the Wall of Love."
"All right."
"Every year we'll go to the Louvre, fail to squeeze in to see the Mona Lisa, and just look at her from outside."
Lu Shiyan turned to look at her, the corner of his mouth lifting. "Su Qingyuan, are you setting up a once-a-year date with me?"
Su Qingyuan's face went red, but she didn't deny it. "Yes. Do you agree?"
Lu Shiyan looked at her for a few seconds, then reached out and gently ruffled her hair. "I agree. Once a year. Not one time less."
Su Qingyuan smiled, her eyes curving into crescent moons.
That afternoon they packed their luggage and checked out. Jean-Philippe drove them to the airport, and the whole way Su Qingyuan watched the Paris streets slide past the window, committing every corner, every tree, every streetlamp to memory. She knew she would come back, but the next time she did, she might already be someone different. Paris wouldn't change—but she would. She would return here carrying new work, new stories, a new self.
The airport. The departure lounge. Su Qingyuan sat by the floor-to-ceiling window, watching the planes out on the tarmac. Sunlight poured in through the vast glass curtain wall and settled over her, warm and gentle. Lu Shiyan had gone to check the bags; she sat there alone, holding two cups of coffee—an Americano, hers, and a latte, his. She looked down at the names written on the cups and suddenly remembered the first time she'd drunk the coffee he bought, back in Nancheng. Back then they weren't yet together. He'd invited her up to his office on the eighty-eighth floor; she'd taken a sip of an Americano and frowned at the bitterness, and he'd immediately had someone swap it for a latte. She hadn't said anything at the time, but she'd kept it in her heart—this man had even noticed how she liked her coffee.
Lu Shiyan came back and sat down beside her. Su Qingyuan handed him the latte. "Yours." He took it and drank a sip. "Thanks." The two of them sat side by side, watching the planes beyond the window. The sunlight grew brighter and brighter, turning the whole departure lounge into one enormous, transparent greenhouse.
"Su Qingyuan, once we're back, what do you most want to do?"
Su Qingyuan thought it over. "Go to the studio and look at the last two pieces of the 'Roots' series. Then find Qu Ya and finalize the last details of the joint exhibition. And then—"
"And then?"
"And then go see the aurora with you."
Lu Shiyan turned to look at her. "Weren't we going to wait until after the joint exhibition?"
"No more waiting," Su Qingyuan said, looking out the window, her voice very soft. "The things you want to do, do them early. The people you want to see, see them early. The places you want to go, go early."
Lu Shiyan fell silent for a few seconds, then reached out and took her hand. "All right. Once we're back, I'll arrange it."
When the plane took off, Su Qingyuan leaned against Lu Shiyan's shoulder, watching Paris grow smaller and smaller beyond the cabin window. Paris was like a painting, slowly shrinking in her field of vision, dwindling to a single point, until at last it vanished into the clouds. She closed her eyes and silently said to herself—Goodbye, Paris. Thank you for these days of light.
As the plane cut through the clouds, Su Qingyuan fell asleep again. She had another dream. In the dream she was still that little girl sitting on the attic floor, drawing. But this time, the sky outside the window wasn't a dull grey—it was blue, so very blue, like the sky over Paris. Downstairs there was no foster mother's voice nagging her to cook; there was only the rustle of wind through the leaves. She kept drawing, and what she drew was light—not the thin thread of gold seeping out from behind the clouds, but light that filled the whole sky, light like the aurora.
Nancheng. Touchdown.
Coming out through customs, Su Qingyuan drew a deep breath of Nancheng air. Cold, dry, carrying the biting chill peculiar to northern cities. So unlike the dampness of Paris—but this was her city. She had grown up here, been wounded here, picked herself back up here, met him here. Every street, every building, every streetlamp in this city held a memory of hers.
"We're back," she said.
"Mm. We're back." Lu Shiyan stood beside her, pushing two suitcases. One black, one white, leaning against each other, like a pair.
"Shall I take you to the studio first?" he asked.
Su Qingyuan thought about it. "Home first. I want to shower, change clothes, and then go to the studio."
"All right."
The car pulled out of the airport and merged into Nancheng's traffic. The streetscape outside rushed backward—familiar road signs, familiar billboards, familiar traffic lights. Su Qingyuan leaned back in her seat, looking out the window, and suddenly felt as though Nancheng had changed a little—not that the city had changed, but that the way she looked at it had. She used to think Nancheng was huge, so huge she couldn't find her own place in it. Now she felt Nancheng was small, small enough that his shadow lingered in every corner.
The car stopped below Su Qingyuan's apartment building. She pushed open the door, got out, and lifted her white suitcase from the trunk. Lu Shiyan got out too, standing across from her. The afternoon sunlight fell in the space between them, like a transparent wall.
"I'll pick you up tonight, for dinner," he said.
"All right."
"What do you want to eat?"
"What you cook."
Lu Shiyan glanced at her, the corner of his mouth lifting. "All right. I'll cook."
Su Qingyuan dragged her suitcase in through the building's entrance and looked back once. He was still standing by the car, the sunlight falling on his shoulders, gazing in her direction, like a lighthouse that would never move. She curved her lips, turned, and went up.
Home. She opened the door, switched on the lights. Everything was as before. The cyclamen on the windowsill was still blooming, its pink-white petals trembling faintly in the afternoon sun. She watered the flowers, opened her suitcase, and took things out one by one—clothes, scarves, sketchbook, carving knife. She opened the sketchbook; tucked inside was a postcard she'd bought at the Louvre, a print of the Mona Lisa. She looked at it for a few seconds, slipped it back between the pages, and closed the book.
She showered, changed into clean clothes, and went out. A taxi dropped her at the entrance of the creative park. She walked in, and from a distance saw her own studio. That door, that window, that bare osmanthus tree. She stood in the doorway, took the key from her bag, slid it into the lock, and turned. The instant the door swung open, the familiar smell washed over her—wood, metal, paper, and a faint lingering trace of osmanthus. She walked in, switched on the light, and sat down at the workbench.
Everything was still here. Her carving knives, her tools, the unfinished fourth sample of the 'Roots' series. She picked up a carving knife, turned it over in her hand, then set it down. No work today. Today she had simply come to tell the studio—I'm back.
She stood, walked to the window, and pushed it open. Cold air rushed in, dispersing the air that had sat stale in the room for over ten days. In the distance, along Nancheng's skyline, the clouds were thick and greyish-white—but the sun broke through the gaps in them, falling onto the ground, onto that bare osmanthus tree, onto her face.
Su Qingyuan drew a deep breath, and let it out.
That evening, Lu Shiyan came to pick her up. The car stopped below his building, and Su Qingyuan looked up at it, remembering how, the last time she'd come, he'd said, "You're welcome here anytime." Today she'd come again.
The elevator rose; the doors opened. Lu Shiyan went in first, Su Qingyuan following behind, swapping her shoes for that same pair of pale grey plush slippers from last time. The living room lights were still that warm yellow, white chamomile still sat on the coffee table, that pale cream throw was still draped over the sofa. Everything was just as it had been last time—but Su Qingyuan noticed that the entryway now held an extra pair of slippers: pale pink, fluffy, in her size.
"New slippers?" she asked.
"Mm. The last pair were guest slippers. These are yours."
Su Qingyuan looked down at the pale pink slippers, and something inside her went soft. She changed into the new slippers, walked into the living room, and sat down on the sofa. Lu Shiyan had gone to the kitchen, and she heard the refrigerator door opening and closing, the sound of running water, the clatter of pots and bowls. She leaned back on the sofa, hugging that pale cream throw, listening to all those small sounds from the kitchen, and suddenly felt—"home" was this sound. Not the luxurious décor, not the expensive furniture, but the sound of someone cooking for you in the kitchen.
Su Qingyuan stood, walked to the kitchen doorway, and leaned against the frame, watching Lu Shiyan. He was chopping vegetables—not exactly skilled, but very earnest. One stroke after another, neither fast nor slow, the shredded potato coming out evenly cut.
"When did you learn to chop vegetables?"
"These past few days, while you were away. I watched a few videos and practiced."
"How many days did you practice?"
"Started the day you left."
Su Qingyuan fell silent. She'd been gone over ten days, and he'd been practicing chopping vegetables from the very day she left. Not for himself—so that when she came back, he could make her a proper meal.
"Lu Shiyan, if you keep this up, I'm going to cry."
"Then cry. It's not like I'll laugh at you."
Su Qingyuan didn't cry. She walked over, wrapped her arms around him from behind, and pressed her face to his back. Through the sweater she could feel the warmth of his body, warm, like a stove in winter.
Lu Shiyan's hands paused mid-chop, then went on. He didn't say anything, but she could feel his heartbeat had quickened.
Dinner was stir-fried shredded potato, scrambled eggs with tomato, and a bowl of seaweed and egg-drop soup. All the simplest of dishes, but Su Qingyuan ate with great care, chewing every bite slowly. Because these were the vegetables he'd chopped for her in the kitchen, the dishes he'd cooked for her, the soup he'd boiled for her.
"Is it good?" he asked.
"It's good."
"Really?"
"Really. A lot better than the congee last time."
Lu Shiyan smiled, his brows and eyes relaxing open, like the first crack splitting across an ice-bound lake in spring.
After dinner, Su Qingyuan offered to wash the dishes. Lu Shiyan stood in the kitchen doorway, leaning against the frame, watching her profile as she washed. The warm yellow light fell over her, her hair loose around her shoulders, sleeves rolled to her elbows, baring slender wrists.
"Su Qingyuan, you look lovely washing dishes."
Su Qingyuan's hands paused. "Have you never washed dishes before?"
"I have. But I've never watched anyone else do it."
Su Qingyuan, blushing, set the last bowl in the rack, wiped her hands, and turned to look at him. "Seen enough?"
"No. I'll never see enough."
Su Qingyuan walked over, rose on her toes, and pecked him lightly on the lips.
"Good night. I should head back."
"I'll walk you."
"No need, you wash the dishes."
Lu Shiyan smiled. "All right. Message me when you get home."
Su Qingyuan changed her shoes, walked to the door, and looked back once. He was standing in the kitchen doorway, apron still on, holding the dishcloth she'd just used, gazing in her direction. She curved her lips, pushed the door open, and went out.
The instant the elevator doors closed, she leaned against the elevator wall, looked at her flushed face in the mirror, and laughed. She took out her phone and sent him a message: "I just left and I already miss you."
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "Then come back."
Su Qingyuan looked at those three words, smiled, and slipped the phone into her pocket. She didn't go back. Because tomorrow she'd come again, and the day after, and every day after that—she could come anytime. One night wouldn't make a difference.
Back at the apartment, she showered, lay down in bed, and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "Home."
Lu Shiyan: "Mm. Shut the windows tight, don't turn the heating up too high."
Su Qingyuan: "Shut them. Set it to twenty degrees."
Lu Shiyan: "Good. See you tomorrow."
Su Qingyuan: "See you tomorrow."
She set the phone on the nightstand and turned off the light. In the darkness, she closed her eyes. So many things were turning in her mind—the sunlight of Paris, the words on the Wall of Love, the Venus of the Louvre, the figure chopping vegetables in the kitchen, that pair of pale pink slippers, his heartbeat when he said "Then come back." Those things turned for a long while, then slowly quieted down.
She rolled over, pulled the blanket up to her chin, and sank into a deep sleep.
The next morning, Su Qingyuan went to Qu Ya's studio. Qu Ya was in the darkroom developing photographs; hearing the doorbell, she came out with wet hands to open the door. "You're back? How was Paris?"
"Wonderful. The Cartier launch was a big success—the 'Light Traces' series was recognized."
"I knew it." Qu Ya smiled and pulled her inside. "Come, take a look at the final plan for the joint exhibition."
The long table in the studio was covered with design drawings, photo proofs, and booth layout plans. Qu Ya pointed at one of them. "The venue's confirmed—the Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art, main hall, five hundred square meters. Your work takes half, mine takes half, and the middle is a shared zone. The theme is called 'Convergence.'"
Su Qingyuan looked at the layout plan, her heart racing. "'Convergence'—the source of the light and the home of the roots, converging right there."
"Exactly. That's the idea." Qu Ya turned to another page. "The promotional materials are settled too. Posters, invitations, media kits—all one visual system. Your 'Roots' series and my 'Roots' series set side by side, one theme, two expressions."
Su Qingyuan turned through it page by page, every page the fruit of her and Qu Ya's labor. This joint exhibition, from the first spark of inspiration to the final plan now, had taken nearly two months. In those two months, she'd made five pieces, Qu Ya had shot twenty photographs, they'd held countless meetings and quarreled countless times—not for the sake of winning, but to make the exhibition better.
"Sister Ya, thank you."
"Thank me for what? I should be thanking you." Qu Ya closed the portfolio and looked at her. "Without your 'Roots,' there'd be no exhibition."
The two of them looked at each other, and both smiled.
As Su Qingyuan came out of Qu Ya's studio, she received a WeChat message. Not from Lu Shiyan, not from Zhou Wanyin, but from a name she hadn't heard from in a long time—Chen Xiaoman.
"Qingyuan, I've made it to Australia. I found a job at a restaurant, room and board included, the pay isn't much but it's enough to live on. The sunshine here is wonderful. I think my life can start over. Thank you for not pressing charges. I'll live well. I'm sorry, and thank you."
Su Qingyuan looked at the message and was silent for a long while.
She didn't reply. Not because she didn't want to, but because she didn't know what to say. "It's all right"? No—because Chen Xiaoman really had hurt her. "I forgive you"? No either—because Chen Xiaoman didn't need her forgiveness. In the end she typed four words: "Live a good life." Then she sent a photo she'd taken that day—Nancheng's sky, greyish-white, but with the sun breaking through from behind the clouds, falling onto the ground, gleaming gold.
After sending it, she put her phone away, walked to the parking lot, and got into the car. She leaned back in the rear seat, watching the streetscape rush backward beyond the window. Nancheng's winter was still going on, but she knew the coldest part was already behind her. Not because Gu Changfeng had lost, but because she herself had won—won the battle inside her own heart. She used to fear being doubted, feared being denied, feared having all her effort wiped away by a single line: "She got there through connections." Now she wasn't afraid anymore, because her work was her proof, and her name was her brand.
Her phone buzzed—a message from Lu Shiyan: "What do you want to eat tonight?"
Su Qingyuan thought for a moment and typed two words: "Hot pot."
Lu Shiyan: "All right. I'll come get you."
Su Qingyuan: "No need to pick me up, I'm already on the way. See you at the studio."
Lu Shiyan: "All right. See you at the studio."
Su Qingyuan curved her lips, put her phone away, and looked out the window. In Nancheng's evening, the sky slowly darkened, the streetlamps lighting up one by one, linking into a warm river of light. She suddenly remembered, a long time ago, one rainy night, standing at the roadside, not knowing where she was going.
Now she knew.
[System: This chapter, returning from Paris to Nancheng, the female lead's 'Roots' series advances, Qu Ya's joint exhibition finalizes its plan, Chen Xiaoman sends word from Australia, and though Su Yurou doesn't appear, her current situation is conveyed indirectly through Chen Xiaoman's lines—"The sunshine in Australia is wonderful, I think my life can start over." Lu Shiyan's back in the kitchen, the pale pink slippers, the sweetness of him saying "I'll never see enough" while she washes dishes. Next chapter, the countdown to the joint exhibition, a new challenge—perhaps from within the industry, perhaps from somewhere farther away. The System stands by.]

========================================

Chapter 35 — Countdown to the Joint Exhibition, and the Storm Returns
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626237.html

Su Qingyuan hadn't expected the news of the joint exhibition to draw such a wave of attention.
The night Qu Ya Studio's official account posted the exhibition poster, the reshares blew past fifty thousand. The comment section was a uniform chorus of anticipation and curiosity — "A crossover exhibition of photography and jewelry? I've never seen that before!" "Qu Ya's work has always had this 'rootedness' to it; pairing it with Su Qingyuan's 'Roots' series is honestly a match made in heaven." "Su Qingyuan's first big move since coming home — can't wait!" There were a handful of skeptical voices too — "A newcomer who just barely surfaced, and she gets to stand shoulder to shoulder with Qu Ya in a joint show? On what grounds?" "Is Qu Ya short on cash or something, collaborating with just anybody?" But those voices were quickly drowned out by the flood of supportive comments.
Su Qingyuan didn't reply to the doubters. She didn't need to — because a month from now, the work would speak for her.
The venue for the joint exhibition was set in the main hall of the Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art: five hundred square meters, eight-meter ceilings, a floor-to-ceiling glass curtain wall facing Nancheng's skyline. The first time Su Qingyuan walked into the space, she was struck silent by its vastness and its quiet. Her work would stand here, alongside Qu Ya's work, before hundreds of invited guests, before everyone who came to see the show.
"Nervous?" Qu Ya stood beside her, the floor plan in her hands.
"A little," Su Qingyuan admitted honestly. "Before, I was always at other people's shows, looking at other people's work. This time it's my own show. Mine and yours."
"Then be nervous. Nerves mean you care." Qu Ya patted her shoulder. "Come on, let's go figure out exactly how to lay it out."
For the next two weeks, Su Qingyuan's life was crammed full of the exhibition.
Up at six every morning, at the studio by seven, handling the follow-up on the "Light Traces" collection — Cartier had finalized the route for the global touring exhibition: after Paris came New York, Tokyo, Shanghai, and finally back to Paris. She had to provide a different display scheme for each stop, and the workload was no small thing. From nine in the morning until four in the afternoon was Qu Ya's time. Venue measurements, booth design, lighting calibration, traffic-flow planning — every single item required her involvement in the decisions. In the evening she'd return to her own studio and keep refining the last two pieces of the "Roots" series — the fourth piece was entering its finishing stage, the fifth still in rough grinding. At night Lu Shiyan would come to pick her up, the two of them would have dinner together, and she'd lean against his shoulder, sometimes falling asleep mid-sentence.
"You're wearing yourself out like this," Lu Shiyan said more than once.
"I'm not tired," Su Qingyuan answered every time. "Doing what you love doesn't tire you out."
Lu Shiyan looked at the faint shadows under her eyes and didn't press her again. He simply brought a different dinner every day — soup one day, plain congee with little side dishes the next, her favorite malatang the day after. He knew he couldn't talk her out of it, but he could use a bowl of hot soup, a warm meal, to let her feel a little bit of warmth at the end of an exhausting day.
Ten days before the exhibition opened, Su Qingyuan got an unexpected phone call.
It was the secretary-general of the Nancheng Design Association, surnamed Bai, a man in his fifties with an extraordinarily wide network and an extraordinarily heavy voice in the industry. Secretary-General Bai's tone was courteous but distant. "Miss Su, I hear you're putting on a joint exhibition with Teacher Qu Ya? Would you mind coming by the association for a chat? There are a few things I'd like to discuss with you."
Su Qingyuan gripped her phone, a thousand thoughts turning over in her mind. She'd had little contact with the Design Association before, but she'd heard Secretary-General Bai's name — famously "hard to get in to see, hard to get a smile out of." For him to call and request a meeting himself was unheard of.
"Of course, Secretary-General Bai. When would be convenient for you?"
"Tomorrow afternoon at three, at the association office."
After hanging up, Su Qingyuan sent Qu Ya a message: "Secretary-General Bai wants me to come to the association tomorrow. Did he say what it's about?"
Qu Ya's reply came quickly: "He didn't say? I got one too. Tomorrow afternoon at three, the association office. Looks like it's something big."
Su Qingyuan stared at the message, a not-so-good premonition settling in. Not fear — instinct. For Secretary-General Bai to summon both her and Qu Ya at once, it couldn't be good news.
The next afternoon, Su Qingyuan and Qu Ya met downstairs at the association. Qu Ya wore a black wool coat, her expression considerably more serious than usual. "Any guesses what this is about?"
"No idea. But it won't be bad news, and it won't be good news either," Su Qingyuan said plainly. Qu Ya glanced at her. "Come on. We'll know once we're inside."
Secretary-General Bai's office was on the seventh floor — not large, but imposing. Photographs of every past chairman of the Design Association hung on the wall, and the bookshelves were crammed with trophies and certificates of every kind. Secretary-General Bai sat behind his desk, his grizzled hair combed meticulously, wearing a dark gray Zhongshan suit, looking like a retired old professor — if one ignored the shrewdness in his eyes, the kind that came from long years at the top.
"Miss Su, Teacher Qu, please, sit." Secretary-General Bai gestured to the two chairs across from him. "Tea or coffee?"
"Tea," Qu Ya said.
"Coffee, thank you," Su Qingyuan said.
Secretary-General Bai nodded and sent his assistant to prepare it. Then he took a document from a drawer and laid it on the desk without opening it. "I asked you here today because I'd like to discuss something with you."
Su Qingyuan and Qu Ya exchanged a glance.
"The venue for your joint exhibition — the main hall of the Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art — may no longer be available."
Su Qingyuan's heart sank. Qu Ya's brow furrowed. "Secretary-General Bai, what do you mean?"
Secretary-General Bai let out a sigh. "The city has a major diplomatic event come up at the last minute, and they need the museum's main hall. The dates fall right on the week of your exhibition. I tried to fight for you, but it was arranged directly by the city. There's nothing to be done."
Qu Ya's face darkened. "Secretary-General Bai, our exhibition posters are already out, the invitations have already been mailed, the media have already been notified. And you're telling me the venue's no longer available?"
"I know, I know." Secretary-General Bai raised a hand in a placating gesture. "That's why I'm helping you find a way around it. There are several alternative venues in Nancheng — I've made a list. Take a look."
He pushed the document across to them. Qu Ya picked it up to read, and Su Qingyuan leaned over. Several alternative venues — too small, too out of the way, or facilities too outdated. Not a single one was worthy of this exhibition.
Qu Ya set the document down. "Secretary-General Bai, none of these venues will do."
Secretary-General Bai's expression stiffened. "Teacher Qu, I know these venues aren't on par with the museum, but these are special circumstances—"
"Special circumstances aren't our problem." Su Qingyuan took up the thread, her tone calm but resolute. "Secretary-General Bai, our exhibition posters say 'Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art.' The invitations say 'Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art.' The press release the media put out says 'Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art' too. Changing the venue at the last minute isn't as simple as swapping one place for another — it's a disrespect to every invited guest, and a disrespect to our work."
Secretary-General Bai looked at her, a hint of appraisal entering his gaze. Clearly, he hadn't expected Su Qingyuan to speak so directly. He'd probably assumed she was just a "newcomer" tagging along on Qu Ya's exhibition, someone he could fob off with any old excuse.
"Miss Su, I understand how you feel, but the city's arrangement—" "Secretary-General Bai," Su Qingyuan cut him off, "we'll handle the venue ourselves. No need to trouble you." Secretary-General Bai was taken aback. "We'll find our own venue. If we find one, the exhibition goes on as planned. If we don't, we can postpone. But those alternative venues — we won't be using them."
Qu Ya turned to look at Su Qingyuan, an emotion in her eyes that was hard to name — something like surprise, and something like admiration.
Secretary-General Bai was silent for a few seconds, then nodded. "Very well. Miss Su, I respect your decision. If you find a suitable venue and need the association's help, come to me anytime."
Walking out of the association building, Qu Ya caught hold of Su Qingyuan. "Qingyuan, do you have a venue in mind?"
"No."
"Then you—"
"I'll find one." Su Qingyuan looked into Qu Ya's eyes. "Sister Ya, this exhibition isn't just my show, and it isn't just your show. It's 'ours.' I won't let it be held in some place that isn't worthy of it."
Qu Ya looked at her, silent for a few seconds, then laughed. "All right. Find it. I'll look with you."
The two of them stood on the steps of the association building, Nancheng's winter wind howling past, whipping their hair into disarray. But Su Qingyuan didn't hunch into herself. She tilted her head back and looked up at the gray, overcast sky. She would find a venue worthy of "Roots and Light." Not out of stubbornness — out of refusing to settle. Her work didn't settle, Qu Ya's photographs didn't settle, and this exhibition wouldn't settle either.
Over the next three days, Su Qingyuan and Qu Ya scoured every venue in Nancheng that could possibly host an exhibition.
Art museums, art centers, creative spaces converted from old factories, the banquet halls of five-star hotels — even a few private clubs. They'd look at one, reject one. Too small, too dark, too noisy, too many columns, ceilings too low, location too remote. Every venue had a fatal flaw; not a single one was worthy of "Roots and Light."
On the evening of the third day, Su Qingyuan sat in her studio, a map of Nancheng spread out before her, covered in red crosses. Every red cross was a venue that had been rejected. She leaned back in her chair, gazing at the ceiling, and let out a long breath.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Lu Shiyan: "Have you eaten?"
Su Qingyuan: "No. Not hungry."
Lu Shiyan: "I'm at the door."
Su Qingyuan blinked, stood up, walked to the door, and pulled it open. Lu Shiyan stood outside, an insulated bag in his hand, wearing a black coat, his scarf blown crooked by the wind. Looking at him, her nose suddenly stung. "Why are you here?"
"You haven't eaten a proper meal in three days." Lu Shiyan walked in and set the insulated bag on the table. "If I didn't come over, were you planning to starve yourself to death?"
Su Qingyuan said nothing — because she had, in fact, planned to finish looking at the map before eating, and after looking at the map she probably wouldn't have wanted to eat at all.
Lu Shiyan opened the insulated bag: a bowl of steaming little wontons, a bowl of red-bean tangyuan, a small dish of pickles. He pushed the wontons in front of her. "Eat first. Once you're done, I'll help you find a venue."
Su Qingyuan picked up the wontons and took a sip of the broth. The savory chicken soup melted on her tongue, warmth traveling all the way down her throat to her stomach. Her eyes suddenly felt hot. "Lu Shiyan, do you know what kind of venue I'm looking for?"
"No. But tell me, and I'll know."
Su Qingyuan set down the bowl and pushed the map in front of him. "The venue I'm looking for needs ceilings at least six meters high, no columns, good natural light, a location that's not too remote, convenient transport, the ability to display both jewelry and photography at once, and a professional lighting system. None of the ones I've seen will do."
Lu Shiyan looked at the red crosses on the map, silent for a few seconds. "There's one place that meets every single one of your requirements."
"Where?"
"The Lu Group headquarters. The ground-floor lobby."
Su Qingyuan froze.
"Eight-meter ceilings, no columns, an entire floor-to-ceiling glass wall, excellent natural light. It's in the core of the CBD, easy to get to. The Lu showroom has hosted countless high-end exhibitions; the lighting system is professional." Lu Shiyan's tone was even, as if stating a simple fact. "And — no charge."
Su Qingyuan looked at him, opened her mouth to say something, but her throat felt as though something were lodged in it. "You... why didn't you say so sooner?"
"You didn't ask before."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head, looked at the red crosses on the map, and suddenly laughed. She'd searched for three days, crossed all of Nancheng, looked at dozens of venues, and not one would do. And the best venue had been beside her the whole time. It wasn't a case of being "blindest under the lamp" — it was that it had never once occurred to her that his company headquarters could become her exhibition hall.
"Lu Shiyan, are you helping me, or being romantic?"
"Helping you." Lu Shiyan reached out and gently wiped a bit of wonton broth from the corner of her mouth. "The romance is just a bonus."
Su Qingyuan laughed and gave him a light punch.
The next day, Su Qingyuan and Qu Ya went to the Lu Group headquarters. Lu Shiyan didn't accompany them; he had his assistant take them to see the venue while he stayed up in his office. He said, "This is your exhibition. If I go, people will think you got the venue through your connection to me." Su Qingyuan understood what he meant. His connections could be used, but no one could be allowed to think her joint exhibition only came together because of his connections. So she went alone, with Qu Ya, in the identity of "Su Qingyuan" — not "Lu Shiyan's girlfriend."
The ground-floor lobby was larger and brighter than Su Qingyuan remembered. An entire floor-to-ceiling glass curtain wall faced Nancheng's skyline, sunlight pouring in from every direction, lighting the whole space through and warm. The floor was pale gray marble, clean as a mirror, mirroring the silhouettes of those who passed. The ceiling looked to be at least eight meters high, not a single column in sight, the view as open as a giant transparent box.
Qu Ya stood in the center of the lobby, turned a full circle, then turned back to look at Su Qingyuan, her eyes full of light. "Qingyuan, this place... it's perfect."
Su Qingyuan smiled too. She took out her phone and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "Venue's settled. This is the one. Thank you."
Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "You're welcome. By the way — the ground-floor lobby's schedule is open, so use it however you like."
Su Qingyuan looked at the words "by the way" and smiled.
With the venue settled, the preparations for the exhibition entered their final sprint. Su Qingyuan and Qu Ya practically lived in the ground-floor lobby of the Lu Group — lighting calibration, booth layout, traffic flow, security plans; every detail had to be confirmed and reconfirmed. Lu Shiyan would come down to take a look every day when he got off work, but he never disturbed them — he'd just stand at a distance and watch for a while, then head back up to keep working.
Sometimes, when Su Qingyuan looked up, she'd see him standing at the entrance of the lobby, hands tucked in his coat pockets, gazing quietly in her direction. She'd wave at him; he'd give a slight nod, then turn and leave. This feeling of being watched but not disturbed gave her a deep sense of steadiness — he was there, but not an interference. He was there, but not in charge. He was simply there.
Three days before the exhibition opened, the unexpected happened.
Su Qingyuan got a call from a number she didn't recognize. When she answered, it was a young woman's voice on the other end, her tone carrying a deliberate politeness. "Miss Su, I'm a reporter with Art and Design magazine. My name is Lin Xiaoxi. We're working on a feature about 'crossover joint exhibitions,' and we'd like to interview you and Teacher Qu Ya. Would that be convenient for you?"
Something stirred in Su Qingyuan's mind. Art and Design was one of the country's top art magazines; a feature on the exhibition there would be a huge boost to publicity. "Reporter Lin, the exhibition's media day is set for the day before the opening. Teacher Qu Ya will be there that day, so you can come interview us then."
"Of course, thank you, Miss Su. There's one more thing — I heard your exhibition venue was changed from the Nancheng Museum of Contemporary Art to the Lu Group headquarters. Was that because the museum's hall was requisitioned by the city? Or is there another reason?"
Su Qingyuan's fingers tightened slightly. She hadn't announced this news publicly yet. Even though the venue had changed, she and Qu Ya had agreed to release the news all at once, only after everything was finalized. How did this reporter know?
"Reporter Lin, you're very well informed. The venue has indeed changed; we'll explain the specific reasons in a press release later."
"Of course, thank you, Miss Su. One more question — there's a rumor going around online that you changed venues because the museum felt your work 'wasn't up to standard' and 'wasn't worthy of the main hall.' Do you have any response to that?"
Su Qingyuan's heart gave a lurch. "Reporter Lin, I won't respond to baseless rumors like that."
"Miss Su, if you don't respond, people will assume you're tacitly admitting it."
Su Qingyuan's grip on the phone tightened further. She took a deep breath, her tone calm. "Reporter Lin, whether my work is up to standard — come see for yourself on opening day. If there's nothing else, I'll hang up now. See you on media day."
After hanging up, Su Qingyuan immediately searched online. Sure enough, a few small accounts were spreading the same story — "Su Qingyuan and Qu Ya's joint exhibition turned away by the museum, last-minute venue change, suspected substandard work quality." It hadn't spread widely yet, but it had already sparked a small ripple of discussion within the industry.
Su Qingyuan put down her phone, leaned back in her chair, and closed her eyes. She'd originally planned to announce the venue change in another couple of days. Now it was clear she had to do it ahead of schedule.
She called Qu Ya and laid out the whole thing. After listening, Qu Ya was quiet for a few seconds. "Qingyuan, who do you think is pulling strings behind this?"
"I don't know. But the only ones who could know both that the museum's venue was requisitioned and that we were changing venues are a handful of people. Secretary-General Bai, his assistant, the two of us, and Lu Shiyan."
"It can't be Lu Shiyan."
"I know. It can't be Secretary-General Bai either — he has no motive. His assistant — I don't know that person."
"I'll look into it." Qu Ya's voice turned grave. "On your end, announce the venue change early. Don't let the rumors keep festering."
"All right."
Su Qingyuan hung up, opened her laptop, and started writing the press release. She didn't respond to the rumors in the statement; she simply stated a fact objectively — that due to the original venue being temporarily requisitioned by the city, the exhibition's location had been changed to the ground-floor lobby of the Lu Group headquarters. With thanks to the Lu Group for its support of the arts. The opening time was unchanged, only the location; their understanding was appreciated. Once finished, she sent it to Qu Ya for confirmation, then released it through Qu Ya Studio's official account.
Within an hour of posting, the reshares passed ten thousand. The tone of the comment section shifted from doubt to understanding — "So it was requisitioned by the city, not that they got kicked out." "The Lu Group is sponsoring the venue? Su Qingyuan's got that much clout?" "It's not clout, it's the work. Lu Shiyan is her boyfriend, but the Lu Group sponsoring the exhibition isn't because she's Lu Shiyan's girlfriend — it's because this show is worth it."
Su Qingyuan looked at those comments and let out a breath of relief. But she also knew the people spreading the rumors wouldn't stop there. They'd switch angles, switch tactics, and keep flinging mud at her exhibition.
She wasn't afraid. Because she held the hardest card of all — her work.
Two days until the exhibition opened.
Su Qingyuan was making the final booth adjustments in the Lu Group's ground-floor lobby. All five pieces of the "Roots" series were in place, displayed in five custom-made cases. The lighting angle of each case had been calibrated over and over, to present the gemstones' most perfect luster.
Qu Ya's photographs hung on the walls — twenty of them, each one personally chosen and finalized by her. The two women's works intertwined throughout the space — not "yours over there, mine over here," but "yours here, mine there, meeting in the middle."
Su Qingyuan stood in the center of the lobby, turned a full circle, taking in the works, the photographs, the lighting, and a scalding emotion welled up inside her. Two months. From inspiration to finished piece, from concept to reality, she had taken two months. In those two months she'd stayed up countless nights, ground countless stones, revised the plans dozens of times. Now, at last, they were about to meet the world.
"Beautiful." Lu Shiyan's voice came from behind her. Su Qingyuan turned and saw him standing at the lobby entrance, two cups of coffee in his hands. "When did you come down?"
"Just now. I saw you spacing out, so I didn't call to you." Lu Shiyan walked over and handed her the coffee. "Americano, no sugar."
Su Qingyuan took the coffee and drank a sip, the bitterness blooming on her tongue, and she suddenly realized the taste was very familiar. A long time ago, the first time he invited her up to the eighty-eighth floor for coffee, she'd taken a sip of the Americano and frowned at the bitterness, and he'd immediately had it swapped for a latte. Today he'd bought an Americano again — because he knew she'd grown used to the bitterness.
"Lu Shiyan, do you think this exhibition will be a success?"
"It will."
"How do you know?"
"Because you're in it."
Su Qingyuan looked at him and smiled. "Not because the venue is at your company?"
"The venue doesn't matter. What matters is the work. Your work is worthy of anywhere."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head and looked at her own reflection in the coffee cup. Then she raised her head, looked at the works about to be exhibited, and said, "Tomorrow. It's tomorrow now."
"Mm. Tomorrow."
The two of them stood side by side in the center of the lobby, surrounded by the works about to make their debut. The light fell over them, casting their two shadows on the floor, close together — so close they nearly overlapped.
Su Qingyuan suddenly remembered a long time ago, when she was a little girl, sitting on the attic floor of the old Su family house, drawing on a crumpled sheet of paper. What she drew was light. Back then she didn't know that one day, that light would become real.
Now she knew.
[System: This chapter — the exhibition venue is temporarily requisitioned, Secretary-General Bai offers alternatives but of poor quality, the heroine refuses to settle, and Lu Shiyan proactively offers the ground-floor lobby of the Lu Group. Reporter Lin Xiaoxi leaks the venue change early and asks pointed questions, hinting that someone is pulling strings behind the scenes. The heroine announces the venue change ahead of time, quelling part of the doubt. Two days left on the exhibition countdown; all the works are in place. Next chapter, the exhibition opens. It may be the climax — the works' debut, industry reviews, media interviews, and perhaps a new twist (such as the rumor-monger appearing). Sweetness on the sly — little wontons, red-bean tangyuan, Americano, his "because you're in it." The System is on standby.]

========================================

Chapter 36 — Roots and Light, the Day They Bloom
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626238.html

Roots and Light, the Day They Bloom
On the morning the joint exhibition opened, a thin snow fell over Nancheng.
Su Qingyuan stood at the floor-to-ceiling windows of the Lu Group lobby, watching the fine flakes drift down through the morning light. The snow was light, melting the instant it touched the ground, but that hushed, white-washed atmosphere made the whole city look like an ink-wash painting. She wore a black long-sleeved dress with that smoke-gray cashmere coat over it, her hair pinned up to reveal the pair of "First Snow" earrings. She had on no other jewelry, because today the star wasn't her—it was her work.
"Nervous?" Lu Shiyan's voice came from behind her. Su Qingyuan turned to see him standing at the lobby entrance in a black suit and a dark gray tie, looking even more nobly aloof than usual. But his hands were tucked into his trouser pockets, his fingertips curled slightly—he was nervous. Nervous for her.
"A little," Su Qingyuan admitted. "But more than that, I'm looking forward to it."
Lu Shiyan walked over and stood in front of her, bending his head to straighten the collar of her coat. "A lot of people will come today. Reporters, critics, collectors, people from the industry. Whatever they say, don't take it to heart. Your work—you know exactly what it's worth."
Su Qingyuan looked at his earnest expression and smiled. "Are you giving me a pep talk?"
"Yes."
"When did you learn how to do that?"
"From the day I met you. I learn a little more every day."
Su Qingyuan reached out, laughing, and adjusted his tie. "Your tie's crooked."
Lu Shiyan looked down at her hand, the corner of his mouth lifting. "Thank you."
At nine in the morning, the guests began to arrive.
The first was Zhou Wanyin. She wore a dark green gown and carried a bouquet of white chamomile, and the moment she spotted Su Qingyuan she hurried over and pressed the flowers into her arms. "These are for you. An opening gift. I didn't buy them—I grew them myself."
Su Qingyuan looked down at the bouquet, the tiny blossoms crowded together, clean and warm. "Sister Wanyin, when did you start growing flowers?"
"After you went to Paris. I had nothing to do, so I grew them for fun." Zhou Wanyin smiled. "Turns out they grew rather well. Just like you—give them a little sunlight and they bloom."
Su Qingyuan laughed, her eyes growing a little hot. She set the flowers down on the sign-in table and turned to link her arm through Zhou Wanyin's. "Come on, let me show you around."
In the exhibition hall, Qu Ya was already greeting the first batch of guests. Today she wore a white shirt and black wide-leg trousers, her hair cut short, looking crisp and capable. Seeing Zhou Wanyin, she came over with a smile. "Wanyin, long time no see."
"Sister Ya, you look wonderful today."
"None of that flattery." Qu Ya laughed and gave Zhou Wanyin a light pat, then turned to Su Qingyuan. "Qingyuan, Secretary-General Bai is here."
Su Qingyuan followed her gaze. Secretary-General Bai was standing before the first piece of the "Roots" series, bent at the waist, peering at the tree-ring brooch through a magnifying glass. His expression was intent, his brow slightly furrowed, as if he were puzzling over some profound question.
Su Qingyuan walked over. "Secretary-General Bai, welcome."
Secretary-General Bai straightened, lowered the magnifying glass, and looked at her. "Miss Su, the tree-ring section of this piece—you used six kinds of gemstones?"
"Yes. At the very center is a dark brown diamond, then moving outward, in order: deep green emerald, golden sapphire, pale yellow diamond, white sapphire, and the outermost layer is a clear diamond."
"Each kind of gem represents one ring of growth?"
"That's right. From origin to growth, from struggle to settling, from settling to protection. The story of a tree is also the story of a person."
Secretary-General Bai was silent for a few seconds, then nodded. "Miss Su, about the venue earlier—that was an oversight on my part. Having seen your work today, I take back what I said before. Your work deserves to be shown anywhere."
Warmth bloomed in Su Qingyuan's chest. "Secretary-General Bai, thank you."
Secretary-General Bai waved a hand and turned to go on to the next piece.
By ten in the morning, the hall was growing more and more crowded. Su Qingyuan stood in the "Roots and Light" intersection zone—a space designed specifically to stage a dialogue between her work and Qu Ya's. On the left was Qu Ya's photograph: the root base of a great tree, thick roots driven deep into the earth, like an old man's hands, like the veins of time. On the right was Su Qingyuan's fourth "Roots" series piece—a necklace called "Veins," woven from antiqued silver and bright gemstones, like roots spreading underground, like blood flowing through the body.
The photograph and the necklace placed side by side—one the roots of nature, one the "roots" within the human body—the same theme, expressed differently. But set together, they seemed to be in conversation.
A collector stood before this zone for a long while, then turned to ask Su Qingyuan, "Miss Su, is this necklace for sale?"
Su Qingyuan shook her head. "The 'Roots' series is not for sale. These are pieces I made for myself. They're not on the market."
The collector was taken aback for a moment, then smiled. "What a pity. But I understand—some pieces are kept to warm yourself by."
Su Qingyuan nodded. Yes—the "Roots" series was kept to warm herself by.
At eleven in the morning, the reporter Lin Xiaoxi appeared in the hall. She wore a red dress and held a recording pen, a photographer trailing behind her. The instant Su Qingyuan saw her, she remembered the sharp questions on the phone that day, and her chest tightened slightly—but nothing showed on her face.
"Miss Su, congratulations." Lin Xiaoxi came over with a professional smile. "The exhibition is a great success."
"Thank you."
"Could I interview you for a few minutes?"
"Of course."
They moved to a quiet corner of the hall. Lin Xiaoxi switched on her recording pen and asked a few standard questions—creative inspiration, the theme of the joint exhibition, how it felt to collaborate with Qu Ya. Su Qingyuan answered each one, neither haughty nor humble.
Then Lin Xiaoxi changed tack. "Miss Su, there's a tip going around online that you switched venues not because the art museum was requisitioned, but because the museum thought your work wasn't 'up to standard.' How do you respond to that?"
Su Qingyuan looked into Lin Xiaoxi's eyes. In those eyes there was curiosity, there was probing, and there was a faint, barely perceptible—excitement. Like a hunter who had finally cornered her prey.
Su Qingyuan didn't shy away from that gaze. She said calmly, "Reporter Lin, you say there's a tip going around online. Can you tell me who tipped you off? Is there any evidence?"
Lin Xiaoxi hesitated. "This… the source is anonymous."
"Then there's no evidence." Su Qingyuan's voice was soft but perfectly clear. "I won't respond to a tip with no evidence. But I can tell you one thing—among the guests here today are Mr. Jean-Pierre, creative director of Cartier headquarters; the editor-in-chief of Vogue's jewelry section; and Madame Isabelle Mollier, chair of the Biennale judging committee. They didn't come because of any connection to me—they came because of my work. If my work weren't 'up to standard,' they wouldn't be here."
Lin Xiaoxi's expression froze for a moment. She clearly hadn't expected Su Qingyuan to push back so directly, and to invoke names like Jean-Pierre and Isabelle—top-tier figures in the industry. The weight of those names was heavier than any defense.
"Reporter Lin, if you want to write a piece about the joint exhibition, you're welcome to view it carefully and write something objective, something with depth. But if you're just looking for a gimmick, digging for dirt, then you've come to the wrong person." Su Qingyuan paused. "My work doesn't need dirt to set it off. And your article doesn't need a gimmick to draw eyes."
Lin Xiaoxi's color changed. From red to white, from white to red. She opened her mouth as if to say something, but in the end nothing came out. She switched off her recording pen and nodded. "Miss Su, I understand. Thank you for the interview."
She turned and left. The photographer shouldered his equipment and followed, glancing back at Su Qingyuan once, his eyes holding both sympathy and admiration.
Su Qingyuan stood where she was, watching Lin Xiaoxi's back vanish into the crowd, feeling no anger—only a strange calm. Lin Xiaoxi wasn't a bad person; she was just a reporter trying to stand out in fierce competition, who had chosen the easiest route—digging up dirt. But Su Qingyuan wasn't her dirt. Su Qingyuan was her blind spot—a person with no dirt to dig up, who let her work speak for itself.
At noon, the guests moved to the adjacent lounge area for lunch. Su Qingyuan didn't go to eat; she held a glass of water and stood in a corner of the hall, watching the people come and go. They stood before her work—some nodding, some smiling, some frowning, some murmuring to their companions. She took in every expression.
"Not going to eat?" Qu Ya came over, holding a plate of sandwiches.
"Not hungry."
"Too nervous to eat?"
Su Qingyuan smiled. "Maybe."
Qu Ya handed her the sandwich. "Eat something. There are more important guests this afternoon."
"Who?"
"The director of the Municipal Bureau of Culture, and a few Nancheng officials." Qu Ya's tone was flat, as if she were mentioning something trivial. "They heard the joint exhibition was being held at Lu Group headquarters and wanted to come take a look."
Su Qingyuan was startled. The director of the Municipal Bureau of Culture? Nancheng officials? People you couldn't get to come even if you begged—and today they were asking to come of their own accord?
"Sister Ya, did you invite them?"
"No. Lu Shiyan did." Qu Ya watched Su Qingyuan's expression and smiled. "But not by pulling strings. It's because this exhibition is worth seeing. Lu Shiyan just put in a word; they looked at the materials and decided to come on their own."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head and took a bite of the sandwich. The bread was a little dry, the ham a little salty, but she chewed it carefully, because Qu Ya had handed it to her.
At two in the afternoon, the director of the Municipal Bureau of Culture and several officials arrived. Su Qingyuan received them personally, walking them through the entire hall and explaining in detail the creative concept and craftsmanship of every piece. The director was a woman in her fifties, short-haired, without makeup, with a capable air. She stood for a long time before the fifth piece of the "Roots" series—a necklace called "Source," whose main stone was an uncut, raw gemstone that kept its natural cracks and inclusions.
"Miss Su, this piece—why isn't it cut?"
"Because 'Source' means the beginning. At the beginning, none of us is perfect. This stone isn't perfect, but it's the most truthful. I wanted to preserve that truth."
The director was silent for a few seconds, then turned to look at her. "Miss Su, your work makes me think of one word—'sincerity.'"
The word seared Su Qingyuan.
"Sincerity. Not technique, not splendor—it's taking yourself out and laying yourself bare for others to see." The director paused. "Your work has that."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot, but she didn't cry. She nodded. "Thank you."
As the director left, she shook Su Qingyuan's hand and said, "Keep it up," then turned and went. Su Qingyuan stood at the lobby door, watching the black sedan drive out of the parking lot and disappear around the corner. She looked down at her hand—the one the director had just shaken—and the warmth of the other woman's palm still lingered on it.
At four in the afternoon, the guests began to leave one after another. The hall gradually grew quiet, with only a few staff left tidying up the materials. Su Qingyuan stood alone in the center of the hall, looking at the pieces, the lighting, the empty aisles, and was suddenly seized by a feeling of unreality.
"Is it over?" she murmured to herself.
"It's over." Qu Ya came up behind her, holding two glasses of champagne. "But it's also a beginning."
She handed one glass to Su Qingyuan. "Congratulations, Qingyuan. The joint exhibition was a success."
Su Qingyuan took the champagne and touched it to Qu Ya's glass. "Congratulations to us."
The two of them drank, the champagne bubbles rising in the glasses like countless tiny stars.
"Sister Ya, how do you think today went?"
"Very well." Qu Ya looked at the pieces in the hall. "But tomorrow will be better. And the day after, better still. Because people will keep coming to this exhibition."
Su Qingyuan nodded. Yes—today was only the beginning. Over the coming month, this hall would welcome more and more visitors. Their work would be seen, discussed, and remembered by more people.
"Qingyuan, I'm heading off. It's been a long day—I'm going home to soak in a bath." Qu Ya set down her glass and patted Su Qingyuan's shoulder. "You should head home and rest too. There's still another day tomorrow."
"Okay. Sister Ya, thank you for all your hard work today."
"You too."
Qu Ya left. Only Su Qingyuan and a few staff remained in the hall. She walked to the "Roots and Light" intersection zone and stood for a long time between the photograph of the great tree and the "Veins" necklace.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Lu Shiyan: "Done?"
Su Qingyuan: "Done. Where are you?"
Lu Shiyan: "Eighty-eighth floor. Come up?"
Su Qingyuan: "Okay."
She took the elevator up to the eighty-eighth floor. The instant the doors opened, she saw Lu Shiyan standing at the end of the corridor, in front of that pair of dark wooden double doors. He had changed clothes—no longer in the formal black suit from this morning, but in a dark gray cashmere sweater and black trousers. He looked far softer.
"You've worked hard today," he said.
"It wasn't hard." Su Qingyuan walked over. "Your venue was very useful."
"You're welcome. It was nothing."
Su Qingyuan smiled and followed him into the office. Beyond the floor-to-ceiling windows, dusk was settling over Nancheng, an orange-red glow spilling across the entire sky. In the distance, the creative park, the Cartier building, the Hunan restaurant, the art museum, Qu Ya's studio, her apartment, his home—the whole city spread out beneath them, like a vast, living painting.
"Lu Shiyan, the sunset's beautiful today."
"Mm. Just like you."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. The sunset fell across his face, his profile especially gentle in the dusk. "When did you learn to say things like that?"
"From the day I met you. I learn a little more every day."
Su Qingyuan laughed and leaned against his shoulder, watching the sunset beyond the window. "Lu Shiyan, do you think we'll always be like this from now on?"
"Like what?"
"Watching sunsets together. Eating together. Doing lots of boring things together."
Lu Shiyan lowered his head, his lips pressed to the crown of her hair. "We will. Always."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes, feeling his warmth. Beyond the window, the sun sank slowly, the sky shifting from orange-red to deep purple, from deep purple to inky blue. The city's lights came on one by one, joining into a warm sea of light.
She suddenly remembered a long time ago—a rainy night, when she'd stood at the roadside, not knowing where she was meant to go. Now she knew. The place she was meant to go was wherever he was.
On the third day, the joint exhibition welcomed its first weekend.
The crowds were even larger than on opening day. The hall was packed—jewelry enthusiasts who'd traveled in from out of town, photography students, young parents with their children, white-haired old couples. Su Qingyuan didn't make an appearance; she stood at the floor-to-ceiling windows on the eighty-eighth floor, watching the people queuing below to get in, and felt something very strange in her heart—not pride, but emotion.
These people were complete strangers to her. They were willing to spend their time, their money, their energy to come see her work. Not because she was famous, but because her work had moved them.
"Not going down to take a look?" Lu Shiyan stood beside her.
"No. Let them look in peace." Su Qingyuan turned to look at his office. "You're not working today?"
"It's the weekend."
"You work on weekends too."
"Not today."
Su Qingyuan looked at him and smiled. "Because I'm coming over?"
"Mm."
Su Qingyuan walked over and kissed him on the cheek. "Thank you."
Lu Shiyan touched the spot where she'd kissed him, the corner of his mouth lifting. "You're welcome."
They sat on the sofa, drinking coffee, looking out at the city. Below were the queuing crowds; above were two quiet people. One above, one below, one moving, one still—like two parallel worlds.
But Su Qingyuan knew these two worlds were connected. Because her work was the bridge that joined them.
In the second week of the exhibition, Su Qingyuan received a letter from Australia. The envelope bore an Australian stamp, the handwriting crooked and uneven—obviously written by Su Yurou.
She opened the letter; inside was only a photograph. It showed a beach, blue sky and emerald sea, with a line of footprints on the white sand winding forward, no end in sight. On the back of the photo was a single line: "A beach in Melbourne. No auroras, but the sunshine is lovely. I think you'd like it. —Yurou"
Su Qingyuan looked at that line for a long time. She put the photograph in a drawer, locked away with those old photos, the USB drive, the envelope. Then she picked up her phone and sent Su Yurou a message—not a reply, but a new conversation: "The Melbourne beach is lovely. The auroras are up north; you can't see them from where you are. If you want to see them, go to Norway. But the sunshine in the Southern Hemisphere is lovely too. Each has its own beauty."
After sending it, she put down her phone and went back to work. The sunlight beyond the window was lovely, falling on her hands, on the gemstone taking shape there, on the five completed pieces of the "Roots" series.
Everything was moving forward. So was she.
In the third week of the exhibition, Su Qingyuan got a call from Jean-Pierre.
"Miss Su, the global tour of the 'Light Traces' collection—the first stop in Paris had an excellent response. New York can hardly wait; they're asking whether we can move it up."
"Move it up to when?"
"March next year."
Su Qingyuan thought it over. "That works. No problem on my end."
