"Then it's settled. March, New York." Jean-Pierre paused. "Miss Su, one more thing. Cartier wants to sign a long-term guest artist agreement with you—not one season a year, but three years. One collection each year, led entirely by you."
Su Qingyuan gripped her phone, her heart racing. "And the split?"
"Sixty for you, forty for Cartier. Same as before."
Su Qingyuan was silent for a few seconds. "Jean-Pierre, I accept. But on one condition."
"What condition?"
"The 'Roots' series—I want to add a stop to the tour. A China stop. Not Shanghai, not Beijing—Nancheng. Because the roots of the 'Roots' series are in Nancheng."
Jean-Pierre was silent for a moment, then laughed. "Miss Su, you're the first designer ever to make such a condition to Cartier. But I'll grant it. Because the 'Roots' series deserves to be shown on its own soil."
After hanging up, Su Qingyuan stood at the window, looking at the osmanthus tree outside. Spring hadn't arrived yet; the branches were still bare. But she could see that the tiny, tender green buds had already, quietly, begun to swell.
Spring was coming.
On the last day of the exhibition, Su Qingyuan went back to the hall once more. Not to work—to say goodbye.
There weren't many people in the hall, since it was a weekday. She walked alone among the pieces, looking at her five works and Qu Ya's twenty photographs, with so much she wanted to say but no one to say it to. At last she stopped at the "Roots and Light" intersection zone, looking at the photograph of the great tree and the "Veins" necklace.
"Thank you," she said softly. "For walking this stretch of road with me."
The exhibition ran for a month, drew over fifty thousand visitors, and garnered more than three hundred media reports, with industry reviews almost entirely positive. One critic wrote a long piece titled "Roots and Light: Su Qingyuan and Her 'Nonfiction' Jewelry." The last line of the article read: "Su Qingyuan's work isn't telling a fictional, perfect story. She's telling her own story—imperfect, but true. And truth is the scarcest thing of this era."
After reading that article, Su Qingyuan saved it. Not on her phone—in her heart.
That evening, Lu Shiyan came to pick her up. The car didn't head toward her apartment; it headed somewhere else—the tallest building in Nancheng, his home.
"We're going to your place?" Su Qingyuan asked.
"Mm. There's something I want to show you."
The elevator rose; the doors opened. Lu Shiyan went in first, Su Qingyuan following, changing into that pair of pale pink fuzzy slippers. The living room lights were still that warm yellow, white chamomile still sat on the coffee table, and the pale beige throw still lay draped over the sofa. Nothing had changed—but Su Qingyuan noticed that there was now a small box on the coffee table. Black, velvet, the kind that held jewelry.
"What's this?"
"Open it and see."
Su Qingyuan picked up the box and opened it. Inside was a necklace—not one she had designed. The chain was fine white gold, and the pendant was a small moonstone, cut in the technique she knew best—the Ripple Cut. The halo radiated outward from the core ring by ring, three graded layers: deep blue on the outside, silver-white in the middle, warm gold within. Exactly like the moonstone in her "Daybreak."
Su Qingyuan's hands trembled. "This is…"
"I had it made. Using your technique." Lu Shiyan's voice was low. "I asked Director Chen of Zhenyi to help. She said it was the hardest necklace she'd ever made. Because the parameters of the Ripple Cut are yours alone—she didn't dare ask you for them, so she tried it herself. She tried for two months and ruined over a hundred moonstones before she made this one."
Su Qingyuan's tears fell.
"Su Qingyuan, this necklace is called 'Light.'" Lu Shiyan lifted the necklace from the box, stepped around behind her, and fastened it for her. His fingers brushed the skin at the back of her neck, cool, but her heart was burning.
"Why 'Light'?" she asked, her voice a little hoarse.
"Because you're my light." Lu Shiyan fastened the clasp, came back around in front of her, and looked down at her. "From the glass door until now, you always have been."
Su Qingyuan reached up to touch the moonstone at her throat, her vision blurred with tears. "Lu Shiyan, you really are insufferable."
"Mm."
"You pull this same trick every time."
"As long as it works."
Su Qingyuan laughed through her tears. She rose onto her toes, wrapped both arms around his neck, and kissed him.
Beyond the window, Nancheng's night view spread out beneath them, ten thousand household lights joining into a warm sea of light. In the distance, somewhere she couldn't see, her studio was still there, the osmanthus tree was still there, the five pieces of the "Roots" series were still there.
But she knew that tomorrow she would carry new work, new stories, a new self, and keep moving forward.
Because the light was ahead. And he was in the light.
[System: This chapter—the joint exhibition opens, with climaxes piling one upon another. Secretary-General Bai gives his approval; the reporter Lin Xiaoxi is rebutted to her face by the heroine; the director of the Bureau of Culture offers the verdict of "sincerity." The career arc advances: Cartier's three-year long-term agreement, and the global tour adding a Nancheng stop. The romance arc: Lu Shiyan commissions the "Light" necklace using the heroine's Ripple Cut technique, confessing, "You are my light." Su Yurou sends a photograph from Australia, the heroine replies, and that thread is drawn to a close. In the end the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition is a complete success, and the heroine's career climbs another step. Next stop, the auroras.]

========================================

Chapter 37 — The Aurora, and His Promise
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626239.html

Three days after the joint exhibition ended, Su Qingyuan was packing up at her studio. Not moving out—packing for a trip to the Nordics. Lu Shiyan had said the matter of the aurora was best done sooner rather than later, and he'd booked tickets for three days out. At the time she'd thought he was joking, right up until he forwarded her the flight details and she realized he was serious.
"You really have the time?" she asked him.
"I took leave."
"You take leave? Aren't you the man who works every day of the year?"
"I used to be. Now I have a reason to take some off."
Su Qingyuan looked at the message, the corners of her mouth curving up, slipped her phone into her pocket, and went back to folding clothes. She packed a lot—a thick down jacket, thermal underwear, wool socks, scarves, gloves, hats. Lu Shiyan messaged that it was minus twenty degrees up north and told her to bundle up. She wrapped herself into a ball to test it, decided it still wasn't enough, and stuffed in two more fleece-lined base layers.
The day they left, it was raining in Nancheng. Su Qingyuan sat in the car, watching the wipers sweep the rain off the windshield again and again, only for it to blur over again and again, and a sense of unreality settled in her chest. A few hours ago she'd still been polishing the samples for the "Roots" series in her studio; now she was off to the Nordics to see the aurora.
"Nervous?" Lu Shiyan asked, hands on the wheel, eyes forward.
"Not nervous," Su Qingyuan said. "It just feels too fast. The exhibition just ended, the Cartier contract was just signed, I haven't even caught my breath."
"Then go before you catch it. Once you do, you'll start thinking, 'I still have so much to do, I can't get away.'"
Su Qingyuan turned her head to look at him. "How do you know I'd think that?"
"Because that's the kind of person you are," Lu Shiyan said, glancing at her. "You always feel there's work you'll never finish. So I'm taking you away before you decide you 'can't get away.'"
Su Qingyuan said nothing. She turned to look out the window; the rain was coming down harder, the whole city wrapped in a gray curtain of it. But inside her was a patch of clear sky—because he was taking her away.
The plane flew from Nancheng to Helsinki, ten hours. Su Qingyuan leaned on Lu Shiyan's shoulder, watching through the porthole as day turned to night, as clouds gave way to stars. She couldn't sleep; too much was turning over in her head—what to do after the "Roots" series, how to arrange the New York stop of the "Light Traces" touring exhibition, what the theme of the first collection under the three-year Cartier agreement should be. These things churned for a long while, then a hand pressed them gently still. Not a real hand—his voice.
"Stop thinking. Close your eyes. Rest."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes. She thought she wouldn't be able to sleep, but before long she sank into a deep slumber.
They changed planes in Helsinki, bound for Ivalo. The plane got smaller, the noise got louder, and the scenery outside shifted from city to forest, from forest to snowfield, from snowfield to a vast expanse of white. Su Qingyuan pressed against the porthole, looking down at the boundless white below, like one enormous, quiet dream.
"Are we there?" she asked.
"We're here."
Ivalo Airport was small—small enough that there was only a single baggage carousel. Su Qingyuan stood beside the belt waiting for her luggage, watching the cluster of fellow tourists waiting too—they wore brightly colored down jackets, chattering excitedly in English, French, Japanese, every face written over with "here to see the aurora." All at once she felt she was one of them. Not Su Qingyuan the designer, not Su Qingyuan, Lu Shiyan's girlfriend—just an ordinary girl come to see the aurora.
The hotel Lu Shiyan had booked was on the outskirts of Ivalo, a glass-igloo resort. Every room was its own freestanding glass dome; lying in bed, you could see the sky. When Su Qingyuan walked into the room, she stood beneath the glass dome and gazed up for a long time. It wasn't dark yet; the sky was a grayish blue, a few birds flying past, leaving blurred shadows on the glass.
"Lie here tonight and you'll see the aurora," Lu Shiyan said, setting down the suitcase. "If we're lucky."
"And if we're not?"
"Then we look again tomorrow. The aurora's there every day—it's just hidden by clouds sometimes."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "How do you know everything?"
"I looked it up."
"How much did you look up?"
"Everything there is on auroras, starting the day I booked the tickets." Lu Shiyan's tone was casual, as if he were mentioning something trivial.
Su Qingyuan looked at him, and her heart went utterly, hopelessly soft.
That evening they had dinner in the hotel restaurant. Reindeer meat, salmon soup, lingonberry sauce—the flavors were unusual, and Su Qingyuan couldn't say whether they were good or not, but she ate carefully, because this was a meal he was sharing with her.
After dinner they went back to the room and changed into warm clothes. Su Qingyuan put on two layers of fleece base layers, a thick sweater, a down jacket, and a windbreaker over it all. Two layers of scarf, two hats, thick gloves, wool socks and snow boots on her feet. She stood in front of the mirror and looked at herself, and thought she resembled a round, roly-poly penguin.
"Do I look good?" she asked.
"You look good." Lu Shiyan stood behind her in a black down jacket, scarf slung carelessly around his neck, looking more than half as bundled as she was.
"You're not cold?"
"Not cold."
"Liar."
Lu Shiyan laughed, reached out and tugged her hat down to cover her ears. "Let's go."
They stepped out of the hotel into a vast, sweeping snowfield. The sky was completely dark now—no moon, no stars, only the faint glow reflected off the snow. The air was cold enough to cut like a knife; the white breath she exhaled gathered into a cloud of fog before her face and lingered, slow to fade. Su Qingyuan trod through the snow, one foot deep, one foot shallow, the snow over her ankles, crunching with every step.
"Where to?" she asked.
"That way. There's an observation deck—best view of all."
They walked about ten minutes and reached a wooden observation deck. No one else was there, only a few ground lamps casting a faint light. Su Qingyuan stood at the deck's edge, looking out at the horizon in the distance. The sky was very dark—dark as one enormous sheet of velvet, without a trace of light.
"No aurora." She felt a little let down.
"Wait."
They waited a long time. Su Qingyuan's feet went numb with cold and her hands stiffened, but she didn't want to go back—because she was afraid that the moment she did, the aurora would come out. Lu Shiyan pulled two hand warmers from his pocket, tore them open, and tucked them into her gloves. "Put your hands in."
Su Qingyuan slid her hands into the gloves; the warmth of the hand warmers seeped slowly into her palms, warming her from her hands to her heart.
"How do you have everything?"
"Saw it while reading up. They say you should bring hand warmers to see the aurora."
Su Qingyuan looked at him and laughed. "Did you read through the entire internet?"
"More or less."
She leaned on his shoulder, watching the sky. It was still very dark, no aurora, but a single cloud drifted slowly over, shifting shape as the wind moved it. Like a bird, like a fish, like a heart. Su Qingyuan looked at that heart-shaped cloud and the corners of her mouth curved up.
"Lu Shiyan, what if we don't see the aurora today?"
"We come again tomorrow."
"And if we don't see it tomorrow?"
"The day after."
"And if we never see it?"
Lu Shiyan looked down at her, the faint glow off the snow falling on her face; in her eyes there was expectation, and also unease. "Then we keep coming. For ten years, twenty, thirty. We'll see it eventually."
Su Qingyuan's nose stung. She lowered her head and buried her face in her scarf. "You—you're really insufferable."
"Mm."
"You always say things like that."
"Because every time, I mean it."
Su Qingyuan fell silent. She leaned on his shoulder and went on waiting.
They waited a long time. Long enough that she thought they wouldn't see it tonight. And then, at the edge of the sky, a band of green light appeared.
Not the overwhelming, sky-filling green of the photographs—a thin band, like a ribbon of green, stretching from one end of the horizon to the other, flowing slowly through the darkness. Su Qingyuan held her breath, afraid that one exhale and it would vanish. But the light didn't vanish; it grew brighter and brighter, from green into pink, from pink into purple, spreading from the horizon to overhead, like an immense, glowing length of silk drifting slowly across the night sky.
Su Qingyuan's tears fell.
Not tears of sorrow—the kind that comes from waiting a long time and finally, finally being rewarded—scalding tears.
"Lu Shiyan, look."
"Mm. I see it."
She turned to look at him, the aurora's light reflected in his eyes—green, pink, purple—as if the entire universe had been gathered into them.
"Su Qingyuan."
"Mm."
"You once said that if you could see the aurora with your own eyes one day, you'd have no regrets left in this life."
Su Qingyuan nodded, tears still flowing.
"Now that you've seen it, do you still have any?"
Su Qingyuan wiped her tears, looked at the flowing light in the sky, and thought about it. "One."
"What?"
"You still haven't said you'll spend a lifetime with me."
Lu Shiyan looked at her, the aurora's light falling on his face, his expression tender and certain. He reached out and gently took her hand, fingers interlaced.
"Su Qingyuan, I'll spend a lifetime with you."
"A lifetime of what?"
"A lifetime—telling you good morning every day, telling you good night every day. Asking you every day what you feel like eating, picking you up from work every day. Bringing you to the Nordics once a year to see the aurora, to ride a sled, to stay in a glass igloo. Going to Paris once a year to eat croissants and see the Wall of Love. Making you a bowl of noodles every year on your birthday. They might not taste good, but I'll keep making them—until they do."
Su Qingyuan laughed through her tears. "Is this a proposal?"
"No."
"Then what is it?"
"A promise. A proposal needs a ring. I didn't bring one."
Su Qingyuan laughed and gave him a light punch. "Then bring one next time."
"Okay. Next time."
The aurora flowed slowly overhead, like a glowing river. Su Qingyuan leaned on his shoulder, watching that light, and felt this life had been worth it. Not because she'd seen the aurora—because the person watching it with her was him.
By the time they got back to the hotel, it was very late. Su Qingyuan peeled off the thick layers, took a hot shower, and lay down in bed. On the glass dome, the aurora was still there, fainter than before but still beautiful, like a painting that moved.
"Lu Shiyan, why do you think the aurora is green?"
"Because charged particles in the solar wind collide with oxygen atoms in the Earth's atmosphere."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "You looked that up too?"
"Mm."
"Could you maybe not be so all-knowing?"
Lu Shiyan laughed. "No."
Su Qingyuan laughed too. She rolled over to face him. The two of them lay beneath the glass dome, watching the aurora overhead, neither saying a word. It was quiet for a long time—long enough that Su Qingyuan thought he'd fallen asleep.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"You said before that a proposal needs a ring. When are you getting ready?"
"Guess."
Su Qingyuan laughed and gave him a punch. "I won't guess. Tell me when you're ready."
"Okay."
She closed her eyes and listened to the sound of his breathing. Outside, the aurora was still there, faintly green, like a lamp that never went out. She suddenly remembered, long ago, a rainy night, when she'd sat on a hotel floor thinking her world was over. Back then she hadn't known that that rain was the end of a storm, and also the beginning of dawn. Now she knew.
The next day, they rode a sled. Huskies pulled the sled racing across the snowfield, fast enough that Su Qingyuan screamed out loud. Lu Shiyan sat behind her, both arms around her waist, saying in her ear, "Hold on tight." She gripped the reins, the wind howling past her ears, snow striking her face, biting cold—but she didn't want to stop.
The third day, they went to the Santa Claus Village. Su Qingyuan wrote a postcard at the post office and mailed it to herself. On the postcard she wrote: "I'm in the Arctic Circle, and I saw the aurora. And him. —Su Qingyuan." She didn't know whether the postcard would ever arrive, but she wanted to send it. Because it was a trace she'd left within the Arctic Circle.
The fourth day, they returned to Nancheng.
As the plane landed, Su Qingyuan looked out at the hazy gray sky and suddenly felt that Nancheng's winter was beautiful too. Not because there was no snow—because he was here.
Back at the studio, everything was as before. The cyclamen on the windowsill had withered and been replaced by a new pot—a white phalaenopsis orchid. Su Qingyuan watered the flower, opened her laptop, and started working through the accumulated tasks. The Cartier contract needed signing, the New York stop of the "Light Traces" tour needed its plan confirmed, the "Roots" series needed its portfolio compiled. There was a lot, but she wasn't anxious. Because she knew that, whatever lay ahead, he would be there.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Lu Shiyan: "What do you feel like eating tonight?"
Su Qingyuan thought for a moment and typed two words: "Hot pot."
Lu Shiyan: "Okay. I'll come pick you up."
Su Qingyuan: "No need, I'll go myself."
Lu Shiyan: "Okay. See you at home."
Su Qingyuan's mouth curved up. She put away her phone and went back to work. Outside, Nancheng's sky was still a hazy gray, but she knew spring would come soon.
[System: This chapter—a Nordic aurora trip. Glass igloos, snowfields, the aurora; the romance dialed all the way up. Lu Shiyan said he'd "spend a lifetime with you"—not a proposal, but better than one. "I'll bring the ring next time" plants a hook. Su Qingyuan mailing a postcard to herself—full of ceremony. Back in Nancheng, life goes on, the career thread hasn't stopped—the Cartier contract, the New York tour. The next chapter might be the New York tour, or the international response to the "Roots" series, or the surprise of him getting the ring ready. The System is on standby.]

========================================

Chapter 38 — The Ring and the Faraway
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626240.html

In the first week back from Scandinavia, Su Qingyuan felt like a clock that had been wound up tight all over again. The final proposal for the Cartier New York tour had to be confirmed before the end of the month, the third production batch of the "Light Traces" collection needed her signature to go ahead, and the portfolio for the "Roots" series was being assembled, ready to submit to the review committee for next year's Venice Biennale. One thing piled onto another, rolling toward her like a snowball. But she didn't feel tired, because all of it was hers by choice.
That evening, Lu Shiyan messaged to say he had a business dinner and couldn't come pick her up. Su Qingyuan replied with an "Okay" and kept working. When she finally lifted her head from the workbench, she realized the sky had gone completely dark. She glanced at her phone — 8:47. Lu Shiyan hadn't messaged; he must still be busy. She packed up her things, locked the door, and walked to the gate of the complex to hail a cab. The early-spring night wind was still cold, and she shrank her neck, tugging her scarf up higher.
The taxi pulled up outside her apartment building. She paid, pushed the door open, and got out. The stairwell lights were on, warm yellow light spilling through the windows and falling across the steps at the entrance. She climbed the steps and was just about to reach for her keys when she suddenly stopped.
There was a box sitting at her door. Small, white, velvet, tied with a champagne-colored ribbon. No sender's name, no card — just that box lying there quietly, like a secret that had been kept for a long time.
Su Qingyuan crouched down, picked up the box, untied the ribbon, and opened it.
A ring.
Not a diamond ring — the center stone was a moonstone, not large, but she knew the cut all too well. The Ripple Cut, three layers of halo: deep blue on the outside, silver-white in the middle, warm gold within. Exactly like the moonstone in her "Daybreak." The band was a thin line of platinum, and engraved on the inside, in a clean, sharp hand, were the words: "From a glass door to the aurora — from now on, it's all you."
Su Qingyuan's tears fell.
She cradled the ring, crouched at her apartment door, and cried like a fool. Not because the ring was so precious, but because he had remembered everything — the glass door, the aurora, every single thing she had ever said.
Her phone buzzed. Lu Shiyan's message: "Did you see the box?"
Su Qingyuan wiped her eyes and typed: "I saw it. When did you leave it here?"
Lu Shiyan: "This afternoon. Your studio door was locked, I couldn't get in, so I left it at your door."
Su Qingyuan: "Why didn't you just give it to me directly?"
Lu Shiyan: "Because I wanted you to see it alone. You don't look good crying in front of other people."
Su Qingyuan laughed through her tears. She picked up the ring and slid it onto the ring finger of her left hand — a perfect fit. He'd known her size all along.
Su Qingyuan: "When did you measure my size?"
Lu Shiyan: "While you were asleep. With a piece of string."
Su Qingyuan thought back to those days in Scandinavia — on the plane, in the car, in the hotel, she had so often fallen asleep leaning against him. So that was when he'd quietly measured her ring size.
Su Qingyuan: "Lu Shiyan, is this a proposal?"
Lu Shiyan: "Yes."
Su Qingyuan: "Last time you said a proposal needs a ring."
Lu Shiyan: "I brought one. This time I brought one."
Su Qingyuan: "Then where are you?"
The other end went silent for a few seconds. Then she heard footsteps behind her. She turned around, and Lu Shiyan stepped out from around the corner of the staircase, a bunch of white chamomile in his hand, wearing a dark grey coat, his scarf blown crooked by the wind.
He walked up to her and looked down at her. "You put the ring on. Does it fit?"
Su Qingyuan lifted her hand; the moonstone glowed softly under the streetlight. "It fits."
"And the answer?"
"What answer?"
"Whether you're willing—"
"I'm willing." Su Qingyuan cut him off, the tears falling again. "Willing. I've been willing since the day you said, 'Tell me once you're sure.'"
Lu Shiyan looked at her and smiled. He reached out and gently wiped the tears from her face, then handed her the chamomile. "These are for you. I didn't pick them up on the way — I bought them specially. From now on, every time will be on purpose."
Su Qingyuan took the flowers and buried her face in the bouquet, breathing in the faint scent of chamomile. Then she rose onto her toes and kissed him.
The light in the stairwell was warm and yellow, falling over the two people holding each other. Outside the window the night wind was cold, but neither of them felt cold at all.
Su Qingyuan couldn't remember how she got upstairs. She only remembered that he held her hand the whole way, fingers laced through hers, the ring pressing against each other's fingers — cool at first, but soon warm.
Inside, she changed her shoes, set the chamomile in a vase, and placed it on the dining table. Then she turned and looked at him.
"When did you get the ring ready?"
"The day we got back from Scandinavia."
"That fast?"
"Not fast. I'd been thinking about it for a long time." Lu Shiyan stood in front of her, helping her undo her scarf. "Since the day you signed the contract with Zhou Wanyin, I'd been thinking about it."
Su Qingyuan paused. "That early?"
"Mm. Even back then I was thinking — once you were standing firmly on your own, I'd ask you."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head and looked at the moonstone on her finger. "How did you know I'd stand firm?"
"Because you're you."
Su Qingyuan's nose stung again. She reached out and hugged him, burying her face in his chest. "Lu Shiyan, thank you for waiting for me."
Lu Shiyan tightened his arms, resting his chin on the top of her head. "I waited. It was worth it."
They stood in the entryway, holding each other for a long time. Long enough for the moon outside to peek out from behind the clouds, long enough for one of the streetlights below to go out.
Su Qingyuan didn't post the news of the proposal to her social feeds, didn't put it on Weibo. She told only two people.
The first was Zhou Wanyin. Zhou Wanyin's reply was a long voice message; Su Qingyuan tapped to play it and heard Zhou Wanyin's voice cracking with excitement: "Ahhhhhh! He proposed!!! A moonstone!!! The Ripple Cut!!! Did he scour the entire internet to find someone who could make this ring?!!" Su Qingyuan laughed after listening and replied: "Sister Wanyin, calm down."
Zhou Wanyin: "I can't calm down!! When's the wedding? I'm going to be a bridesmaid!!"
Su Qingyuan: "Not decided yet. I'll tell you when it is."
Zhou Wanyin: "Okay!! I'll be waiting for your news!!!"
The second was Qu Ya. Qu Ya's reply was very short, just one line: "He deserves it. So do you. Congratulations."
Su Qingyuan looked at these two messages, and her heart felt warm.
She didn't tell Su Yurou, and she didn't tell Chen Xiaoman. It wasn't that she was deliberately hiding it — she just felt that some people no longer needed to know.
Preparing for the New York tour was more complicated than Su Qingyuan had expected.
Cartier's Fifth Avenue flagship store in New York was one of the global landmarks of the jewelry industry. Su Qingyuan's "Light Traces" collection would be exhibited there for two weeks. She needed to go to New York in person to oversee the display, and she had to give interviews to several American media outlets. In the week before departure, she practically lived in the studio.
Lu Shiyan didn't try to talk her out of working so hard. He simply showed up on time every day, bringing food, watching her finish it, then clearing away the containers. One night, deep in the early hours, Su Qingyuan lifted her head from the workbench and saw him sitting on the sofa, laptop open, the screen's glow lighting up his earnest profile.
"Why haven't you left yet?"
"Waiting for you."
"You don't have to wait for me. I might be up all night."
"Then I'll stay up all night. I'll keep you company."
Su Qingyuan looked at the faint shadows under his eyes, and her heart softened. She walked over, sat down beside him, and leaned against his shoulder. "Lu Shiyan, if you keep doing this, I'm going to get used to it."
"Then get used to it."
"And if I get used to it and then you're not there?"
"I won't not be there."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes and listened to his heartbeat. One beat, two beats, three. Each one steady and strong, like some kind of promise.
Spring came earlier to New York than to Nancheng. Su Qingyuan stood at the entrance of the Cartier flagship on Fifth Avenue, looking up at that elegant building, sunlight reflecting off the glass curtain wall so brightly that she had to squint. Lu Shiyan stood behind her, pushing the suitcase, her handbag hanging off it.
"Nervous?" he asked.
"A little," Su Qingyuan said. "But more than that, I'm excited."
"Same as what you said before going to Paris."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him and smiled. "How do you remember everything?"
"Because every word you've ever said is worth remembering."
Su Qingyuan smiled and took his arm, and they walked through Cartier's doors.
The response to the New York tour exceeded all expectations. The headline of the New York Times review read: "Light from the East, Illuminating Fifth Avenue." The social media account of Vogue's American edition posted three updates in a row, calling the "Light Traces" collection "the most noteworthy jewelry work of the year." A Hollywood star even placed an order on the spot at the exhibition, buying the diamond necklace with the half-suspended setting.
Su Qingyuan stood in a corner of the exhibition hall, watching the people gathered before her work, and a strange feeling welled up in her — not pride, but calm. Once she had longed to be recognized; now she had been recognized. But she discovered that being recognized didn't feel as earth-shaking as she had imagined. It was very quiet, like a river that, after a long, rushing journey, finally flows into the sea — not an ending, but a vaster beginning.
"Miss Su, congratulations." A familiar voice came from behind her. Su Qingyuan turned and saw Jean-Pierre standing there, holding two glasses of champagne. "Jean-Pierre! How did you get here?"
"A Cartier tour in New York — how could I not come?" Jean-Pierre smiled and handed her a glass of champagne. "Miss Su, the response to the 'Light Traces' collection in New York has been even better than in Paris. Do you know what that means?"
"What?"
"It means your light belongs not only to the East, and not only to the West. It belongs to the whole world."
The hand holding the champagne glass trembled slightly. "Jean-Pierre, that's too much to live up to."
"It's not too much. You can carry it." Jean-Pierre clinked his glass against hers. "Congratulations, Miss Su. I look forward to our next collection."
Su Qingyuan took a sip of champagne, the bubbles bursting on the tip of her tongue with a hint of sweetness.
On the night the tour ended, Lu Shiyan took Su Qingyuan up to the observation deck at the top of the Empire State Building. All of New York spread out beneath their feet, ablaze with lights, like a golden sea.
"Beautiful?" Lu Shiyan asked.
"Beautiful." Su Qingyuan looked at the city below. "But not as beautiful as the aurora."
Lu Shiyan smiled. "Then next year we'll go see the aurora again."
"Okay."
"Every year."
"Okay."
"Every year we'll go to Paris, eat croissants, see the Wall of Love."
"Okay."
"Every year we'll come to New York, see the Empire State Building."
"Okay."
"Every year we'll go back to Nancheng, eat hot pot."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him and smiled. "Why does everything have to be once a year with you?"
"Because I want to spend many years with you. One year isn't enough, two isn't enough either. It has to be many, many years."
Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder, looking at the thousands of lights below. "Lu Shiyan, where do you think we'll be in the future?"
"Anywhere. Wherever you are, that's where I'll be."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes, feeling the night wind brush her cheeks. New York's night wind was drier than Nancheng's, carrying the city's distinctive clamor. But she felt at peace, because he was here.
By the time they got back to Nancheng, it was already mid-April. Spring had finally come.
The osmanthus tree outside the studio window had put out new leaves, tender green, glittering in the sunlight. Su Qingyuan stood by the window, looking at those new leaves, and thought back to last autumn when she'd first moved into this studio, when the osmanthus had bloomed all over the tree, its fragrance sweet to the point of cloying. Back then she'd only just gotten together with Lu Shiyan; everything had only just begun, everything was unknown. Now, new leaves had sprouted on the once-bare branches of the osmanthus tree, and new leaves had sprouted on her career too — the global tour of the "Light Traces" collection had won its opening battle, the "Roots" series had been selected for the Venice Biennale, and the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition had been named the year's best cross-disciplinary art project.
Everything was new.
Her phone buzzed. Lu Shiyan's message: "What do you want for dinner tonight?"
Su Qingyuan thought for a moment and typed two words: "Your cooking."
Lu Shiyan: "Okay. I'll come pick you up."
Su Qingyuan's lips curved up. She put her phone in her pocket, picked up her carving knife, and went back to polishing the first sample of the new collection. This series didn't have a name yet; she'd come up with several and wasn't satisfied with any of them. Maybe she shouldn't rush to name it — let it grow slowly. Once it had grown, the name would come on its own.
That evening, Lu Shiyan came to pick her up. The car stopped outside his building; Su Qingyuan pushed the door open, got out, and stepped into the elevator. The doors opened, she changed into those pale pink fuzzy slippers, and walked into the living room.
On the dining table sat two dishes and a soup: stir-fried seasonal greens, braised pork ribs, tomato egg-drop soup. All simple dishes, but plated with care. Su Qingyuan sat down, picked up a piece of rib, took a bite, and her eyes lit up.
"Good?"
"So good! When did you learn to make ribs?"
"While you were in New York. I watched a few videos, tried a few times."
"Tried a few times?"
"Five times."
Su Qingyuan looked at him and laughed. "You tried five times to make one batch of ribs?"
"I tried five times to make one batch of ribs for you."
Su Qingyuan set down her chopsticks and looked at him. "Lu Shiyan, why are you so good to me?"
Lu Shiyan thought for a moment, then said one word: "Worth it."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot. She lowered her head and kept eating the ribs, pressing the heat in her eyes back down.
After dinner, Su Qingyuan went to wash the dishes. Lu Shiyan stood in the kitchen doorway, leaning against the frame, watching her profile as she washed. The warm yellow light fell over her, her hair loose over her shoulders, her sleeves rolled up to the elbows, revealing her slender wrists and that moonstone ring.
"Su Qingyuan."
"Mm?"
"The Venice Biennale — I'll go with you."
"Okay."
"The New York tour — I'll go with you."
"Okay."
"The Paris launch — I'll go with you."
"Okay."
"Everything from now on — I'll go with you."
Su Qingyuan turned off the faucet and turned to look at him. "Then it's settled."
"It's settled."
She walked over, rose onto her toes, and pressed a very light, very gentle kiss to his lips.
Outside the window, Nancheng's night was deepening. But their sky stayed bright, always.
[System: This chapter — Lu Shiyan proposes, a moonstone ring, the Ripple Cut technique, the words engraved inside the ring: "From a glass door to the aurora — from now on, it's all you." The heroine says yes. The career arc advances: the New York tour is a success, the New York Times and Vogue give rave reviews, Jean-Pierre declares "your light belongs to the whole world." On the Empire State Building observation deck, they make the "once a year" promise. Back in Nancheng, a new collection is in the works. Next chapter — perhaps the Venice Biennale, or the wedding, or the birth of the new collection. The System stands by.]

========================================

Chapter 39 — The Light of Everyday Life
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626241.html

Su Qingyuan had been putting together a new collection lately. She hadn't settled on a name yet, but the theme was already crystal clear—the everyday. Those overlooked, trivial, hardly-worth-mentioning moments: the back of him making coffee in the morning light, the profile of his face as he waited under the lamp for her to finish work, the angle at which he tilted the umbrella on rainy days, the way he tucked her cold hands into his own pocket in winter. These moments were so small—too small to deserve a place in any story. But Su Qingyuan wanted to make them into jewelry. To let that tenderness, the kind so easily forgotten, be worn over the heart, hung at the ear, held in the palm of a hand.
She sketched many drafts. The first was a brooch shaped like one hand holding another—not the tight grip of interlaced fingers, but a loose, natural clasp, like the casual linking of hands on a stroll. She used two different metals, platinum and rose gold, woven together, like two people with different skin tones and different warmth who fit together just right when their hands met. The second was a pair of stud earrings, inspired by a streetlamp. She made a tiny, glowing lamp, set with a yellow sapphire inside its shade, the light rendered as fine engraved lines radiating outward from the wick. On every late night home, the streetlamp was waiting for her. The third was a necklace, its pendant a half-open door, warm yellow light spilling through the crack. Not a door flung wide, nor one shut tight—a half-open one, waiting for you to come back. This was her own favorite, because every time Lu Shiyan came to her studio, he never knocked. He had a key; he'd push the door open and walk in, a thermal bag in his hand. "The door's unlocked," he always said. Only later did she understand—he hadn't forgotten to knock. He wanted her to know: this door would always be open for him.
When Su Qingyuan finished the third sketch, she looked up out the window. The new leaves on the osmanthus tree had grown a little larger, the tender green deepening to emerald, shimmering faintly in the afternoon sun. She closed her sketchbook, took out her phone, and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "Come home early today. I'll cook for you." Lu Shiyan's reply came quickly: "You sure?"
Su Qingyuan: "Sure."
Lu Shiyan: "All right. I'll come home early."
Su Qingyuan's lips curved up. She put the sketchbook back in the drawer, grabbed her keys, and went out. She made a trip to the supermarket and bought pork ribs, greens, tofu, and a fish. The ribs she'd do sweet-and-sour, the greens stir-fried, the tofu in a soup, and the fish… she thought it over and decided on steaming it, simple. Back home, she changed into an apron, washed her hands, and started cooking. First she blanched the ribs, then mixed the sweet-and-sour sauce by the recipe from the video—one spoon of cooking wine, two of light soy, three of sugar, four of vinegar, five of water. She mixed and tasted as she went, adding sugar when it was too sour, vinegar when it was too sweet, and the flavor she ended up with wasn't bad at all. She cleaned the fish, stuffed it with ginger slices and scallion, drizzled steamed-fish soy sauce over it, and put it in to steam. She washed the greens and crushed the garlic, planning to stir-fry them once the ribs were done. She cut the tofu into cubes and simmered it with tomato into a soup, adding a pinch of salt and some chopped scallion.
When Lu Shiyan came home, she was stir-frying the greens. The range hood was humming, and she didn't hear the door open. Not until a pair of hands wrapped around her waist from behind did she jump, nearly dropping the spatula.
"You scared me half to death." She turned her head and glared at him.
Lu Shiyan didn't let go, his chin resting on her shoulder. "Cooking all this?"
"Mm. To celebrate."
"Celebrate what?"
"To celebrate that today you wanted to eat my cooking."
Lu Shiyan laughed, low, the vibration carrying through her back, ticklish. She shrank her neck. "Stop it, the food's going to burn." Only then did he let go and walk over to the table to help set out the bowls and chopsticks.
Four dishes and a soup: sweet-and-sour ribs, stir-fried seasonal greens, steamed sea bass, tomato and tofu soup. Su Qingyuan untied her apron, sat down across from him, and watched him a little nervously. "Try it."
Lu Shiyan picked up a piece of rib, took a bite, and chewed for a long time.
Su Qingyuan's heart climbed into her throat. "Not good?"
"It's good."
"Really?"
"Really. Much better than the first time. The first time was too sour."
Su Qingyuan paused. "The first time? When did you ever eat the first thing I cooked?"
Lu Shiyan looked at her, the corners of his lips lifting. "The first time you made ribs, you made them for yourself. That day you worked overtime until very late, came home and cooked ribs, and posted about it on your social feed. You said, 'Worked till the dead of night, treated myself to a plate of sweet-and-sour ribs—a little sour, but very satisfying.' Three years ago, December seventeenth, eleven forty-two at night."
Su Qingyuan froze. "You… you've seen my feed from three years ago?"
"Not seen. Scrolled through. From your very first post all the way to the last."
"You went through the whole thing?"
"Many times."
Tears slipped down Su Qingyuan's face. She lowered her head and poked at the rice in her bowl with her chopsticks, not letting him see her face. "Lu Shiyan, you're really such a freak."
"Mm."
"Who digs up someone's posts from years ago and reads them all?"
"I do."
Su Qingyuan laughed through her tears. She lifted her head and wiped her eyes with her sleeve. "So what else did you see?"
"I saw that on your birthday three years ago, you posted, 'No one remembers today.' I saw that on New Year's Eve two years ago, you posted, 'Eating instant noodles alone in my rented room—happy new year.' I saw that last spring, you posted, 'The osmanthus is blooming, it smells so sweet.'"
Su Qingyuan's tears wouldn't stop. She covered her face, her shoulders trembling faintly. All those years, she'd thought no one remembered her birthday, no one cared how she spent New Year's Eve, no one noticed the curve of her smile when she caught the scent of osmanthus. It turned out it wasn't that no one cared—it was that he hadn't appeared yet.
"Lu Shiyan, why didn't you come find me back then?"
"Because back then you weren't ready yet." Lu Shiyan reached out and gently took hold of the hands covering her face. "I knew you'd be ready sooner or later. I waited for you."
Su Qingyuan looked at him through the gaps in her fingers, eyes blurred with tears. "How did you know I'd be ready?"
"Because you're you."
Those same three words again.
Su Qingyuan lowered her hands, sniffled, and picked up her chopsticks. "Eat. The food's getting cold."
"All right."
She picked up a piece of rib and put it in her mouth. A little sour, and a little sweet.
That night, Su Qingyuan didn't go back to her own apartment.
It wasn't the first time she'd stayed over, but tonight was a little different. Lu Shiyan had prepared a new toothbrush for her, a new towel, new pajamas—pale pink, pure cotton, the material she liked. After her shower, wearing the new pajamas, she stood at the bedroom's floor-to-ceiling window, looking out at the night view.
"Do you like it?" Lu Shiyan came out of the bathroom, hair still wet, wearing dark blue loungewear.
"Mm. The pajamas are very comfortable."
"I don't mean the pajamas. I mean here."
Su Qingyuan turned and looked at him. "Here is wonderful."
"Then move in?"
Su Qingyuan paused. "What?"
"Move in. Then you won't have to run back and forth anymore." Lu Shiyan walked over and stood in front of her. "Your studio is in the south of the city, and from here it's a twenty-minute drive to the creative park. Your apartment is too small; it can't hold your work. There's an empty room here that can be turned into your second studio. You can go back to your place whenever you don't want to stay—but at least you'll have a place that's yours."
Su Qingyuan looked at him for a long while. "Lu Shiyan, is this an invitation to move in together?"
"Yes."
"Last time you proposed, this time you want to live together. Is the next step supposed to be a wedding?"
Lu Shiyan laughed. "If you want, then yes."
Su Qingyuan turned her face away, looking out at the night view. "I didn't say I wanted to."
"Then you don't want to."
"I didn't say I didn't want to either."
Lu Shiyan looked at the reddened tips of her ears and laughed. "So are you moving in or not?"
Su Qingyuan was silent for a few seconds. "I'll move in."
"Tomorrow?"
"The day after. I have to pack up tomorrow."
"All right. The day after, I'll come pick you up."
Su Qingyuan nodded, then turned and walked to the bed, pulled back the covers, and slipped underneath. The blanket was soft, carrying the fresh scent of laundry detergent, the kind of smell she liked. She rolled over, her back to him. After a while, the other side of the bed sank down, and he turned off the light.
In the darkness, Su Qingyuan lay with her eyes open, watching the moonlight that filtered through the gap in the curtains. "Lu Shiyan, are you asleep?"
"No."
"Tell me, will we fight in the future?"
"We will."
"How do you know?"
"Because when two people are together, they always end up fighting. Even teeth and lips bicker—how much more two people."
Su Qingyuan rolled over to face him. In the dark she couldn't make out his face, only the silhouette of his outline. "Then what do we do when we fight?"
"Make up."
"How do we make up?"
"However you want to make up, that's how we'll make up. If you don't want to make up, I'll wait for you. Once your temper cools, I'll come find you again."
Su Qingyuan reached out, feeling through the darkness until she found his hand and held it. "Lu Shiyan, tell me, why are you so good to me?"
"Because I have you."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes, holding his hand, and sank into a deep sleep.
On moving day, Lu Shiyan came to help her pack. Su Qingyuan didn't have much—clothes, books, sketchbooks, carving knives, a few half-finished pieces. The heaviest was that box of sketchbooks, from her university days until now, dozens of them, every one filled to the margins.
"All of these are coming?" Lu Shiyan looked at the box of sketchbooks.
"All of them."
"Then they're coming." He hoisted up the box. "Go take a look and see if anything's been missed."
Su Qingyuan made a round through the apartment. The pots and pans in the kitchen, the chamomile on the table, the moth orchid on the windowsill, the key rack in the entryway. Every single thing held a memory, but she couldn't carry all of those memories away. She took only a few—her favorite cup, the scarf Lu Shiyan had given her, the pot of moth orchid from the windowsill. The rest she left behind, for whoever moved in next.
"Let's go," she said.
Lu Shiyan carried the box, she held the moth orchid, and the two of them walked out the door. Su Qingyuan glanced back at the apartment she'd lived in for so long—empty now, sunlight pouring in through the windows and falling across the floor, bright enough to dazzle. She closed the door.
The car was parked downstairs at Lu Shiyan's building. Su Qingyuan pushed the door open and got out, looking at the familiar tower, and suddenly a strange feeling rose in her heart—not unfamiliarity, but at last.
"From now on this is your home too," Lu Shiyan said, standing beside her, still holding the box.
"Mm. My home."
It took them several trips to move everything in. Lu Shiyan had turned an empty room into her second studio; the workbench was newly bought, identical to the one in her south-city studio. The display cases, the tool racks, the lighting system—all of it arranged the way she liked.
Su Qingyuan stood in that little studio, looking at the familiar setup, warmth spreading through her chest.
"Do you like it?" Lu Shiyan stood in the doorway.
"I like it."
"Then from now on it's yours."
Su Qingyuan turned, walked over, rose onto her toes, and kissed him on the lips. "Thank you."
"You're welcome."
On the third day after moving into Lu Shiyan's home, Su Qingyuan received an email from Venice. The Venice Biennale jury committee was formally notifying her that her "Roots" series had been selected for the Biennale's "Thematic Exhibition" section. This wasn't a parallel exhibition, nor a fringe one—it was the Biennale's most central, most closely watched thematic show. The artists selected for the thematic exhibition were all among the world's very best. She was one of them.
After reading the email, Su Qingyuan set her phone face-down on the desk, slumped over the workbench in her studio, shoulders trembling faintly. Not crying—overcome.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Lu Shiyan: "See it?"
Su Qingyuan: "How did you know?"
Lu Shiyan: "Cartier told me. They have a partner in Venice who saw the list."
Su Qingyuan: "Since when have you been so close with Cartier?"
Lu Shiyan: "Since the day you signed the contract. Anything to do with you, I want to know."
Su Qingyuan's lips curved up. She picked up her phone and wrote back: "Dinner's on me tonight. Really on me—the kind where I don't let you pay."
Lu Shiyan: "All right. You pick the place."
Su Qingyuan: "Hotpot. The usual spot."
Lu Shiyan: "All right. I'll come get you."
In the evening, Lu Shiyan came to pick her up. The car stopped outside that Hunan restaurant—the red lanterns still just as red, the "signature chopped-chili fish head" ad on the glass window still just as eye-catching. Su Qingyuan pushed the door open, got out, took his arm, and walked in.
Still the same private room, still the same table, still the same pot of red oil rolling at a boil. Su Qingyuan blanched a slice of tripe—seven dips up, eight dips down—and put it in her mouth, sucking in air at the spice but unwilling to spit it out. "Delicious."
Lu Shiyan looked at her lips, gone red from the spice, his gaze as soft as moonlight. "Su Qingyuan."
"Mm?"
"The Venice Biennale—I'll go with you."
"Don't you have to work?"
"Work can come with a laptop."
Su Qingyuan set down her chopsticks and looked at him. "Lu Shiyan, you didn't used to be like this. You used to put work first."
"I didn't have you before."
Su Qingyuan lowered her head and went on eating hotpot, her ears red.
The Venice Biennale was in June. Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan arrived in Venice three days early, staying at a small hotel beside an old canal. Open the room's window and there was the water; gondolas glided past below, the boatmen's songs drifting across the surface.
"Beautiful?" Lu Shiyan stood behind her.
"Beautiful." Su Qingyuan looked out at the canal. "But thinking about setting up the exhibition tomorrow makes me a little nervous."
"No need to be nervous. Your work belongs here."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "How is it you say that every single time?"
"Because every single time, that's how I feel."
Su Qingyuan laughed, rose onto her toes, and kissed him on the cheek. "Come on, let's go out and walk around. We're here, after all."
They strolled slowly along the canal. Venice's water lanes were narrow and deep, sunlight slipping down through the gaps between buildings to fall on the water and shatter into a sheet of golden light. Su Qingyuan walked very slowly, because she wanted to remember every little alley, every little bridge, every streetlamp. Maybe one day she'd turn them into jewelry.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Tell me, what kind of place will we live in someday?"
"What kind of place do you want to live in?"
Su Qingyuan thought about it. "One with a courtyard, with an osmanthus tree planted in it. In autumn the whole yard would be sweet. With a big studio, windows facing south, plenty of sun. And a kitchen—it has to be big, because you like to cook."
Lu Shiyan looked at her and smiled. "All right. We'll live in a place just like that."
Su Qingyuan's lips curved up, and she kept walking, the flagstone road beneath her feet worn smooth by the years, refracting the light of the setting sun. She suddenly felt that the future was far away, yet every step landed on solid ground.
The thematic exhibition hall of the Venice Biennale was in the Giardini park. Su Qingyuan's "Roots" series had been placed in a small standalone gallery—not large, but in an excellent spot, the second gallery from the entrance, so everyone passing through would see it. Setting up took two days; the placement angle of every piece, the direction of light from every lamp, she adjusted all of it herself. Lu Shiyan couldn't help with the work, so he sat on the bench outside the gallery waiting for her, his laptop open, handling emails now and then, glancing up at her now and then.
Once the last piece was in place, Su Qingyuan stood in the center of the gallery and turned a full circle. Five works glowed with a soft light under the lamps—every gem, every line of texture, every detail clearly visible.
"Done?" Lu Shiyan walked in.
"Done."
"Then it's all on tomorrow."
"Mm. On tomorrow."
On opening day, the crowd was large. Su Qingyuan stood in the gallery, watching the people come and go—some stopped to look at her work, some took photos, some discussed in low voices. She heard people speaking Italian, people speaking English, people speaking languages she couldn't understand. But she could read their expressions—some were nodding, some were smiling, some read the text on the placard, then looked up at the work again, as if searching for something.
A white-haired old lady stood before the "Veins" necklace for a long time, then turned and said something to Su Qingyuan in Italian. Su Qingyuan didn't understand, and the interpreter beside her said, "She says this piece reminds her of her grandmother. Her grandmother's hands had a lot of bulging veins too, like tree roots. She used to think they were unsightly; now she thinks they were the most beautiful."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot. She walked over and took the old lady's hand. "Thank you."
The old lady held her hand and patted it, then said a few more words in Italian. The interpreter smiled. "She says: young one, your work has a soul. Keep doing this, and do it well."
Su Qingyuan nodded.
After the Venice Biennale ended, the "Roots" series won the thematic exhibition's Special Mention award. Not the top prize, but for a jewelry designer attending the Biennale for the first time, it was already an extremely high honor. Su Qingyuan didn't go up to accept it; she had the curator accept it on her behalf. Because she felt the "Roots" series didn't need her standing on a stage—it would speak for itself.
By the time they got back to Nancheng, it was already the end of June. Summer had come; the leaves of the osmanthus tree were greener now, a green too thick to dissolve. Su Qingyuan stood at the studio window, looking at that osmanthus tree, and remembered last autumn, when she'd just moved into this studio and the tree was covered in blossoms, the fragrance sweet to the point of being cloying. Back then she and Lu Shiyan hadn't been together long; everything was just beginning, everything was unknown. Now, the osmanthus tree was still here, the studio was still here, and he was here too.
Her phone buzzed. A message from Lu Shiyan: "What do you want to eat tonight?"
Su Qingyuan thought about it and typed two words: "Dumplings."
Lu Shiyan: "All right. I'll come get you. We'll make them together."
Su Qingyuan's lips curved up. She slipped her phone into her pocket, locked the door, and walked out of the studio. The sun was lovely, falling on her shoulders, warm. She drew in a deep breath and let it out. The summer air was hot, carrying the green, unripe scent of osmanthus leaves.
She walked out of the creative park and waited for the car at the intersection. In the distance, a black car was driving toward her.
She knew—that was her light.
[System: This chapter—moving in together, the Venice Biennale, the "Roots" series Special Mention award, everyday sweetness: making dumplings, taking walks, watching the sunset. Lu Shiyan scrolling through her social feed from three years ago—a detail that lands like a kill shot. The ending echoes the rainy night of the opening, but now it's a clear day. Next chapter, it might be preparing for a wedding, or the birth of a new collection, or the return of some old friend (Qu Ya? Zhou Wanyin? Chen Xiaoman? Su Yurou?). System on standby.]

========================================

Chapter 40 — Light and Everyday Life
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626242.html

After Venice, Su Qingyuan's life was filled with two things: the creation of her new collection, and—what Lu Shiyan called "wedding preparations." In truth, he did most of it; her job was only to nod or shake her head. Choosing the venue, she nodded; choosing the date, she nodded; choosing the invitation design, she looked at it for three seconds—"This one"—and went back to bending over her sketches. Lu Shiyan never rushed her. She knew he loved this state of things: her busy, him beside her.
One evening she lifted her head from her workbench and found Lu Shiyan sitting on the sofa, a notebook in his hand, writing something.
"What are you writing?"
"The guest list."
"Let me see."
Lu Shiyan handed the notebook over. Su Qingyuan took it and looked—it was crammed full of names. Zhou Wanyin, Qu Ya, Anna, Jean-Pierre, President Chen, Secretary-General Bai, the director of the Cultural Bureau… She looked at those names and suddenly realized how large her world had become. A year ago, her phone's contact list had held only a handful of names; now her wedding could fill an entire hall.
"Isn't that too many people?" she asked.
"You want fewer?"
"Not exactly. I just feel… it's a little unreal."
Lu Shiyan looked at her. "What part is unreal?"
"A year ago, I was on my own. Now I'm about to marry you."
Lu Shiyan was silent for a second, then said, "A year ago, you weren't on your own either. It was just that I wasn't beside you yet."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot. She handed the notebook back and turned to keep sketching. What she was drawing was the jewelry she would wear at the wedding—not for anyone else, for herself. A necklace, its pendant a single moonstone, cut in the Ripple Cut—but this time the halo wasn't three layers, it was four. At the very center was deep blue, then silver-white spreading outward, then warm gold beyond that, and the outermost layer a ring of clear, almost invisible light. She wanted to call that fourth layer "the future"—an unknown light, but a light that existed.
The wedding was set for late September, when the osmanthus bloomed. The venue was a manor on the outskirts of Nancheng, not large, but with one enormous osmanthus tree, its canopy spread open like a golden umbrella, its fragrance so thick it would not dissolve. The first time Su Qingyuan went to see the place, she stood beneath that osmanthus tree and tilted her head up at the little golden-yellow flowers crowding the branches, and suddenly remembered the autumn before, the day she had just moved into her studio—the osmanthus by the door had smelled just like this.
"Here," she said.
"All right." Lu Shiyan stood behind her, looking at that osmanthus tree too.
The wedding was small; only some fifty-odd people were invited. Su Qingyuan didn't wear a bridal gown. She wore a white floor-length dress of her own design, its hem embroidered with osmanthus branches in real silk and silver thread, so that when she walked the sprays of blossom seemed to sway gently in the wind. The necklace was made by her own hands—the four-layered moonstone, glowing with a soft light in the sun.
Lu Shiyan wore a black suit, no tie, the top button of his shirt left undone—she'd told him to leave it. "Too tight," she said. "It's not like you're going to a meeting." He did as she said.
The ceremony was simple. No officiant, no long-winded vows. They stood beneath the osmanthus tree, face to face, and said only a few words.
Lu Shiyan said, "Su Qingyuan, from that glass door to today, I've thought many times about what I'd say to you. But standing here, I find there's nothing I need to say. Because everything I wanted to say, you already know."
Su Qingyuan's tears fell.
She said, "Lu Shiyan, I used to think I'd used up all the luck of my entire life. Later I found out I hadn't. The best one was saved for the very end."
Lu Shiyan reached out and gently wiped the tears from her face. Then he lowered his head and kissed her.
Osmanthus flowers fell on their shoulders, like golden snow.
Zhou Wanyin stood off to the side, crying harder than Su Qingyuan. Qu Ya handed her a tissue; she took it, wiped her eyes, and cried again. Anna stood at the back, taking a few photos with her phone and posting them to the Cartier headquarters group chat. Jean-Pierre replied with a thumbs-up.
After the ceremony, everyone sat beneath the osmanthus tree to eat. The food was Lu Shiyan's choosing—not the standard hotel banquet sort, but the most authentic home-style cooking of Nancheng: sweet-and-sour pork ribs, stir-fried seasonal greens, red-braised pork, tomato and egg-drop soup. Every dish was something Su Qingyuan loved.
Zhou Wanyin drifted over to Su Qingyuan's side, a glass of red wine in hand. "Qingyuan, you look beautiful today."
"Thank you, Wanyin."
"I'm not being polite. You really do look beautiful. Your eyes used to always have that 'I need to prove myself' light. Today it's gone. Today it's the light of 'I know who I am.'"
Su Qingyuan looked at Zhou Wanyin and smiled. "Wanyin, thank you. Without you, there'd be no me as I am today."
"Don't say that. Without me, you'd have gotten here too. Just a little slower."
Qu Ya came over as well and clinked her glass against Su Qingyuan's. "Qingyuan, the 'Roots and Light' joint exhibition—do you want to do a second season next year?"
"Sure. What's the theme?"
"Haven't thought of one yet. Tell me once you've figured it out."
"All right."
Anna said a blessing in French, and Jean-Pierre translated it into English: "He says Cartier is waiting for your next collection." Su Qingyuan smiled and nodded.
The guests left one by one. The sky gradually darkened, and the lights of the manor came on one after another. The long table beneath the osmanthus tree was littered with cups and dishes, but it was warm and cozy. Su Qingyuan sat under the tree, leaning against Lu Shiyan's shoulder, watching the little golden-yellow flowers glittering across the whole tree in the lamplight.
"Tired?" he asked.
"No."
"Really?"
"Really. Today is the least tired day of my whole life."
Lu Shiyan smiled and reached out to pick an osmanthus blossom from her hair.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"From now on, every year at this time, let's come here to see the osmanthus."
"All right."
"Every year."
"Every year."
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes and breathed in the fragrance of osmanthus. She thought of the autumn before, the day she first went to the studio—there had been an osmanthus tree by the door then too, and he had stood beneath it waiting for her, a key in his hand. He'd said, "The key's here. Come look anytime." Back then she'd thought he was just being kind, helping her find a studio; only later did she learn that, for that one studio, he had taken control of the entire creative park.
She opened her eyes and looked at him. "Lu Shiyan, when did you start liking me?"
Lu Shiyan thought about it. "The glass door."
"That early?"
"Mm. I didn't know it then—that it was liking you."
"When did you know?"
"The rainy night. You were crouched at the side of the road, soaked through. I thought: this person, I can't let her be alone."
Su Qingyuan's tears fell again. She reached out and held him, burying her face against his chest. "Lu Shiyan, thank you for not giving up on me."
Lu Shiyan tightened his arms, his chin resting on the top of her head. "Giving up on you was never one of my options."
Beneath the osmanthus tree, the shadows of the two embracing fell on the ground, very close together.
After the wedding, Su Qingyuan didn't change her surname. Lu Shiyan never brought it up either. Once Zhou Wanyin asked her, "Your husband, President Lu, didn't make you change your name?" Su Qingyuan smiled. "He said, Su Qingyuan is Su Qingyuan. Change it and you wouldn't be you anymore." Zhou Wanyin sighed, "Did you save the galaxy in a past life or something?"
Su Qingyuan thought about it. "Maybe. But I think it's just that I got lucky in this life."
The first year after the wedding, they went to Northern Europe to see the auroras. The second year, they went to Paris and took another photo together in front of the Wall of Love. The third year, they went to New York, and on the observation deck of the Empire State Building, Su Qingyuan's belly had already begun to swell a little. She was pregnant.
On the day she found out she was pregnant, Su Qingyuan was in the studio polishing a piece for her new collection. She set down her carving knife and made a trip to the hospital. When she got the results, she sat on the long bench in the hospital corridor and sent Lu Shiyan a message: "You're going to be a father." There was a full minute of silence on the other end. Then the phone rang. His voice shook a little. "Really?"
"Really."
"Where are you?"
"The hospital."
"Don't move. I'll come get you."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "You don't have to come get me. I can go home on my own."
"Don't move. I'll come get you."
He hung up. Su Qingyuan sat on the bench, looking at that message on her phone screen—"You're going to be a father"—and the corners of her mouth curved up. After about twenty minutes, Lu Shiyan appeared at the far end of the hospital corridor, his suit jacket askew from running, his tie loosened, his hair blown into a mess by the wind. He had never been so disheveled, and he had never looked so good. He came over, crouched in front of her, and took her hands. "How far along?"
"Six weeks."
"Are you all right? Anything feel uncomfortable?"
"No. Just a little tired."
"Then let's go home. Rest."
Su Qingyuan looked at how nervous he was and smiled. "Lu Shiyan, you're more nervous than I am."
"Mm. Nervous."
He helped her up, his arm circling her waist, and they walked very slowly, as if he were afraid she might fall. Su Qingyuan leaned against him, breathing in his familiar scent, and felt that this life had been worth it.
Through the pregnancy, Su Qingyuan didn't stop working. She said using her brain was good for the baby. Lu Shiyan didn't try to stop her; he only made nutritious meals every day, varying them constantly, and watched over her while she ate. She put on fifteen pounds; he lost ten.
At seven months pregnant, Su Qingyuan completed the fifth and final piece of her new collection. She named this piece "Beginning"—the same character as in "First Light." She said the child's arrival was another beginning.
On the day of the delivery, Lu Shiyan stayed with her at the hospital for a full twenty hours. Su Qingyuan was in so much pain she couldn't speak, so he held her hand and said, over and over, "I'm here." She didn't cry; he did.
Their daughter was born at dawn, the sky outside just brightening, morning light leaking through the gap in the curtains to fall on her wrinkled little face. Su Qingyuan looked at her, tears streaming unstoppably.
"She's like you," Lu Shiyan said, his voice hoarse in a way that didn't sound like him.
"Like me how?"
"Her eyes. The way you looked at me that first time outside the glass door—that light."
Su Qingyuan laughed through her tears. She reached out a finger and gently touched her daughter's tiny hand. That little hand instantly gripped her finger, tight.
Their daughter's name was Lu Xi. Lu Shiyan chose it. Xi—morning light. The same meaning as the "Light" in "First Light." He said she was born in the morning light, and she was their light.
After her confinement month, the first thing Su Qingyuan did was go to the studio. After more than a year without working in earnest, a thin layer of dust had settled on her workbench. She opened the window, and the autumn wind surged in, sweeping away the stale air in the room. Outside, the osmanthus tree was covered in blossom, the fragrance sweet to the point of cloying.
She sat at her workbench, picked up her carving knife, and began to polish a new piece—a small brooch, shaped like a door, half-open, with light spilling through the crack. When she finished, she set it at the head of her daughter's crib. When she grew up, she would tell her—this door is the door where Papa waited for your mama to come home. And it's the door where Mama waited for you to come into this world.
Sometimes Su Qingyuan would think of that rainy night. Of how she had crouched at the side of the road, soaked through, thinking her life was over. She hadn't known then that that rain was the end of the storm, and also the beginning of dawn.
Now she knew.
She sat beneath the osmanthus tree, Lu Xi in her arms, Lu Shiyan beside her with a cup of tea in his hand. Sunlight leaked down through the gaps in the leaves, falling over all three of them, like gold shattered across the ground.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"What do you think Lu Xi will do when she grows up?"
"I don't know. But whatever she does, it'll be wonderful."
"Why?"
"Because she's your daughter."
Su Qingyuan smiled and lowered her head to look at Lu Xi, already asleep in her arms. The tiny mouth was pursed slightly, the breathing soft, the lashes long and curled. She reached out and gently touched her cheek.
Lu Shiyan watched the two of them, the light in his eyes gentler than it had ever been.
"Su Qingyuan."
"Mm."
"Thank you."
"Thank me for what?"
"Thank you for letting me wait until I found you."
Su Qingyuan looked up at him. Sunlight fell across the side of his face; his profile was still as handsome as ever.
"You're welcome."
She leaned against his shoulder, holding their daughter, breathing in the fragrance of osmanthus. In the distance the city glittered in the autumn sunlight; everything was quiet, everything was beautiful.
Su Qingyuan suddenly thought of the words she had written long ago, in that eighty-square-meter studio—"All the darkness will pass. The sky will always brighten in the end."
The sky had brightened. And it would go on brightening, forever.

========================================

Chapter 41 — First Light (Side Story)
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626243.html

First Light
The year Lu Xi turned three, Su Qingyuan made a decision—to take her to Northern Europe to see the aurora.
It wasn't a whim. For three years she had gone with Lu Shiyan every year, but it had always been just the two of them, with Lu Xi left in Zhou Wanyin's care. This year, Lu Xi said, "Mama, I want to see the dancing lights too." She had started talking earlier than other children her age—full sentences, clear logic, just like her father.
Su Qingyuan talked it over with Lu Shiyan. He thought for a moment. "She's too little. Twenty below zero—she won't be able to take it."
"We can stay in a glass igloo and not go outside. She can watch from indoors."
Lu Shiyan looked at the light in her eyes—a light he had seen before. It appeared every time she had a new creative spark, or whenever there was something she desperately wanted to do. He didn't object again. "All right. We'll go."
On the day they set off, Lu Xi wore a little penguin-shaped backpack and a red down jacket, looking for all the world like a plump, round little tomato. She held Su Qingyuan's hand and looked excitedly around the airport. "Mama, the plane is so big."
"Mm. We're going to ride this big plane to see the aurora."
"Where is the aurora?"
"In the sky."
"Where in the sky?"
Su Qingyuan crouched down and straightened her scarf. "Tonight, when it gets dark, look up and you'll be able to see it."
Lu Xi nodded, half understanding, and turned to look at Lu Shiyan. "Papa, have you seen the aurora?"
"I have."
"With who?"
"With your mama."
"Why didn't you take me?"
Lu Shiyan crouched down to meet her eyes. "Because you hadn't been born yet."
Lu Xi thought about it. "But I'm born now. Can you take me?"
Lu Shiyan smiled and reached out to ruffle the top of her head. "Yes."
On the plane, Lu Xi sat between Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan, buckled into a child's seatbelt, a picture book about the aurora in her hands, flipping through it over and over. Su Qingyuan leaned against Lu Shiyan's shoulder, watching her daughter's earnest profile—round face, big eyes, long curled lashes, like her. But that focused expression was like his. When she read, her little brow would furrow slightly, and she turned the pages so gently, as if afraid of damaging them. Those small details were exactly like him.
"Lu Shiyan, look at the way she turns the pages."
Lu Shiyan glanced down, the corner of his mouth lifting. "Like you."
"Like me? I don't turn pages like that."
"You do when you're flipping through your sketchbook. Very gently, very slowly, as if afraid of disturbing something."
Su Qingyuan looked at him, and something inside her went warm.
They changed planes in Helsinki, flying on to Ivalo. Lu Xi pressed herself against the window, watching the vast white snowfield below, clapping in excitement. "Mama! Snow! So much snow!"
"Mm. When we get to the hotel, you can go play in the snow."
"What about the aurora? Is the aurora in the snow too?"
"The aurora is in the sky."
Lu Xi tilted her head back to look at the sky outside the window. It was very blue, with no aurora. She was a little disappointed, but the snowfield outside quickly drew her attention again.
The glass igloo hotel. It was the first time Lu Xi had stayed in a room like this, and she was so excited she bounced up and down on the bed, kicked off her shoes, and spun in circles in her socks beneath the glass dome. "Mama! The ceiling is glass! You can see the sky!"
"Mm. Tonight, when it gets dark and the aurora comes out, you'll be able to see it just lying in bed."
"Really?"
"Really."
Lu Xi stopped bouncing, lay down obediently, pulled the covers up, and kept her eyes wide open, staring at the sky beyond the glass dome. It wasn't dark yet—a blue-gray, with a few birds flying past.
Su Qingyuan sat on the edge of the bed and tucked her in. "Sleep for a bit. I'll wake you when it gets dark."
"I'm not sleepy."
"Then lie down and rest."
Lu Xi blinked. "Mama, tell me a story."
Su Qingyuan thought for a moment. "What kind of story?"
"The story about you and Papa."
Su Qingyuan glanced at Lu Shiyan. He was standing by the window, a cup of coffee in his hand, and at those words, the corner of his mouth lifted slightly.
"Where should I start?"
"From the very beginning."
Su Qingyuan looked at her daughter's bright, shining eyes and thought for a few seconds. "In the very beginning, Mama didn't know Papa yet. Once, Mama went to see an exhibition but couldn't get in, so she stood outside the glass door looking in. Papa was inside the door, and he saw Mama."
"And then?"
"And then, a long time later, one night it rained. Mama didn't have an umbrella and was crouched at the side of the road. Papa drove by, stopped, and asked if I wanted to get in the car."
"Did you get in?"
"I did."
"Weren't you afraid he was a bad person?"
Su Qingyuan smiled. "No."
"Why?"
"Because of his eyes. Just like yours when you read—very earnest, very gentle."
Lu Xi nodded, half understanding. "And then?"
"And then Papa stayed by Mama's side ever after."
"Ever after?"
"Ever after."
Lu Xi thought about it, then asked, "And me? Where was I?"
"You were still up in the sky."
"Where in the sky?"
Su Qingyuan pointed at the glass dome. "Up in the sky, waiting until Papa and Mama were ready, and then you came down."
Lu Xi followed her finger to the sky. It had grown dark now, a deep blue, and the first star had begun to shine. "Is that star me?"
"Maybe."
Lu Xi smiled, her eyes curving into little crescents. She rolled over and hugged Su Qingyuan's arm. "Mama, I like this story."
"As long as you like it. Go to sleep now."
Lu Xi closed her eyes, her lashes trembling faintly. After a while, her breathing grew even—she had fallen asleep.
Su Qingyuan gently slipped her arm free, tucked her in, and got off the bed. Lu Shiyan stood by the window watching her, the cup of coffee in his hand already gone cold.
"That story you told her—was it true?"
"Most of it was true."
"That part about the rainy night—you said you got in the car because of my eyes?"
Su Qingyuan walked over and stood before him. "Mm. Your eyes. Very earnest, very gentle. Not like a bad person's."
Lu Shiyan looked at her and smiled. "That's not what you said at the time. At the time you said, 'Sorry to trouble you.'"
"That was just being polite."
"And now you're not being polite?"
"Now there's no need to be polite." Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder. "Now you're Lu Xi's papa."
Lu Shiyan reached out and wrapped an arm around her shoulders. "Su Qingyuan, thank you for telling me that story."
"You're welcome."
The aurora appeared in the dead of night. Su Qingyuan was gently shaken awake by Lu Shiyan. "The aurora." She opened her eyes. Across the glass dome, green light flowed—brighter than any time before, sweeping across the whole sky like a luminous river. She turned to look at Lu Xi. The little one had woken at some point and was staring up at the sky with wide eyes, her mouth open in a small O.
"Mama, it's moving."
"Mm. It's dancing."
"How long will it dance?"
"I don't know. Maybe a few minutes, maybe a few hours."
Lu Xi said nothing, and kept watching the flowing light overhead. Green, pink, purple, drifting slowly across the night sky like a living painting.
"Mama."
"Mm."
"The aurora is so pretty."
"Mm."
"Can we come and see it every year from now on?"
Su Qingyuan glanced at Lu Shiyan. He was looking at her too, a smile in his eyes. "Is that all right?" Su Qingyuan asked Lu Shiyan.
"All right."
Lu Xi smiled, reached out her hands, took Su Qingyuan's with her left and Lu Shiyan's with her right. "Then it's settled. We come every year. Pinky promise."
Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan exchanged a glance and reached out together to hook pinkies with Lu Xi.
"Pinky swear, cross my heart, no changing for a hundred years." Lu Xi's childish voice echoed through the glass igloo.
The aurora still flowed overhead. Su Qingyuan looked at her daughter's little face lit by its glow, and suddenly remembered, years and years ago, sitting on the floor of the attic in the old Su family house, reading a worn-out travel magazine. In it had been a photograph of the aurora. Back then she had thought: in this lifetime, I must go see it once. Not only had she seen it herself—she had brought her daughter along too. That girl drawing light on the attic floor probably never imagined that one day, her light would shine on so many people.
The next day, they built a snowman on the snowfield. Lu Xi named it "Lumi-Lumi," because it was made of snow, but when the sun shone on it, it sparkled and glowed. Lu Xi wrapped her own scarf around the snowman, stepped back to look, decided she wasn't quite satisfied, then took the scarf off and rewrapped it. "Mama, it doesn't have eyes."
