Su Qingyuan pulled two black buttons from her pocket and handed them to Lu Xi. Lu Xi pressed the buttons into the snowman's face, then found a little twig for a nose.
"There. Does it look good?"
"It looks good."
Lu Xi nodded with satisfaction, then turned and ran off toward the snow in the distance, leaving a trail of tiny little footprints behind.
Lu Shiyan stood beside Su Qingyuan, watching their daughter race across the snow. "Su Qingyuan, would you say she's like you, or like me?"
"Like you."
"How is she like me?"
"The way she turns pages is like you. The earnest way she looks is like you. The stubborn way she gets is like you too."
Lu Shiyan turned his head to look at her. "The way she smiles is like you. The light in her eyes is like you. The kind way she is, that's like you too."
Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder, watching that little red figure in the distance frolicking through the snow. "Lu Shiyan, we're very lucky."
"Mm."
"It's not luck. We worked for it."
"Mm. We worked for it, and then the luck came."
Lu Xi ran back, a handful of snow cupped in her hands. "Mama, Papa, what are you talking about?"
"About you." Su Qingyuan crouched down and brushed the snow off her.
"What about me?"
"Saying you're like Mama, and like Papa too."
Lu Xi thought about it, then smiled. "Then I'm both of you."
Su Qingyuan looked at her, and her nose suddenly stung. "Mm. You're both of us."
After they returned to Nancheng, Su Qingyuan made a new series—three pieces. The first was a brooch, shaped as two large hands holding one small hand, using three metals—platinum, rose gold, and yellow gold. They represented Papa, Mama, and child. The second was a pair of stud earrings, one large and one small—the larger a moon, the smaller a star. The moon cradled the star, like a mother cradling her daughter. The third was a necklace, its pendant a teardrop-shaped moonstone with a halo of four layers—the same as the one she had worn at her wedding. But this time, the fourth layer was not "Future"—it was "Xi." Her daughter, her light.
She didn't release this series publicly, nor did she exhibit it. She placed the brooch on Lu Shiyan's desk, the earrings in her own jewelry box, and hung the necklace at the head of Lu Xi's bed. Others couldn't see them, but they could.
That was enough.
The days passed one by one. Lu Xi learned to walk, to run, to talk, to write. The first picture she drew was of a family of three—three stick figures holding hands, with a glowing yellow ball above their heads. She said it was the sun, or it could also be the aurora.
Lu Shiyan stuck this drawing on the wall across from his desk, so he could see it every time he looked up. Once, his assistant came in to deliver documents, saw the drawing, and paused. "President Lu, this is...?"
"My daughter drew it."
The assistant looked at the drawing, then at Lu Shiyan's expression, and tactfully asked no more, setting down the documents and leaving.
When Su Qingyuan heard about it, she laughed for a long time. "Your subordinates must think you've changed."
"Have I?"
"You have. Your office wall used to have a strategic map. Now it has a child's drawing."
Lu Shiyan looked at the drawing on the wall, the corner of his mouth lifting. "Strategic maps change every year. This one I never have to change."
Su Qingyuan looked at his earnest expression, and her heart softened.
The year Lu Xi turned four, the final stop of the global tour for Su Qingyuan's "Light Traces" collection came back to Paris. She brought Lu Xi along. It was Lu Xi's first time in Paris, and everything looked new to her. Beneath the Eiffel Tower, she tilted her head back so far that she didn't even notice her hat fall off. "Mama, it's so tall."
"Mm. It's called the Eiffel Tower."
"Why does it have to be so tall?"
"So that people can see it."
"See what?"
"See all of Paris. See where they stand."
Lu Xi nodded, half understanding, then bent down to pick up her hat, brushed off the dust, and put it back on.
Montmartre, the Wall of Love. Su Qingyuan took Lu Xi to see the wall covered with "I love you."
Lu Xi didn't recognize most of the words, but she recognized those three in Chinese. "Mama, what does this say?"
"'I love you.'"
"Who loves who?"
Su Qingyuan crouched down and pointed at the words on the wall. "Everyone loves everyone."
Lu Xi thought about it. "Then I love you."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot. "I love you too."
"Papa loves you too."
"Mm. Papa loves me too."
Lu Shiyan stood behind them, watching this scene, saying nothing. But he took out his phone and snapped a photo, saving it into an album called "Xi." It was full of photos of his daughter—her first smile, first steps, first time calling out "Papa" and "Mama," first snowman, first aurora, first time in Paris.
He rarely posted on his social feed, but that day he posted a photo of the mother and daughter from behind, standing before the Wall of Love. The caption was just two words: "Mine."
The comments section exploded. Zhou Wanyin: "Ahhhh so sweet!!!" Qu Ya: "When are you bringing little Xi to see my exhibition?" Anna: "C'est beau (so beautiful)." Jean-Pierre: "Félicitations (congratulations)."
When Su Qingyuan saw that post, she smiled. She reshared it with the caption: "Mine too."
Beneath her reshare, Lu Shiyan replied with one line: "You're mine too." Su Qingyuan didn't reply, but her ears turned red.
On their last night in Paris, they stayed at the same little hotel from their first visit. Su Qingyuan stood at the window, watching the Eiffel Tower glittering gold against the night.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Do you still remember the first time we came to Paris?"
"I remember. You said, 'Paris is wonderful.'"
Su Qingyuan smiled. "You still remember what I said?"
"I remember. You said, 'The best part is that you brought me here.'"
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "How do you remember everything?"
"Because anything to do with you is worth remembering."
Su Qingyuan walked over and leaned against his shoulder. "Lu Shiyan, do you think we'll always be like this?"
"Like what?"
"Traveling together, watching the aurora together, looking at the tower together. With little Xi."
Lu Shiyan lowered his head, his lips against the crown of her hair. "We will. Always."
Outside the window, the lights of the Eiffel Tower flickered on and off, as if telling them—goodnight.

========================================

Chapter 42 — Beyond the Light (Side Story)
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626244.html

Beyond the Light
Su Yurou saw that post on a rainy day in Melbourne. Lu Shiyan had put it up: in the photo, Su Qingyuan stood with a small girl in front of the Wall of Love, and the caption was just two words — "Mine."
She stared at the picture for a long time. The little girl had her hair tied up in two small bunches and wore a red coat, head tilted back as she looked up at the words on the wall, as if struggling to make out strokes she didn't yet recognize. Her profile was very like Su Qingyuan's, yet the outline of her brows and eyes plainly carried the shadow of that other man. Su Yurou turned her phone face-down on the table and closed her eyes. It was raining outside the café; Melbourne winters were full of rain, one shower after another, like old memories that wouldn't stop coming. She thought of an afternoon many years ago, the day Su Qingyuan first came to her home — it had been raining then too. A small, thin girl had stood in the doorway, her hair dripping wet, an old schoolbag in her hand. Her mother, Liu Xiulan, had said, "This is your big sister. She'll be living with us from now on." She had looked at the girl, and the girl had looked back at her. In the girl's eyes there was fear, there was hope, and there was something she couldn't name — later she would learn it was called wariness. The kind of carefulness everyone develops when they live under someone else's roof.
At the time she had thought it was fun. She'd run over and grabbed the girl's hand. "Big sister, what's your name?" The girl had said softly, "Su Qingyuan." She'd said, "Sister Qingyuan, I'll show you to your room." It was a tiny room, originally a storage closet, but it had been tidied up — there was a small bed, a small desk, a little lamp. The girl set her schoolbag on the desk, turned, and said, "Thank you." That was the first "thank you" Su Qingyuan ever said to her, as far as Su Yurou could remember. After that, Su Qingyuan said countless "thank yous" — thank you for helping with my homework, thank you for taking the blame for me, thank you for giving me the good food, thank you for not fighting me over Mom and Dad. Every "thank you" was a step backward, a concession. She had grown used to Su Qingyuan's concessions, used to her good temper, used to the fact that she never seemed to get angry. So when Su Qingyuan finally stopped giving way, she panicked. She had done so many terrible things — stealing her designs, stealing her boyfriend, falsely accusing her of plagiarism. She'd thought Su Qingyuan would hate her, take revenge, make her wish she were dead. But Su Qingyuan did nothing of the kind. She simply removed her from her life, cleanly and completely, like deleting someone you'll never contact again.
That was the cruelest revenge of all — you're still busy hating, and she's already moved on.
Su Yurou picked up her phone, typed a few lines, deleted them, typed again, deleted again. In the end she sent just one thing: "It's raining in Melbourne. How about where you are?" She didn't wait for a reply. She slipped the phone into her pocket, took up her umbrella, and walked out of the café.
The Melbourne rain was still falling, pattering against the canopy of her umbrella.
Back at her rental flat, she changed into dry clothes and cooked herself a bowl of noodles. The noodles were dried ones from the supermarket; the broth was just water and salt, a few slices of luncheon meat, a poached egg nestled on top. She carried the bowl to the table and sat watching the rain outside the window. Melbourne winters were truly cold — colder than a southern hemisphere had any right to be. Her phone lit up. Su Qingyuan's reply was a single sentence: "It's raining in Nancheng. Xixi says rain is the sky watering its flowers."
Su Yurou looked at the line of text, and her tears dropped into the noodle bowl. She raised a hand to wipe them away, sniffled, and wrote back: "Then it must be your sky doing the watering. The Melbourne sky forgot to water today." After she sent it she set the phone down and finished the noodles. They'd gone soft and clumped, the broth had cooled, but she ate every last mouthful.
That night the rain stopped. Su Yurou stood at the window, looking at the distant sky. A seam had torn open in the cloud cover, and moonlight leaked through it, falling onto the wet streets and glittering. She picked up her phone and scrolled to Su Qingyuan's post. The photo before the Wall of Love was still there, the little girl's face lit by sunshine, laughing until her eyes curved into crescent moons. Su Yurou looked at it for a long while, and then she tapped "like." It was the first time she had ever liked one of Su Qingyuan's posts. Before, she hadn't dared; later, she'd been too ashamed; today, for some reason she didn't understand, she simply wanted to. Maybe it was because the Melbourne rain had stopped, maybe because she'd eaten that bowl of noodles, or maybe because — she could finally admit that Su Qingyuan was doing well, and that she, too, was doing all right.
Chen Xiaoman's restaurant was in a small town outside Melbourne, a two-hour drive from the city. It wasn't large — a dozen-odd tables — and it served Chinese food: home-style dishes, nothing fancy, but good. Most of the customers were regulars from nearby who'd order a plate of sweet-and-sour pork ribs, a plate of tomato and scrambled eggs, a bowl of rice, then wipe their mouths and go. Chen Xiaoman was busy every day — shopping for ingredients in the morning, prepping, opening for the lunch rush, resting a while in the afternoon, then back to it in the evening. When the day was done, after she'd closed up, she would cook herself a bowl of noodles and sit on the front steps, looking out at the quiet streets of the little town.
Sometimes she would think of Su Qingyuan — of that studio, of the moonstone that had glowed with three layers of halo under the light. She had secretly photographed that stone and sent the picture to Su Yurou in exchange for fifty thousand yuan. She hadn't spent a single cent of that money; later she'd tried to return it to Su Qingyuan. Su Qingyuan wouldn't take it. "Your mother's ill. Keep it for that," she'd said at the time. In the end her mother passed away anyway. She used that fifty thousand to open this restaurant. Her mother had always said: live so you can face your own conscience. She didn't know whether this restaurant could face her conscience — she hadn't stolen, hadn't cheated, every dish was cooked by her own hands, every cent earned by herself. But she knew that without Su Qingyuan's words, "keep it for that," she might still be drifting along somewhere in a daze.
One day Chen Xiaoman saw news of Su Qingyuan online — the "Light Traces" collection on a global tour: New York, Tokyo, Shanghai, Paris. She looked at the photo of that woman in a white gown, standing in the exhibition hall of Cartier's flagship store, and felt that they didn't belong to the same world. And yet they had once stood so close — close enough for her to photograph the secret on her workbench. She dug out that stolen photo, looked at it for a long time, and then deleted it. It wasn't that she feared leaving evidence; it was that the photo shouldn't exist at all. When she'd taken it, her heart had been full of money. Now her heart held something else — maybe guilt, maybe gratitude, or maybe just a plain, simple wish: I hope you're doing well.
She sent Su Qingyuan a message — not deliberately, but because Melbourne's sunset that evening happened to be lovely, and she'd snapped a photo. The caption: "The sunset here is pink. Can you see it over there?" Su Qingyuan's reply came late: "Nancheng's is blue. Xixi says the sky is the sea turned upside down."
Chen Xiaoman read the words and smiled. She sent back a smiley face, then set down her phone and went on washing the dishes.
Zhou Wanyin had lately been preparing an exhibition of her own — not a jewelry exhibition, but a painting show. She had studied painting when she was young, then changed paths and become a jewelry designer. Decades had passed, and now she'd picked up the brush again. The reason was Lu Xi, Su Qingyuan's daughter. Once, when Zhou Wanyin was visiting Su Qingyuan's home, Lu Xi had been painting in the living room — a painting of flowers, a messy, unidentifiable bouquet, but the colors were lovely: pink, yellow, blue, all mixed together, like an overturned box of paints.
"Xixi, what are you painting?"
"Flowers. For Auntie Wanyin."
Zhou Wanyin looked at the painting, and something stirred in her heart. She realized she hadn't painted in a long time — not for lack of time, but because she'd forgotten. Forgotten that feeling of painting for no purpose at all, just because you wanted to. She told Su Qingyuan she wanted to hold a painting exhibition. Su Qingyuan said, "Do it. I'll help you." Zhou Wanyin laughed. "Aren't you going to ask whether my paintings will even sell?" Su Qingyuan said, "Whether they sell isn't what matters. What matters is that you want to paint."
Zhou Wanyin's eyes went red. She hugged Su Qingyuan. "Qingyuan, thank you." Su Qingyuan patted her back. "Sister Wanyin, you're the one who taught me — doing what you love never wears you out."
The exhibition was held in a very small space in the south of the city, and quite a few of Zhou Wanyin's old customers and old friends came. Su Qingyuan brought Lu Xi. Lu Xi stood in front of one painting, head tilted, studying it for a long while. "Auntie Wanyin, what did you paint?"
"A sunset."
"Why is the sunset blue?"
"Because that's when the sky is about to go dark, when the sunset is almost gone, and the blue is about to cover over the pink."
Lu Xi thought about it. "I think pink is prettier."
Zhou Wanyin smiled. "Then next time I'll paint more pink."
Lu Xi nodded, satisfied, and ran off to find Su Qingyuan. Qu Ya had come too, and she stood before one painting, examining it for a long time. "Wanyin, you studied painting before?"
"I did. A long time ago."
"Why did you stop?"
"Because I felt I wasn't good at it."
"And now?"
"Now I feel whether I'm good or not doesn't matter. As long as I'm happy while I paint."
Qu Ya smiled. "You've finally figured it out." Zhou Wanyin looked at her. "Figured out what?" Qu Ya said, "Making art isn't about proving yourself. It's about expressing yourself." Zhou Wanyin was silent for a few seconds, then smiled. She looked at the paintings around the hall — every one of them had flaws: the composition not quite perfect, the colors not quite harmonious, the brushwork not quite practiced. But they were her paintings, the marks she had left on this world.
After the exhibition ended, Zhou Wanyin gave that blue sunset to Lu Xi. Lu Xi stuck it up at the head of her bed and looked at it for a while every night before sleep. Su Qingyuan asked her, "Xixi, which one do you like best?" Lu Xi said, "I like Auntie Wanyin's sunset best." Su Qingyuan said, "Why?" Lu Xi said, "Because blue sunsets are rare. And rare things are worth loving more."
Su Qingyuan went still for a moment, then smiled. She thought of long ago, when Lu Shiyan had seen her through a glass door and said there was something in her eyes — something very clean, very rare. She looked at her daughter's bright, shining eyes and felt that she too would one day meet someone — after she'd grown up, after she was ready, after she'd become her own light.
That someone would be like her father: he would see her through a glass door, and then he would wait. Wait until she was ready, wait until she turned around and saw him.
Sometimes Su Qingyuan would wonder: if not for that rainy night, where would she be now? Maybe still working in the Su family's shop, maybe married to some ordinary man, maybe still painting, but with no one to see it. She didn't know. She only knew that she was here now — in this courtyard with its osmanthus tree, in this room with its workbench, in this home with Lu Shiyan and Lu Xi.
Lu Xi was five this year. She asked Su Qingyuan, "Mama, will I meet someone like Papa one day too?" Su Qingyuan thought about it. "Not necessarily." Lu Xi looked a little disappointed. "Why?" Su Qingyuan crouched down and retied her crooked braid for her. "Because first you have to become someone like your mama. Once you've become yourself, that person will come."
Lu Xi nodded, half understanding, and ran off to play. Su Qingyuan watched her small figure disappear behind the osmanthus tree, then stood and turned back to her workbench. The sixth piece of the new collection still needed its final step. She picked up her engraving knife and went on polishing. Outside the window, the sunlight was beautiful. The osmanthus had bloomed especially lush this year, the whole tree golden, its fragrance sweet to the point of cloying. Lu Shiyan came out from inside carrying two cups of tea, one for her, one for himself.
"Where's Xixi?"
"Out in the back catching grasshoppers."
"You're not keeping an eye on her?"
"Let her play. When she can't catch any, she'll come back."
Su Qingyuan took the tea and sipped it. It was Longjing, newly bought this year; he said green tea was good to drink in spring.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"What do you think Xixi will do when she grows up?"
"Whatever she does, she'll be wonderful at it."
"Why?"
"Because she's our daughter."
Su Qingyuan laughed and leaned against his shoulder, looking at the osmanthus tree in the courtyard. Sunlight slipped through the gaps in the leaves and fell to the ground, scattering into a sheet of golden light.
From far off, Lu Xi's laughter drifted over. "Mama! I caught one!"
Su Qingyuan straightened up. "Caught what?"
"A grasshopper! A green one!"
"Bring it over and show Mama."
Lu Xi came running, a tiny green grasshopper cupped in her palms, hopping this way and that as she giggled. Su Qingyuan looked at her daughter's little face, glowing in the sunlight, and suddenly remembered many years ago, when she had sat on the attic floor of the old Su family house and painted — painted light. Back then she hadn't known that one day those lights would become real.
Now she knew.

========================================

Chapter 43 — His Point of View (Side Story)
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626245.html

His Point of View
The first time Lu Shiyan saw Su Qingyuan was in the autumn of that year.
The Nancheng Design Association's annual gala — he hadn't meant to go. That kind of event was crowded, noisy, and every person who came over to exchange pleasantries had an agenda. His assistant had said, "Mr. Lu, a brand under the Lu Group has been shortlisted for this year's Design Award. Just show your face." So he went. He spent less than twenty minutes in the banquet hall, dealt with the people who had to be dealt with, and was about to leave. But at the door, he stopped.
Outside the glass doors stood a girl.
She wore a sweater washed pale with age, her hair tied carelessly behind her head, a sketchbook in her hands as she bent over it, drawing something. She was utterly absorbed, her brow faintly furrowed, her lips lightly pressed together, the tip of her pencil darting swiftly across the paper. She had no idea anyone behind the glass was watching her. Lu Shiyan stood there and watched her for a long time. Long enough that his assistant came over and asked in a low voice, "Mr. Lu, the car is ready." He didn't answer. He kept watching. The girl finished her drawing, lifted her head, and looked into the banquet hall. There was no envy in her eyes, no longing — only a very quiet light. That light, he had never seen in anyone else's eyes: neither servile nor proud, neither grasping nor competing, simply looking, as if to say, "One day, I'll stand in there too."
"Find out who that girl is," he said. The assistant blinked. "Which one?" He gestured with his eyes. The assistant looked. "I don't know her. I'll go ask around." Later the assistant told him: the girl's name was Su Qingyuan. She wasn't an invited guest — she'd come to deliver documents. She'd been waiting at the door for nearly an hour.
By the time Lu Shiyan walked out of the banquet hall, she was already gone. The doorway was empty, only the night wind tumbling a few fallen leaves across it. He stood there a while. The assistant asked, "Mr. Lu, shall we go?" He said, "Let's go."
After that, he thought of her now and then. Not deliberately — it would be when he passed the creative district, when he saw a jewelry design magazine, on a rainy night. He would wonder: what was that girl doing now? Was she still drawing? Had anyone seen her light? He thought about going to find her, but he didn't. Because he knew she wasn't ready yet. Her eyes still held that "carefulness" — the kind everyone develops after living too long under someone else's roof. He didn't want to appear before she'd found her footing; that way he'd become her "backer" instead of her "fellow traveler." So he waited for her.
On the night of that rainstorm, he drove down that street. It wasn't coincidence — he knew she lived nearby. Ever since the glass doors, he'd had someone keep an eye on how she was doing. Not surveillance, just occasionally knowing that she was all right. The rain was heavy that night, his headlights cutting the road ahead, and he saw someone crouched at the roadside. Soaked through, hair plastered to her face, her expression impossible to make out — but that silhouette, he remembered it. He stopped the car, opened an umbrella, and walked over. She raised her head, rain mixed with tears, her face indistinguishable between the two. He recognized those eyes — the same as outside the glass doors. They held light.
"Get in the car," he said. Not "Why are you here," not "Let me take you home," but "Get in the car." Because he knew what she needed now wasn't a greeting, but an umbrella, a car, an "I'll take you away" that asked no questions.
She hesitated, then stood and went with him. In the car, she leaned back, trembling all over. He turned the heater up high, took off his coat, and handed it to her. She took it, draped it over her shoulders, and said, "Thank you. Sorry to trouble you." Four words, polite, distant. But he could hear the exhaustion beneath them — the exhaustion of someone who had held on for so long and could finally, at last, stop holding on.
He didn't speak, just drove quietly. He knew what she needed now wasn't comfort, but quiet.
Everything that followed felt like the natural course of things — and also like something he'd long been scheming toward. In the hotel lobby, she was set upon by her foster parents and her sister while he stood watching from a distance. He didn't step in right away, because she didn't need him to fight this battle for her. What she needed was to win it herself. And she won. He watched her back as she walked into the elevator, his heart racing faster than it ever had during any business decision.
He sent her a message: "Are you free tonight?" After he hit send, he felt like a green boy. But he didn't regret it. Because he'd waited too long for her, and didn't want to wait any longer.
At the salon, she wore a white knit dress and talked with the industry's heavyweights, neither servile nor proud, perfectly composed. He stood by the window, holding a glass of water, watching her profile. She didn't know he was watching her — no, she knew. She just didn't turn around.
The keys to her studio — he'd prepared them a long time. From the day she signed the contract with Zhou Wanyin, he had his assistant search the creative district for available spaces. The assistant found several, and he was satisfied with none. Finally the assistant said, "Mr. Lu, why don't you go look yourself?" So he went, and stood in that empty eighty-square-meter room, its windows facing an osmanthus tree, sunlight pouring in, bright and warm. He thought of the look in her eyes outside the glass doors — that "one day I'll stand in there too" light. This studio was her "in there."
He had it remodeled, the walls redone in a warm white, the floor laid with a pale grey self-leveling finish, fitted with a professional workbench and lighting. He put a small sofa in the rest area and set a pot of white chamomile on the windowsill. She liked clean, warm colors. She'd said so once, and he'd remembered.
The day he handed her the keys, she took them, her fingertips brushing his, and her ears turned red. He saw it, but said nothing. Some things don't need to be said; it's more interesting to let her discover them herself.
The proposal ring — he'd considered many designs. A diamond was too ordinary, not her style. He thought of her "Daybreak" — that moonstone with three layers of halo. That was her light, and it should become her ring too. He contacted Mr. Chen at Zhenyi and asked whether they could make a moonstone using the Ripple Cut technique. Mr. Chen said, "Mr. Lu, the parameters of the Ripple Cut are exclusively Miss Su's. I wouldn't dare ask her for them." He said, "Then you try. Try as many times as it takes. I'll cover the cost."
They tried for two months and ruined over a hundred moonstones before finally producing one. Three layers of halo — deep blue on the outside, silver-white in the middle, warm gold at the core. Exactly like the one he'd first seen on "Daybreak." Mr. Chen said, "Mr. Lu, this stone was harder to make than Miss Su's. Because there were no parameters." He looked at the moonstone and said, "Worth it."
The inside of the band was to be engraved, and he thought long about the words. In the end he engraved: "From the glass doors to the aurora — from now on, it's you." The glass doors were the beginning; the aurora was the promise he made her. From now on, it's you — not "I like you," not "I love you," but "from now on, it's you." Because "like" and "love" might change, but "from now on" wouldn't.
He left the ring at her door rather than handing it to her himself. Because he was afraid that if he were there, she'd be too embarrassed to cry. He wanted her to see it alone, cry alone, and put on the ring alone. And then, by the time he appeared, she would already have her answer ready.
She did cry, just as he'd expected. He hid around the bend of the stairwell, listening to her sobbing as she crouched at the door, and his heart ached a little. He waited a while, until she had finished crying, before stepping out. She was crouched there, the ring cradled in her hands, her eyes red, like a little cat that had just been picked up off the street. He asked, "You put the ring on. Does it fit?" She raised her hand, the moonstone glowing softly under the streetlight. "It fits." He asked again, "And the answer?" Before he could finish "Will you" — she cut him off. "I will. I have since the day you said, 'Tell me once you're sure.'"
In that moment, he felt he'd spent all the luck of his entire life on this one moment.
On their wedding day, she wore a white dress she'd designed herself, the osmanthus sprigs along the hem glittering in the sunlight. As she walked toward him, his eyes grew hot. Not from being moved, but because he thought of that girl outside the glass doors — in a sweater washed pale, standing at the door, waiting for an hour. No one knew she was standing there. No one knew what she was drawing. But he, behind the glass, had seen it. He had seen her light. Back then he hadn't known that beam of light would illuminate all the days of his life to come.
Now he knew.
The day Lu Xi was born, he stood outside the delivery room, his palms slick with sweat. He had negotiated countless times and never once been nervous. But in that moment, he was nervous. Not because he feared something would go wrong — he trusted the best doctors and the best equipment were all there. It was because the person he was about to meet was the "from now on" of him and Su Qingyuan.
When the nurse carried her out, the little thing was wrinkled, eyes shut, lips puckering in and out. He looked at that tiny face, and a voice inside him said — this is it. He reached out and awkwardly took her, like cradling a fragile treasure. She was so small, so light, so soft. His tears fell. When Su Qingyuan was wheeled out of the delivery room and saw him crying, she smiled. "You're crying?" she said. He wiped his eyes. "No." She said, "Liar." He didn't argue. He walked over and bent down to kiss her forehead. "You worked hard."
She said, "It wasn't hard." Then she reached out and touched their daughter's face. "She looks like you." He said, "Her eyes look like yours." She smiled, and after smiling she cried again. He held her hand and stayed with her, until she had finished crying.
When Lu Xi was three, she asked a question. "Daddy, how did you meet Mommy?" He thought for a moment. "Behind a glass door." Lu Xi didn't understand. "Behind a glass door?" He said, "Mm. Mommy was outside the door, and Daddy was inside. She didn't know Daddy was watching her." Lu Xi asked again, "Then why didn't you go out and find her?" He said, "Because Mommy wasn't ready yet." Lu Xi blinked, half understanding. "Is she ready now?" He smiled. "She's ready." Lu Xi nodded, satisfied. "Good, then."
He picked his daughter up and walked to the window. Down in the yard, Su Qingyuan was crouched beneath the osmanthus tree gathering fallen blossoms, picking them up carefully, one by one, and placing them in a bamboo basket. She'd said she was going to make osmanthus preserve to spread on Lu Xi's bread.
The sunlight fell over her, the wind tousling her hair a little, a few stray strands brushing the side of her face. She wore that old sweater — not the one from outside the glass doors, that one she'd long stopped wearing, but this one was white too. She liked white. As if she'd sensed his gaze, she lifted her head and smiled at him.
That smile was a world apart from the one outside the glass doors. Back then she wouldn't have smiled like that; back then her smile was careful, edged with a wish to please. Now her smile was open, sure, the smile of someone who knew she was loved.
He set his daughter down, walked out of the house, and went over to her. "How many have you gathered?"
"Half a basket. That's enough. Let's go back and make the preserve."
"I'll carry it for you."
She stood, handed him the bamboo basket, and patted the dirt off her knees. He took the basket, and with his other hand he took hers. She didn't pull away — she held his hand back.
The two of them walked back to the house, behind them a ground strewn with golden osmanthus and a whole yard full of sweet fragrance.
Sometimes Lu Shiyan wondered: if not for that rainy night, where would he be now? Perhaps still in his office on the eighty-eighth floor, looking out at the night view alone. Perhaps he'd think now and then of that girl outside the glass doors, but he wouldn't go find her, because he'd believe she wasn't ready yet. He didn't know that she had, in fact, always been ready. It was only that he had appeared at exactly the right moment — not too early, not too late, on a rainy night.
He was grateful it rained that day. Grateful she hadn't brought an umbrella. Grateful he'd driven down that street. Grateful that when she crouched at the roadside, he'd stopped the car. Grateful that she'd said "Sorry to trouble you," and he'd said "Get in the car." Grateful that all the gratitude had turned into "from now on."

========================================

Chapter 44 — Light and Xi (Side Story)
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626246.html

Light and Xi
The year Lu Xi turned six, the final stop of Su Qingyuan's "Light Traces" world tour returned to Nancheng. Cartier set the exhibition in the ground-floor lobby of Lu Group — the very same place where the "Roots and Light" joint exhibition had been held all those years ago. Standing in that familiar hall, looking at the pieces arranged in their display cases, Su Qingyuan felt something strange well up inside her. Five years ago, she had held a joint exhibition here, so nervous she couldn't sleep. Five years later, she stood in the same spot, and her heart held nothing but calm.
"Mama, what's this?" Lu Xi pointed at the diamond necklace half-suspended in its setting inside the case.
"That's a necklace Mama made. It's called 'Light Traces.'"
"Why is it called 'Light Traces'?"
"Because light leaves traces behind. Look at this diamond — it hangs here, and when the light shines up from below, it casts a shadow across your collarbone. That shadow is the trace of the light."
Lu Xi tilted her head and thought about it. "Then if I stand here looking at Mama's work, am I a trace of light too?"
Su Qingyuan froze for a moment, then crouched down and looked into her daughter's bright, sparkling eyes. "Yes. You're Mama's most important trace of light."
Lu Xi smiled and threw her arms around Su Qingyuan's neck. "Mama, when I grow up I want to be someone just like you."
Su Qingyuan patted her back. "You don't have to be like Mama. Just be like yourself."
After the exhibition opened, Su Qingyuan gave an interview to a media outlet. The reporter asked, "Ms. Su, your work has gone from 'First Light' to 'Light Traces' to 'Roots,' and now your recent 'Everyday' series. The themes keep changing. What is it that sustains your creativity?"
Su Qingyuan thought about it. "Life."
"Life?"
"Mm. I used to think that creating took inspiration, took talent, took hard work. Later I realized what it really takes is life. You live your days well, you eat with care, you walk with care, you love someone with care — and all those feelings turn into work on their own."
The reporter laughed. "Then what inspiration has your life now given to your next piece?"
Su Qingyuan glanced at Lu Shiyan, standing in a corner of the hall — he was holding Lu Xi's hand, helping her look at the pieces in the cases. Father and daughter had their heads bowed, leaning together, murmuring about something. Suddenly Lu Xi burst out laughing, a crisp, clear sound, like a wind chime.
"My next piece," Su Qingyuan turned back to face the reporter, "might be called 'Xi.'"
The year Lu Xi turned seven, she learned to ride a bicycle.
Su Qingyuan stood in the courtyard, watching her wobble around in circle after circle, while Lu Shiyan ran along behind, holding the seat, drenched in sweat.
"Papa, don't let go!" Lu Xi shouted.
"I won't!" Lu Shiyan shouted back — though he had already quietly let go.
Lu Xi had ridden a dozen meters before she realized there was no one behind her. She panicked, the handlebars veered, and she went toppling — bike and all — into the grass at the side of the path. Su Qingyuan ran over and fished her out of the weeds. Lu Xi didn't cry; she just sat there, dazed, staring at the bicycle lying on the ground.
"Does it hurt?" Su Qingyuan asked.
"No."
"Then why aren't you crying?"
Lu Xi lifted her head and looked at Su Qingyuan. "Papa said crying doesn't solve anything."
Su Qingyuan turned and shot Lu Shiyan a look. He was just walking over, a guilty flicker on his face. She smiled and reached out to brush the bits of grass off Lu Xi. "Papa's right — but when you feel like crying, you can cry too. Cry it out first, then solve the problem."
Lu Xi thought about it. "Then I want to cry now."
"Then cry."
Lu Xi's lips puckered, and the tears came pattering down. Su Qingyuan gathered her into her arms and gently patted her back. Lu Shiyan stood off to the side, at a loss for what to do. Su Qingyuan glared at him. "Go pick up her bike." He hurried off to right the bicycle.
When Lu Xi had finished crying, she wiped her eyes, stood up, and said, "Mama, I want to ride again."
"Okay. Mama will hold it for you."
"I don't want Mama to hold it. I want to ride by myself."
Looking at her red-rimmed eyes and that stubborn expression, Su Qingyuan suddenly felt that she had grown up. Not in body — in spirit. That certainty of "I can do it," so much like her own self back in the day.
Lu Xi rode off. This time she didn't fall. She rode circle after circle, the wind tossing her hair into a tangle, laughing with delight. Su Qingyuan stood in the courtyard, watching her daughter's figure on the bike, and Lu Shiyan came over to stand beside her.
"Like you," he said.
"How am I like that?"
"That refusing-to-lose look."
Su Qingyuan laughed. "Like you. You're the same when you negotiate a deal — you fall, you climb right back up."
Lu Shiyan reached out and pulled her against his shoulder. "It's us."
In the distance, Lu Xi rode her bicycle and shouted toward them, "Papa! Mama! I can ride!" Su Qingyuan waved at her, the corners of her mouth curving up.
The year Lu Xi turned eight, Su Qingyuan received a letter from Australia.
Not from Su Yurou — Su Yurou hadn't written in a long time. Once in a while she'd post a photo of the Melbourne sky to her social feed, and Su Qingyuan would give it a like, occasionally commenting "Beautiful." This letter was from Chen Xiaoman. Inside was a photograph: a small restaurant with a wooden sign hanging by the door that read "Qingyuan's Little Kitchen."
Su Qingyuan stared at that wooden sign for a long while. On the back of the photo was a line of writing: "Sister Qingyuan, my restaurant has opened. I used your name for it — you don't mind, do you? I just wanted to say, without you, there would be no me today. — Xiaoman."
Su Qingyuan showed the photo to Lu Shiyan. He looked at the wooden sign and was silent for a few seconds. "Do you mind?"
Su Qingyuan thought about it. "I don't mind. If she could use my name, it means she thinks I'm a good person."
Lu Shiyan looked at her. "Of course you're good."
Su Qingyuan smiled, picked up her phone, and sent Chen Xiaoman a message: "Got it. The name is lovely. May business flourish."
Chen Xiaoman's reply came quickly: "Thank you, Sister Qingyuan. I'll do my very best."
Su Qingyuan set down her phone and stuck the photo on the wall of her studio. The wall was already covered with photos — Lu Xi's one-month portrait, the scene at Zhou Wanyin's painting exhibition, Qu Ya's new work, the Nordic aurora, the Wall of Love in Paris, the Empire State Building in New York. Every single photo was a light in her life.
The year Lu Xi turned nine, she asked a question.
"Mama, did you used to suffer a lot?"
Su Qingyuan was making osmanthus jam, and her hands paused. "Who told you that?"
"Auntie Wanyin. She said Mama had a hard time before, that you went through a lot of suffering."
Su Qingyuan set down the spoon and looked at her daughter. "Do you think Mama suffers?"
Lu Xi thought about it. "Before, you suffered. Now you don't anymore."
"Why?"
"Because now you have Papa, and you have me."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot. She reached out and gently ruffled her daughter's hair. "Mm. Now I don't suffer anymore."
Lu Xi looked up at her. "Mama, when I grow up I want to be like you too. Not afraid of suffering, as long as it can turn into something sweet."
Su Qingyuan's tears fell. She held her daughter close and buried her face in her small shoulder. "You've already turned into something sweet."
Lu Xi reached out and clumsily patted her back. "Don't cry, Mama. Crying makes you not pretty."
Su Qingyuan laughed through her tears. She let go of her daughter, wiped her eyes, and said, "Mama isn't crying. It's the osmanthus jam — it's too sweet, so sweet it makes Mama's eyes sting."
Lu Xi looked at her and didn't call out the lie. She scooped up a spoonful of the osmanthus jam, put it in her mouth, and smacked her lips. "It really is sweet."
Looking at her daughter's bright, sparkling eyes, Su Qingyuan suddenly felt that all that bitterness really had turned to sweetness.
The year Lu Xi turned ten, Su Qingyuan planted a new osmanthus tree in the courtyard.
Not the original one — that one was still there, blooming every year, fragrant every year. This new one stood beside the old osmanthus tree, small, no taller than a person. Lu Xi asked, "Mama, why are you planting a new one?"
Su Qingyuan crouched down and pressed the soil firm. "Because your Papa planted the old one. This one is for you."
"For me?"
"Mm. When you grow up, it'll grow up too. When you bloom, it'll bloom too."
Lu Xi crouched down and looked at the little osmanthus tree. "Then when I grow up, I want to be just like it."
"Like what?"
"Standing here. Blooming. All fragrant."
Su Qingyuan smiled and pulled her daughter into a hug. "Okay. Standing here. Blooming. All fragrant."
Lu Shiyan walked out of the house carrying two cups of tea. Looking at the mother and daughter crouched together in the courtyard, hands covered in mud, the corners of his mouth lifted. He set the tea on the stone table, walked over, and crouched down. "All planted?"
"Done." Su Qingyuan stood up and brushed the soil off her knees.
Lu Shiyan looked at the little tree. "When will it bloom?"
"Next year."
"Then we'll all watch it together next year."
"Okay."
Lu Xi stood up and tugged at the hem of Lu Shiyan's shirt. "Papa, crouch down."
Lu Shiyan crouched down. Lu Xi rose onto her tiptoes and kissed him on the cheek. "Thank you, Papa, for planting the old osmanthus tree. Thank you, Mama, for planting the new osmanthus tree. Thank you both, for letting me be here."
Lu Shiyan went still. Su Qingyuan went still too. Then the two of them smiled at the same moment, and as they smiled, their eyes turned red.
Lu Xi looked at them. "Why are you crying?"
"We're not crying," Lu Shiyan said.
"What about Mama?"
"Mama isn't crying either." Su Qingyuan wiped the corner of her eye.
Lu Xi looked at them and smiled. "You grown-ups really aren't honest."
Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan exchanged a glance, and both of them laughed.
That night, Su Qingyuan leaned against Lu Shiyan's shoulder, sitting beneath the osmanthus tree in the courtyard. The moonlight was lovely, lighting the tree full of osmanthus blossoms until they looked like scattered gold. Lu Xi was already asleep, and the courtyard was very quiet, with only the calls of the autumn insects.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Do you think — what will Xi-Xi do when she grows up?"
"Whatever she does, she'll do it well."
"Why do you always say that?"
"Because I always feel that way."
Su Qingyuan laughed. She reached out and clasped his hand. "Lu Shiyan, thank you."
"Thank me for what?"
"Thank you for stopping the car that day."
Lu Shiyan lowered his head to look at her. The moonlight fell across her face, and there was light in her eyes — exactly the same as that day outside the glass door.
"You're welcome."
He lowered his head and kissed her forehead. Osmanthus blossoms fell onto their shoulders, like golden snow. In the distance, the lights of the city merged into a single warm sea of light. In the courtyard, the old osmanthus tree and the newly planted one stood side by side, one tall, one small. Like them — and like the two of them as well.
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes and breathed in the fragrance of the osmanthus. She thought back to many years ago, when she sat on the attic floorboards of the old Su family house, drawing. She had drawn light. Back then she hadn't known that one day, that light would become real.
Now she knew.
That light had become Lu Shiyan, had become Lu Xi, had become this courtyard full of osmanthus fragrance, had become the scalding, real happiness she held in her hands.

========================================

Chapter 45 — The Direction of Light (Side Story)
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626247.html

The year Lu Xi turned twelve, she picked up a carving knife for the first time.
Not a toy—it was a carving knife Su Qingyuan took down from her workbench, one she'd used for many years. The handle had been worn smooth, and the blade gave off a cold, white gleam under the lamp. When Su Qingyuan handed her the knife, her expression was more solemn than at any launch she'd ever held for her own work. "This knife has been with Mama for a long time. Today I'm lending it to you."
"Why lend it?"
"Because you're going to use this knife to carve out the thing inside your heart."
Lu Xi took the carving knife. It was heavy—heavier than she'd imagined. She sat down at the workbench. In front of her lay a moonstone—not large, ordinary in quality, its surface threaded with fine cracks. Su Qingyuan hadn't picked the material for her; Lu Xi had chosen it herself from the pile of rough stones. She'd searched for a long time, and in the end picked up this small, unremarkable stone. "Mama, I want to carve this one."
Su Qingyuan glanced at the stone and didn't ask why. "All right. First sketch a draft on paper, and once you've finished it, then set the knife to the stone."
Lu Xi held the pencil and drew in her sketchbook for a long while. Drawing and erasing, erasing and drawing, over and over again. Su Qingyuan didn't rush her; she sat beside her, quietly sipping tea. Sunlight poured in through the window and fell over mother and daughter, warm and golden. An hour later, Lu Xi turned the sketchbook around to show Su Qingyuan.
On it was a tree—not a whole tree, but the roots of one: thick, sturdy roots driving deep into the soil, crisscrossing and entwined, like blood vessels. It was very much like that photograph of Qu Ya's, but the lines were more childlike, more tender, carrying the particular touch of a twelve-year-old girl.
"You want to carve this tree?"
"Mm. I want to carve roots."
Su Qingyuan looked at her, silent for a few seconds. "Why do you want to carve roots?"
Lu Xi thought it over. "Because the roots are the part you can't see. But they're very important."
Su Qingyuan's nose stung. She remembered the year she was twenty-six, creating the "Roots" series—she'd thought the very same thing. She reached out and gently ruffled her daughter's hair. "All right. Mama will teach you."
The first cut was the hardest. Lu Xi held the carving knife, her hand trembling a little. Su Qingyuan didn't grip the knife for her; she only laid her own hand over the back of Lu Xi's, steadying it. "Take your time. If one cut isn't enough, make another. It doesn't matter if you carve it wrong—the stone can be replaced. But the thing in your heart, you can't leave it uncarved just because you're afraid of carving it wrong."
Lu Xi drew a deep breath and made the first cut. The feel of the knife biting into the stone traveled up through her fingertips—a little rough, a little cool. She watched the blade leave a shallow trace on the stone's surface, her heart pounding fast, but her hand no longer trembling.
That afternoon, Lu Xi carved at that moonstone for a long time. Su Qingyuan stayed beside her the whole while, offering a word of guidance now and then, but most of the time simply watching in silence. When Lu Shiyan came home from work, he stood in the doorway of the studio, saw mother and daughter with their heads bent close together over the workbench, and didn't go in. He leaned against the doorframe and watched for a long time. Not until Lu Xi lifted her head and called out to him: "Papa! Look what I carved!"
He walked over and bent down to look at the stone. The lines were still clumsy, the composition not yet balanced, but in those cut-by-cut traces there was something very familiar to him—seriousness. The exact same seriousness as her mother's.
"It's beautiful," he said.
"Really?"
"Really. A lot better than the first time Papa ever cooked."
Lu Xi laughed and turned to look at Su Qingyuan. "Mama, can I carve again tomorrow?"
"You can. I'll keep lending you this knife."
"I don't have to give it back?"
"When you're done with it, just return it to Mama."
Lu Xi carefully set the carving knife down on the workbench, hopped off her chair, and ran outside to play.
Su Qingyuan watched her daughter's retreating figure and smiled. Lu Shiyan stood beside her. "Is she going to study jewelry design?"
"Not necessarily. Maybe it's just a passing whim."
"It won't be," Lu Shiyan said. "She's like you. Once she's set her mind on something, she won't let go of it easily."
Su Qingyuan turned to look at him. "How do you know?"
"Because I watched you do it."
Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder, looking at the carving knife on the workbench. Fine stone dust still clung to the blade, glittering under the lamp. She remembered, long ago, that she too had a carving knife, bought with money she'd saved up over a long time. She'd used that knife for many years, and later gave it to a young apprentice at Zhou Wanyin's studio. She didn't know how that apprentice was doing now, but her carving knife was surely still somewhere, carving out the light in someone's heart.
The year Lu Xi turned thirteen, she carved her first complete piece.
Still the roots of a tree, but far more mature than that sketch from a year before. The lines were more fluid, the composition more balanced, the details richer—at the place where the roots crossed she'd carved a few fine cracks, like wounds, and also like the traces of healing. Su Qingyuan looked at the piece and was silent for a long time.
"Mama, is it no good?" Lu Xi was a little nervous.
"It's not that it's no good." Su Qingyuan picked up the piece and held it to the light. "It's that it's too good."
"Too good?"
"Mm. At thirteen, you've carved what Mama only managed to carve at twenty-six."
Lu Xi was stunned. She lowered her head and looked at her own hands. There were calluses in her palms—growing in the very same spot as the calluses on Su Qingyuan's hands.
"Mama, can I keep going?"
"You can. But you have to promise Mama one thing."
"What thing?"
"Don't carve for other people. Carve for yourself. For the light in your own heart."
Lu Xi nodded. "I promise."
Su Qingyuan placed the piece in the display case, setting it alongside her own "Roots" series. One large and one small, one tall and one short, like two trees—one an old tree, one a fresh seedling. When Lu Shiyan came home from work and saw a new piece in the display case, he crouched down and looked at it for a long time.
"Xixi carved this?"
"Mm."
"When did she carve it?"
"These past few months. After school, on weekends. When you weren't home."
Lu Shiyan stood up and looked at Su Qingyuan. "You taught her?"
"She taught herself. I only gave a little guidance now and then."
Lu Shiyan was silent for a few seconds, then said: "Su Qingyuan, you have a wonderful daughter."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "So do you."
The year Lu Xi turned fourteen, Su Qingyuan received an email from the Biennale organizing committee. It wasn't an invitation for her—it was an invitation for Lu Xi. A "Young Artists Special Program," open to young creators aged fourteen to eighteen from all over the world; those selected could hold a solo exhibition during the Biennale.
Su Qingyuan showed Lu Xi the email. Lu Xi read it and was silent for a long while.
"Mama, I want to take part."
"All right."
"You're not going to ask whether I'm confident?"
"No. Because if you dare to take part, that means you're ready."
Lu Xi held her phone, her eyes reddening. "Mama, I'm afraid I won't do it well."
Su Qingyuan crouched down to meet her at eye level. "Xixi, Mama has been making pieces for all these years, and every single one has its regrets. There's no such thing as a perfect work—only a sincere one. If you're sincere, others will see it."
Lu Xi nodded and wiped away her tears. "I understand."
Lu Xi spent three months preparing her work for the Biennale. She didn't make jewelry—she made an installation piece: the roots of a tree. Not carved stone, not metal, but real tree roots she'd gathered from the hill behind her school. She'd collected many, large and small, all different in shape. She washed them, dried them, and bound them together with fine wire to make a new "tree." This tree's roots were aboveground and its branches and trunk below—she hung the roots upside down so that what viewers saw were the roots, not the branches and leaves.
The piece was titled "The Part You Can't See." The theme was the same as her mother's "Roots" series, but the expression was entirely different. When Su Qingyuan saw the piece, she cried. Not from sorrow—it was the kind of tears that come from "the child has grown up," both proud and reluctant to let go.
Lu Shiyan stood behind her and handed her a tissue. "She's like you."
Su Qingyuan wiped her tears. "She's braver than I am."
"You're very brave too."
"No. Back when I made 'Roots,' I was so nervous I couldn't sleep."
Su Qingyuan looked at the piece and smiled. "But I still made it."
The Biennale was in Venice. It was the first time Lu Xi traveled abroad on her own; Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan went with her. On the day of setting up the exhibition, Lu Xi wouldn't let Su Qingyuan help. "Mama, I'll do it myself." She climbed up and down, adjusting the angles of those tree roots, her hands covered in dust, her forehead covered in sweat. Su Qingyuan stood outside the exhibition hall, watching her through the glass door. Lu Shiyan stood beside her. "Are you nervous?"
"I'm not nervous. She can do it."
"How do you know?"
"Because she's our daughter."
Lu Shiyan laughed.
On opening day, many people came to see Lu Xi's piece. A white-haired old lady stood before "The Part You Can't See" for a long time, then asked Lu Xi: "Child, why did you put the roots on top?" In her not-quite-fluent English, Lu Xi said: "Because people always see the branches and leaves, and forget the roots. I wanted them to see the part you can't see." The old lady nodded, her eyes reddening. "I'm eighty years old, and this is the first time anyone has let me see the roots."
Lu Xi was stunned. She turned to look toward the entrance of the hall, where Su Qingyuan stood, giving her a smile. She smiled too, her eyes curving into little crescents.
After returning from Venice, Lu Xi donated "The Part You Can't See" to her school. The school placed it at the entrance of the library, where everyone passing by would see those upside-down tree roots. Someone asked Lu Xi: "Doesn't it hurt to give it away? Once you've donated it, it's gone." Lu Xi said: "It means more sitting there than sitting at my home."
Su Qingyuan knew about this. She didn't say "you're amazing," and she didn't say "are you sure you can part with it." She simply cooked a meal—all of Lu Xi's favorite dishes. Sweet-and-sour ribs, stir-fried seasonal greens, steamed sea bass, tomato and egg-drop soup.
Lu Xi looked at the table full of food. "Mama, what's the occasion today?"
"Today is the day you gave your light to someone else."
Lu Xi lowered her head, picked up her chopsticks, and took a piece of rib. "Mama, thank you."
"Thank me for what?"
"Thank you for lending me that carving knife."
Su Qingyuan's eyes grew hot, but she didn't cry. She placed a piece of fish in Lu Xi's bowl. "You're welcome. From now on, lend your carving knife to others too."
Lu Xi nodded.
That night, Su Qingyuan leaned against Lu Shiyan's shoulder, sitting beneath the osmanthus tree in the courtyard. The moonlight was lovely, lighting the tree full of blossoms until they looked like scattered gold. Lu Xi was already asleep, and the courtyard was very quiet, with only the sound of the autumn insects.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Tell me, what do you think Xixi will do someday?"
"I don't know. But she already knows to give her light to others."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "Mm. She's better at it than I am."
"You're good at it too. You've given your light to so many people—me, Xixi, Wanyin, Qu Ya, Xiaoman, and all the people who wear your work."
Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder and closed her eyes. "So have you. You're my light."
Lu Shiyan lowered his head and kissed her forehead. "And you're mine."
Far off, the city's lights merged into a warm sea of light. In the courtyard, the old osmanthus tree and the newly planted one stood side by side, one tall, one short. The moonlight fell over them, as if plating them with a layer of silver.
Su Qingyuan opened her eyes and looked at the two trees. "Lu Shiyan, when Xixi grows up, she'll leave home and go somewhere far away."
"Mm."
"Will it be hard for you to let her go?"
"It will."
"Then what do we do?"
"Let her go. And then wait for her to come back."
Su Qingyuan smiled and took his hand. "You're always so easy about things."
"It's not that I'm easy about it. It's that I know—no matter how far she goes, this place is her roots."
Su Qingyuan looked at him; the moonlight fell across his face, his profile still as handsome as ever. She suddenly remembered, many years ago, a rainy night—she'd been crouching at the side of the road, and a car had stopped. The window came down, revealing his face. He'd said: "Get in." She got in, and from then on she never got out again.
That night was the end of the storm, and also the beginning of the dawn.
And the dawn has been here ever since.

========================================

Chapter 46 — Light Years Away (Side Story)
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626248.html

Light Years Away
The year Lu Xi turned eighteen, two university acceptance letters arrived. One was from Central Saint Martins in London, the other from the Cartier Design Academy in Paris. She sat beneath the osmanthus tree in the courtyard, turning the two letters over in her hands again and again.
Su Qingyuan came out carrying two cups of tea and handed her one. "Have you decided?"
"No." Lu Xi took the tea and sipped it. "Mom, how did you choose, back then?"
Su Qingyuan sat down beside her and thought for a moment. "I didn't choose. Back then there was only one option—to keep moving forward."
Lu Xi was quiet for a while. "So should I pick the better one?"
"Which one is better?"
"The Cartier Design Academy. They offered a full scholarship, and after I graduate I could go straight to work at Cartier."
"Do you want to go?"
Lu Xi lowered her head, her fingers tracing the edge of the envelope. "I do. But I'm afraid people will say I got in because of my mother's connections."
Su Qingyuan looked at her and smiled. "Xixi, do you know what I was most afraid of, back then?"
"What?"
"I was most afraid people would say I was leaning on your father. Later I figured it out—what other people say doesn't matter. What matters is whether you yourself know what it is you're standing on."
Lu Xi lifted her head. "What am I standing on?"
"Your hands." Su Qingyuan took hold of her hand. "Your hands can carve stone, can sketch designs, can turn the light inside you into something real. That—no one can ever take it from you."
Lu Xi looked at her own hands. There were calluses on her palms, in exactly the same place as the calluses on Su Qingyuan's. She curled her fingers into a fist. "Mom, I understand now. I'll go to London."
"Why London?"
"Because Central Saint Martins is harder to get into. A place that's hard to get into proves I made it on my own."
Su Qingyuan smiled and reached out to ruffle her daughter's hair. "Good. Mom supports you."
The day Lu Xi left, a light rain was falling over Nancheng. Su Qingyuan stood in the airport departure hall, adjusting the scarf around Lu Xi's neck. "Call me when you land."
"Okay."
"Don't skip meals every day."
"Okay."
"Put on more clothes when it gets cold."
"Okay. Mom, if you keep going, I'm going to cry."
Su Qingyuan's eyes had already gone red. She didn't cry; she just reached out and held her daughter. "Xixi, Mom is proud of you."
Lu Xi's tears fell. She buried her face against Su Qingyuan's shoulder and cried for a while. Then she let go and turned to look at Lu Shiyan. He stood a step away, pushing the suitcase, his expression calm. But Lu Xi saw that his eyes had reddened too.
"Dad."
"Mm."
"I'm leaving."
"Mm. Call when you land."
"Okay."
Lu Shiyan stepped over and put his arms around his daughter. He held her tightly—tighter than any time before. Lu Xi froze for a moment, then hugged him back just as tightly.
"Dad, I've grown up."
"Mm. You've grown up."
"Don't worry about me."
"Okay."
Lu Xi released him, stepped back, wiped her tears, and smiled. "I'm going now. Take care of yourselves."
She turned and wheeled her suitcase toward the security checkpoint. Su Qingyuan watched her retreating figure—thin, her back held very straight. She remembered, years ago, how she too had wheeled a suitcase away from the old Su family house. Back then no one had seen her off, no one had told her to "call when you land." But her daughter had that. She had a mother who loved her, a father who loved her, and a whole courtyard of osmanthus-tree memories.
"She'll be just fine," Lu Shiyan said, standing beside her.
Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder. "Mm. She'll be just fine."
In her first week in London, Lu Xi posted a long update on her social feed. She wrote about London's weather, the school cafeteria, her dorm roommates, and how she'd felt after giving her first presentation in English. The last paragraph she wrote for Su Qingyuan: "Mom, today I used the carving knife you gave me. A classmate said it was beautiful and asked where I bought it. I told them: it's my mother's. When she was young, she used this knife to carve out her own light. Now I'm going to use it too—to carve out mine."
Su Qingyuan read that post and cried. Lu Shiyan handed her a tissue. "She's like you."
Su Qingyuan wiped her tears. "She's braver than I was."
"You were brave too."
"No. When I went to Paris back then, I was so nervous my palms were sweating."
"But you still went."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "Mm. I still went."
The year Lu Xi turned twenty-two, she graduated. She didn't return to Nancheng; she stayed in London and joined an independent jewelry studio. The studio was small—only five people, including her—but the work they made had real soul. Su Qingyuan saw her pieces online and sent her a message: "Very good. Better than I was at your age."
Lu Xi's reply came quickly: "Impossible. Your 'First Light' collection from back then—I couldn't make that in a lifetime."
Su Qingyuan: "You can. Your light is just different from mine."
Lu Xi: "Different how?"
Su Qingyuan: "My light grew out of darkness. Your light grew up in sunlight. Both are bright—but they're not the same."
Lu Xi was silent for a long time. Then she replied: "Mom, thank you for raising me in the sunlight."
Su Qingyuan looked at those words, and the tears fell again.
The year Lu Xi turned twenty-five, she started dating a boyfriend. An Englishman named James, an architecture student, who wore round-framed glasses and spoke in a slow, deliberate way. The first time Su Qingyuan had a video call with him, he was so nervous he spilled his coffee on his white shirt.
"Hello, Auntie. I'm James."
"Hello. Are you drinking coffee?"
"I just spilled it. I'll skip it."
Su Qingyuan laughed. Lu Xi, beside him, was doubled over laughing. James turned red. "Xi, stop laughing."
Lu Xi only laughed harder. Lu Shiyan walked into frame from off-screen, holding a cup of tea. "Who's this?"
"Xixi's boyfriend," Su Qingyuan said.
Lu Shiyan glanced at the red-faced young man on the screen with the coffee stain on his shirt and was silent for a few seconds. "Hello."
"Hello, Uncle."
"Would you like some tea?"
"No… no thank you, Uncle."
Su Qingyuan smiled and ended the call. Lu Shiyan sat down beside her. "What do you think?"
"Think of what?"
"That James."
"He's nice. Sincere."
Lu Shiyan considered it. "Mm. Sincerity matters most."
That autumn, James came to Nancheng. He brought two bottles of red wine, a box of chocolates, and a bouquet of white chamomile. When Su Qingyuan saw the chamomile, she froze. "How did you know I like these?"
James pushed up his glasses. "Xi told me."
Su Qingyuan turned to glance at Lu Xi, who stuck out her tongue. Su Qingyuan smiled and took the flowers. "Come in."
James stayed in Nancheng for a week. Su Qingyuan took him to the creative park, the lobby of the Lu Group building, that Hunan restaurant, and the replica of the Montmartre Wall of Love—there was a small park in Nancheng with a Wall of Love too, built with donations from Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan. James stood before the Wall of Love, looking at the wall covered in "I love you"s, then suddenly turned to Lu Xi and said, in Chinese: "I love you."
Lu Xi froze. "When did you learn that?"
"Yesterday. Uncle taught me."
Lu Xi turned to look at Lu Shiyan. He stood a little way off, expression calm. Su Qingyuan stood beside him, holding back a laugh. "Dad!" Lu Xi cried. Lu Shiyan didn't change his expression. "He asked me. I just told him how to pronounce those three words." Su Qingyuan finally laughed out loud.
The year Lu Xi turned twenty-eight, she got married. The wedding was held at that manor in Nancheng—the same place where Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan had married, years ago. Beneath the osmanthus trees, white banquet tables, the whole courtyard filled with sweet fragrance. Lu Xi wore a wedding gown of her own design—white satin, osmanthus branches embroidered along the hem, much like her mother's dress from years before, but not quite the same. On her hem there was one small extra butterfly. She said the butterfly meant freedom.
James stood beneath the osmanthus trees, watching Lu Xi walk toward him, his eyes reddening. He wiped his eyes, and they reddened again. Su Qingyuan sat in the first row, watching her daughter walk step by step toward that young man, gripping Lu Shiyan's hand very tightly.
"Are you nervous?" Lu Shiyan asked quietly.
"I'm not nervous."
"Then why is my hand about to be crushed?"
Su Qingyuan smiled, loosened her grip, then took his hand again. "Xixi looks so beautiful today."
"Mm. Like you."
Lu Xi reached James, and the two of them stood face to face. There was no officiant, no long vows. Lu Xi said, "James, thank you for coming all that way to find me." James said, "Xi, thank you for letting me find you." Then they exchanged rings—Lu Xi gave James a plain platinum band, and James gave Lu Xi a moonstone ring with four layers of glow. The very center was deep blue, then silver-white, then warm gold, and the outermost layer was a ring of transparent, almost invisible light. Lu Xi looked at the ring and froze. "This is…"
"Your mother's formula. I asked Uncle for it." James's voice trembled a little. "The fourth layer of light—she said it was called 'Future.' But I think 'Xi' suits it better."
Lu Xi's tears fell. She turned to look at Su Qingyuan. Su Qingyuan sat there, smiling, her tears falling too.
The year Lu Xi turned thirty-two, she gave birth to a pair of twins. A boy and a girl—the elder brother named Lu Yinuo, the younger sister named Lu Yiyan. Su Qingyuan had become a grandmother.
She held her little granddaughter, standing beneath the osmanthus tree in the courtyard. The little one's round eyes were wide open, gazing at the canopy full of small golden flowers, reaching out to grab at them. Su Qingyuan smiled. "You like osmanthus too?"
The little one babbled back at her.
Lu Shiyan stood beside her, holding their little grandson. Yinuo was very quiet, leaning against his shoulder, and before long he had fallen asleep.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"Who do you think Yinuo takes after?"
"You. Quiet, but with light inside."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "And Yiyan?"
"Xixi. Lively, but focused."
"How do you always see everything?"
"I've been watching for thirty years. If I still couldn't see it, all that watching would have been wasted."
Su Qingyuan leaned against his shoulder, looking at the little granddaughter in her arms. The little one had already fallen asleep, her tiny hand still clutching a single osmanthus flower.
"Lu Shiyan, what do you think they'll do when they grow up?"
"Whatever they do, they'll be wonderful."
"Why do you always say that?"
"Because every time, that's how I feel."
Su Qingyuan smiled. She looked at the osmanthus trees in the courtyard—the old one had grown taller, its crown spread open like an enormous umbrella. The newly planted one had grown up too, standing shoulder to shoulder with the old tree, their branches and leaves interlacing until you couldn't tell one from the other.
In the distance, Lu Xi and James were playing on the lawn with Yinuo and Yiyan. Their laughter carried over, crisp and clear, like wind chimes.
Su Qingyuan closed her eyes, breathing in the fragrance of the osmanthus. She remembered, years ago, that rainy night when she crouched at the side of the road, soaked through. A car had stopped, the window rolling down to reveal his face. He'd said, "Get in." She got in, and from that moment on she never got out again.
That year, she was twenty-four. This year, she was fifty-six. Thirty-two years. In thirty-two years she had made many works, won many awards, traveled to many places. But the work she was proudest of was not "First Light," not "Light Traces," not "Roots"—it was Lu Xi. It was Lu Yinuo and Lu Yiyan. It was this courtyard, this osmanthus tree, and this man who had stood by her side for thirty-two years.
"Lu Shiyan."
"Mm."
"When we're old, what do you think we'll be like?"
"We'll watch the osmanthus together. Drink tea together. Wait together for Xixi to come home."
"And then?"
"And then we'll turn into osmanthus trees together."
Su Qingyuan smiled. "Osmanthus trees have no feet. They can't wander around."
"Then I'll become the osmanthus tree beside you. Wherever you want to go, the wind will carry my words to you."
Su Qingyuan's tears fell. She leaned against his shoulder, looking up at the osmanthus blossoms covering the tree. Sunlight slipped down through the gaps in the leaves, falling on the two of them, on the little granddaughter in her arms, on the golden petals scattered across the ground.
Everything was quiet, everything was beautiful.
From the lawn in the distance, Lu Xi's voice carried over: "Mom! Come take a photo!"
Su Qingyuan wiped her tears. "Coming."
She stood up and took Lu Shiyan's hand. The two of them walked side by side toward that patch of sunlight. Behind them, the osmanthus trees swayed gently in the wind, petals rustling down like golden snow.
Many years later, someone asked Su Qingyuan: "What do you think is the finest work of your life?"
Su Qingyuan thought for a moment, then said: "Light."
"What light?"
She didn't answer. She looked out the window, where the two osmanthus trees in the courtyard were glittering in the sunlight. On the swing beneath them, Yinuo and Yiyan were squabbling over a single flower. Lu Xi came out carrying a plate of sliced fruit, James following behind with two cups of juice in his hands. Lu Shiyan came out from inside the house and draped a coat over her shoulders. "The wind's picking up."
She smiled. Then she turned back and said to the person who had asked: "The finest works are the light that shines into your life. And the light that you become."

========================================

Chapter 47 — Light Without End
https://www.xbanxia.cc/books/417510/72626249.html

The story should have ended here. But Su Qingyuan said it hadn't. She said light doesn't end. It only changes direction and keeps on shining.
The last time I went to Nancheng was in autumn. The osmanthus trees were in full bloom, their fragrance so sweet it was almost cloying. Su Qingyuan and Lu Shiyan still lived in that courtyard. The old osmanthus tree had grown taller, and the ones they'd planted later had long since spread into full, leafy canopies. Beneath the trees, Lu Shiyan had set out a stone table and two rattan chairs, and on the table there was always a pot of tea—sometimes Longjing, sometimes Pu'er. Every morning they drank tea together, then went their separate ways to their own work—she to her studio, he to the company. In the evening they came home together, sat out in the courtyard, and waited for Lu Xi to video-call with the children.
Lu Xi was in London now, already the design director of a renowned jewelry brand. James had opened his own architecture firm and cycled to work and back every day. Yinuo and Yiyan had planted an osmanthus tree in their garden—not in Nancheng, but in a garden on the outskirts of London. Lu Xi said that once the tree grew up, its fragrance would drift across the whole yard, just like the one at Grandma's house.
Su Qingyuan showed me the photo: two little children crouched beside the planting hole, hands covered in mud, eyes crinkled into crescent moons from laughing. She said, "See? Light passes itself down on its own." I said, "Like your work." She thought for a moment. "Will my work get passed down too?" I said, "It will. Because sincere things never go out of style."
Zhou Wanyin had handed her jewelry studio over to the younger generation and moved to the suburbs to focus on painting. She held an exhibition once a year—small, but every time someone made the trip just to see it. She said it didn't matter whether the paintings were good or not; what mattered was that there were still things she wanted to paint. Qu Ya was still taking photographs. She'd shot her "Roots" series for thirty years, from Nancheng to all corners of the world. She said that after thirty years she'd come to realize that roots aren't a place—they're relationships. The connection between you and a person, a tree, a city.
Chen Xiaoman's restaurant had opened a branch in the city center of Melbourne, and business was good. Every year she sent Su Qingyuan a photo, and in it was the osmanthus tree outside the restaurant's door—a sapling she'd brought over from Nancheng, planted ten years ago, and which had finally bloomed. Su Qingyuan pinned that photo to the wall of her studio, alongside all the others. She said, "See? Light finds its own place to take root."
News of Su Yurou grew scarcer and scarcer. Her social media had gone quiet since last spring, the final photo a beach in Melbourne, captioned with just two words: "Clear skies." Su Qingyuan tapped a like. She didn't comment. Some relationships need neither closeness nor severance. They simply exist, like a tree that has stopped growing taller—not flourishing, but still alive.
The day I left, Su Qingyuan walked me to the gate. She broke off a small sprig of osmanthus from the tree, wrapped it in white paper, and handed it to me. "Take it home. Set it on your table—it'll stay fragrant a long while."
I took it and bent my head to breathe in the scent. "Su Qingyuan, do you still paint these days?"
"I do. Every single day."
"Paint what?"
"Light. Same as when I was a child."
"The light from your childhood, and the light now—how are they different?"
She thought about it, then said, "The light when I was a child was what I wanted to become. The light now is what I already am."
She didn't see me all the way to the front gate; she stopped beneath the osmanthus tree. She said she was used to seeing people off here. Whenever Lu Shiyan went out, she walked him to this spot; whenever Lu Xi left home, she walked her here too. This was the place where she watched all her light travel off into the distance.
I turned and walked a few steps, then looked back. She was still standing there, in that old sweater, cradling that cup of unfinished tea in her hands. Osmanthus blossoms fell on her shoulder, and she didn't brush them away. The sunlight fell over her, and she didn't shy from it.
In that moment I suddenly understood the "light" she spoke of. It wasn't the aurora in the distance, nor the sun overhead—it was her, standing right there. Quietly, surely, becoming her own light.
I wrote this story for a long time. By the end, I could no longer tell which parts were real and which I had imagined. But one thing I am certain of—Su Qingyuan is real. Not real as a character in a novel, but real as "a possibility." She tells you: a person can climb up out of the mud, can choose not to forgive yet still let go, can shine by her own strength, can love and be loved. These aren't fairy tales—they're the road she walked.
If you are in the dark right now, I hope this story can give you a little light. Not the kind that's dazzling, blinding, that makes you feel ashamed of yourself, but the kind that's gentle and quiet, the kind that tells you, "You can, too."
Turn off the light—the day will always break. This isn't consolation. It's a fact.
